      ==================================================================
==============

INDIVIDUAL AND GROUP PSYCHOLOGY

1. The opposition between individual psychology and social/group psychology is less 
sharp than it appears - individual psychology is always already social, involving 
relations to others.

2. Group psychology concerns the individual as member of a tribe, nation, caste, 
profession, institution, or crowd.

3. Social instincts are not primary or irreducible - they can be traced back to origins in 
the narrower family circle.

4. The psychology of groups is the oldest human psychology; individual psychology 
emerged only gradually from group psychology.

      ==================================================================
==============

CRITIQUE AND APPROPRIATION OF LE BON

5. Le Bon correctly identified key characteristics of group behavior: diminished 
intellectual capacity, lack of restraint, incapacity for moderation, and emotional 
intensification.

6. Groups display heightened suggestibility, contagion of emotions, and a tendency 
toward extremes.

7. In groups, the heterogeneous is submerged and the unconscious foundations 
common to all come to the fore.

8. The group mind produces lowered intellectual performance, uninhibited affect, and 
incapacity for delay - resembling primitive mental life and childhood.

9. Groups are credulous, influenced by images rather than logic, and think in terms of 
wish-fulfilling associations.

10. Le Bon's description of group characteristics is accurate, but his explanation 
through "suggestion" and "prestige" is circular and inadequate.

      ==================================================================
==============

LIBIDO THEORY APPLIED TO GROUPS11. Libido - the energy of love instincts - is the force that holds groups together.

12. Eros, which holds together everything in the world, is what binds group members to 
each other and to the leader.

13. Groups are held together by a libidinal bond, not merely by self-interest or rational 
advantage.

14. The essence of a group lies in the libidinal ties existing within it.

15. Aim-inhibited love (tenderness, affection) is as much a product of sexual instincts 
as directly sexual love, and is equally capable of forming lasting bonds.

      ==================================================================
==============

THE ROLE OF THE LEADER

16. Groups require a leader; leaderless groups are unstable or must substitute an 
abstraction (idea, shared hatred) for the leader.

17. The leader does not need to love the group members; he may be narcissistic, self-
confident, and independent.

18. The leader occupies the position of the primal father - he is the group ideal that 
governs the ego in place of the ego ideal.

19. The leader is loved by all group members, and this shared love for the same object 
creates the mutual identification among members.

20. The hypnotist and the leader both occupy the place of the ego ideal and exercise 
power through this position.

      ==================================================================
==============

THE TWO ARTIFICIAL GROUPS: CHURCH AND ARMY

21. The Church and the Army are highly organized artificial groups that demonstrate 
the structure of group psychology with clarity.

22. Both groups involve the same libidinal structure: a head (Christ, Commander-in-
Chief) who loves all members equally.

23. The illusion of the leader's equal love for all members is essential to group cohesion.

24. Panic in armies results from the dissolution of libidinal ties - the loss of the leader or 
the sense that he has ceased to care for his followers.25. Religious intolerance and cruelty toward out-groups stem from the same libidinal 
bonds that create in-group cohesion.

26. Groups are intolerant of outsiders because narcissistic self-love is limited only by 
love for others who are libidinally bound to the same object.

      ==================================================================
==============

IDENTIFICATION

27. Identification is the earliest expression of an emotional tie with another person - it is 
pre-oedipal and distinct from object-choice.

28. Identification is ambivalent from the start - it can express tenderness as well as the 
wish to eliminate.

29. There are three sources of identification: (a) the original emotional tie with an object,
 (b) regressive substitution for an abandoned object-choice by introjection, and (c) 
perception of a common quality shared with another person who is not a sexual object.

30. Group members are identified with one another through having placed the same 
object (the leader) in the place of their ego ideal.

31. Identification is partial and highly limited - it borrows a single trait from the object.

32. Hysterical identification involves adopting a symptom from another person with 
whom one shares an unconscious common element (often a repressed wish).

33. Identification based on shared ego ideal is the primary mechanism of group 
formation among members.

      ==================================================================
==============

THE EGO IDEAL

34. The ego ideal is a differentiating grade within the ego - the heir to the original 
narcissism.

35. The ego ideal has the functions of self-observation, moral conscience, censorship 
of dreams, and a major influence in repression.

36. In group formation, the ego ideal is replaced by the group's shared object (leader or 
leading idea).

37. When the ego ideal is externalized and projected onto a leader, the individual 
relinquishes critical judgment and submits to the group.38. The separation between ego and ego ideal is reversible - in states of triumph, 
mania, and group membership, the two may temporarily coincide.

      ==================================================================
==============

BEING IN LOVE AND HYPNOSIS

39. Being in love involves overvaluation of the object - the object is treated as if it were 
one's own ego, and serves as a substitute for one's own unattained ego ideal.

40. In extreme love, the ego impoverishes itself in favor of the object; the object 
consumes the ego.

41. Hypnosis resembles being in love minus direct sexual satisfaction - the hypnotist 
occupies the place of the ego ideal.

42. Hypnosis is a group of two - it shares the essential structure of group psychology, 
with the hypnotist as leader.

43. Suggestion, rather than explaining hypnosis and group psychology, is itself a 
derivative of libido and must itself be explained.

      ==================================================================
==============

THE PRIMAL HORDE

44. The primal horde (Urhorde) described by Darwin constitutes the prehistoric origin of 
group psychology.

45. The primal father was free while all other members of the group had their will 
restricted; individual psychology began with the father.

46. Groups recreate the primal horde situation - the leader is the dreaded primal father, 
and the group wants to be dominated by unlimited force.

47. The uncanny power of hypnosis derives from its connection to the primal father - 
the hypnotist awakens a portion of the archaic heritage.

48. The primal father was the original ego ideal; the father of the primal horde was the 
group ideal that governed individual egos.

49. Totemism, with its taboos, represents the conditions of the overthrown primal father 
imposed on the group.

      ==================================================================
==============NEUROSIS AND GROUP PSYCHOLOGY

50. Neurosis is antisocial - it achieves privately what group formation achieves through 
collective means.

51. The neurotic creates his own private substitutive formations (symptoms) instead of 
participating in shared group formations.

52. The complaint that civilization is responsible for neurosis contains a kernel of truth - 
neurotic substitutes are attempts to achieve what society achieves through collective 
institutions.

      ==================================================================
==============

ADDITIONAL STRUCTURAL POSITIONS

53. There is a gradation from being in love, through hypnosis, to group formation - all 
involve the same libidinal dynamics with variation in the sexual aim and number of 
objects.

54. Groups can substitute a common tendency, wish, or abstraction for a concrete 
leader - even shared hatred can unify.

55. The group increases affect and diminishes intellect because identification with 
fellow members permits regression to early mental states.

56. Herd instinct (Trotter) does not explain group formation; rather, the sibling 
relationship and shared parental attachment explain the later sense of group belonging.

      ==================================================================
==============

TOTAL POSITIONS: 56

      ==================================================================
==============POSITION STATEMENT FREUD CIVILIZATION AND ITS DISCONTENTS

1. There exists a fundamental and irresolvable tension between the individual's desire 
for instinctive freedom and civilization's demand for conformity and repression.

2. Human beings possess certain characteristic instincts that are immutable and cannot 
be eliminated, only redirected or suppressed.

3. Primitive instincts such as the desire to kill and the insatiable craving for sexual 
gratification are inherently harmful to collective wellbeing.

4. Civilization requires the renunciation of instinctual satisfaction as its most essential 
characteristic.

5. The guilty conscience is the psychological price paid by individuals to belong to 
civilized society.

6. Guilt often remains unconscious and manifests as anxiety or generalized discontent.

7. The "oceanic feeling" of limitlessness and connection to the universe is a residue of 
early infantile ego-states rather than evidence of religious truth.

8. Religion functions as a mass delusion that helps humans cope with suffering but 
exacts an enormous psychological cost.

9. The ego is not sharply delimited but extends inward into an unconscious entity called 
the id.

10. In infancy, the ego includes everything; the division between self and external world 
develops through experience.

11. There are three primary sources of human suffering: the frailty of our bodies, the 
superior power of nature, and the inadequacy of social regulations.

12. Of these sources of suffering, social relations appear controllable but prove to have 
obdurate psychological causes that resist reform.

13. Civilization is built upon wish-fulfillments of human ideals including control, beauty, 
hygiene, order, and intellectual achievement.

14. The development of civilization parallels the libidinal development of the individual.

15. Civilization serves to redistribute pleasure more equally among individuals while 
demanding compromises in innate egocentrism.

16. Human beings enter social bonds by sacrificing a portion of their happiness in 
exchange for greater security.

17. Eros and Ananke—love and necessity—are the two parents of civilization.18. The purpose of Eros is to combine individuals, then families, then races, peoples, 
and nations into one great unity of mankind.

19. There exists alongside Eros an independent aggressive instinct—the death drive or 
Thanatos—that shares world-dominion with the life instinct.

20. The inclination to aggression is an original, self-subsisting instinctual disposition in 
human beings.

21. Aggression constitutes the greatest impediment to civilization.

22. The evolution of civilization represents the struggle between Eros and Death, 
between the instinct of life and the instinct of destruction.

23. Civilization obtains mastery over individual aggression by installing the super-ego 
as an internal agency to watch over dangerous desires.

24. The super-ego develops through the internalization of aggressive instincts that 
cannot be successfully directed outward.

25. Instincts can never be dispelled but only diverted or redirected according to the 
economy of the psyche.

26. The more aggression that is diverted inward, the greater the power of the super-
ego becomes.

27. Those least inclined to immoral acts are often most severely punished by their own 
conscience.

28. Guilt is produced both for misdeeds actually committed and for the mere intention 
of committing misdeeds.

29. Human culture is bound up in an ancient Oedipal drama of brothers banding 
together to kill their father and then creating rules to mediate ambivalent desires.

30. The commandment "Love thy neighbor" is psychologically untenable because 
human beings are primarily aggressive rather than loving.

31. Love within families and groups tends to isolate these units and prevent individuals 
from maturing independently.

32. When instinctual trends undergo repression, their libidinal elements become 
neurotic symptoms while aggressive elements form the basis for guilt.

33. There may exist a "cultural super-ego" that functions as a conscience for society as 
a whole.

34. The cultural super-ego, like its individual counterpart, often makes excessive demands that should be lowered.

35. Professional work offers a means of sublimation that can displace libidinal 
  components—narcissistic, aggressive, and erotic—onto productive activity.

36. Freely chosen work that enables sublimation of existing inclinations is a source of 
special satisfaction.

37. Man has become a "prosthetic God" through technological advancement, yet 
increased misery accompanies these achievements.

38. The assertion that we would be happier if we abandoned civilization and returned to 
primitive conditions is ridiculous.

39. Civilization may not bring liberty or happiness, but it does restrain primitive 
impulses and prevent many problems.

40. The "psychological poverty of groups" poses a danger when social bonds are 
constituted primarily by identification among members rather than by genuine 
leadership.

41. Societies themselves, like individuals, can be understood as having psychologically 
determined "characters" reflecting different blends of Eros and Thanatos.

42. Societies, like individuals, may become "neurotic" when overcome by excess 
anxiety regarding base impulses.

43. A real change in relations to possessions would help civilization more than ethical 
commands, but this recognition has been obscured by idealistic misconceptions of 
human nature.

44. The sacrifice of libidinal pleasure and happiness in return for security may not be 
worth the effort, though this cannot be conclusively determined.

45. The final question for humanity is whether civilization can prevail over the human 
instinct of aggression and self-destruction.

46. Religion helps civilizations by taming asocial instincts and creating community 
around shared beliefs.

47. Organized religion makes individuals perpetually subordinate to the primal father 
figure embodied by God.

48. The death instinct originally aims at self-destruction but is partially diverted outward 
as aggression to protect the organism.

49. Sadism and masochism are manifestations of the death drive directed outward and 
inward respectively.50. Repetition of painful situations, self-destructiveness, and death wishes are 
expressions of the internally directed death drive.COCAINE PAPERS FREUD POSITION STATEMENT

POSITION STATEMENTS - FREUD: ÜBER COCA (1884)

1. The coca plant (Erythroxylon coca) thrives best in warm valleys on the eastern 
slopes of the Andes, 5000-6000 feet above sea level, in a rainy climate free from 
extremes of temperature.

2. Coca leaves provide an indispensable stimulant for approximately 10 million people.

3. Indians under the influence of coca can withstand exceptional hardships and perform 
heavy labor without requiring proper nourishment during that time.

4. By using coca, Indians are able to travel on foot for hundreds of hours and run faster 
than horses without showing signs of fatigue.

5. The effect of coca on Europeans is the same as that which coca leaves have on the 
Indians of South America.

6. Immoderate use of coca leads to a cachexia characterized physically by digestive 
complaints and emaciation, and mentally by moral depravity and complete apathy 
toward everything not connected with the stimulant.

7. The moderate use of coca is more likely to promote health than to impair it, and 
coqueros live to a great age.

8. Cocaine is the true agent of the coca effect, which can be produced just as well in 
Europe as in South America.

9. In small doses, coca has a stimulating effect on the nervous system; in larger doses, 
a paralyzing effect.

10. In cold-blooded animals the paralyzing effect of cocaine is particularly noticeable, 
while in warm-blooded animals symptoms of stimulation are most apparent.

11. After doses of cocaine, dogs show obvious signs of happy excitement and a 
maniacal compulsion to move.

12. Cocaine affects all nerve centers, producing stimulation of the brain centers which 
control voluntary movement.

13. A few minutes after taking cocaine, one experiences a sudden exhilaration and 
feeling of lightness.

14. One senses an increase of self-control and feels more vigorous and more capable 
of work under cocaine's influence.

15. The characteristic urge for immediate activity which alcohol produces is completely 
absent with cocaine.16. One is simply normal under cocaine's influence and soon finds it difficult to believe 
one is under the influence of any drug at all.

17. The mood induced by coca is due not so much to direct stimulation as to the 
disappearance of elements in one's general state of well-being which cause depression.

18. The euphoria resulting from good health is nothing more than the normal condition 
of a well-nourished cerebral cortex which is not conscious of the organs of the body to 
which it belongs.

19. Long-lasting, intensive mental or physical work can be performed without fatigue 
under the influence of coca.

20. Under coca's influence, the need for food and sleep which otherwise makes itself 
felt peremptorily at certain times is completely banished.

21. Cocaine dispels extreme fatigue and a well-justified feeling of hunger.

22. While under the influence of coca, one can eat copiously but has the clear feeling 
that the meal was superfluous.

23. During the first hours of the coca effect one cannot sleep, but this sleeplessness is 
in no way distressing.

24. Coca wards off hunger, sleep, and fatigue and steels one to intellectual effort.

25. The euphoria induced by coca is not followed by any feeling of lassitude or other 
state of depression.

26. After moderate doses, a part at least of the coca effect lasts for over twenty-four 
hours.

27. Coca, if used protractedly but in moderation, is not detrimental to the body.

28. A first dose or even repeated doses of coca produce no compulsive desire to use 
the stimulant further; on the contrary, one feels a certain unmotivated aversion to the 
substance.

29. Coca is a far more potent and far less harmful stimulant than alcohol.

30. The instantaneous effect of a dose of coca cannot be compared with that of a 
morphine injection, but there is no danger of general damage to the body as with 
chronic morphine use.

31. Protracted use of coca can lead to a lasting improvement if the inhibitions 
manifested before it is taken are due only to physical causes or to exhaustion.

32. Psychiatrists have ample drugs for reducing excitation of nerve centers but none to increase reduced functioning; coca fills this gap.

33. Coca is effective in treating functional disorders grouped under the name 
neurasthenia.

34. Coca is almost universally effective in improving hysteria, hypochondria, 
melancholic inhibition, stupor, and similar maladies.

35. Coca banishes dyspeptic complaints and the disorders and debility associated 
therewith, and after protracted use results in a permanent cure.

36. The effect of cocaine on the stomach is two-fold: stimulation of movement and 
reduction of the organ's sensitivity.

37. Coca achieves brilliant successes in treatments of gastralgia and enteralgia, and all 
painful and cramping afflictions of the stomach and intestines.

38. The use of coca is definitely indicated in cases of atonic digestive weakness and 
nervous stomach disorders, achieving not merely relief of symptoms but lasting 
improvement.

39. Long-term use of coca is recommended in all diseases involving degeneration of 
tissues, such as severe anemia, phthisis, and long-lasting febrile diseases.

40. In phthisis, coca has a limiting effect on fever and sweating.

41. Coca preparations possess the power to suppress the craving for morphine in 
habitual addicts.

42. Coca reduces to negligible proportions the serious symptoms of collapse which 
appear while a patient is being weaned from morphine.

43. The treatment of morphine addiction with coca does not result merely in the 
exchange of one kind of addiction for another—the use of coca is only temporary.

44. Coca has a directly antagonistic effect on morphine.

45. Coca can suppress the irresistible compulsion to drink in alcoholism, though 
suppression of alcohol craving proves more difficult than suppression of morphomania.

46. The natives of South America did not doubt the stimulative effect of coca on the 
genitalia; coqueros sustain a high degree of potency into old age.

47. Coca can restore potency and cause the disappearance of functional weaknesses.

48. Cocaine and its salts have a marked anesthetizing effect when brought in contact 
with the skin and mucous membrane in concentrated solution.

49. The anesthetizing properties of cocaine should make it suitable for a good many applications as a local anesthetic.

50. Coca spares the usual symptoms of mountain sickness (shortness of breath, 
pounding heart, dizziness) during high-altitude climbing, likely due to early paralysis of 
certain branches of the vagus nerve.POSITION STATEMENTS: FREUD - THE FUTURE OF AN ILLUSION (1927)

1. Civilization exists to help individuals repress their animalistic and violent instincts, 
allowing them to live in harmonious community.
2. The individual is essentially an enemy of society and has instinctual urges that must 
be restrained to help society function.
3. Human nature is anti-social, rebellious, and has high sexual and destructive 
tendencies.
4. Among the fundamental instinctual wishes that must be repressed are those of 
incest, cannibalism, and lust for killing.
5. Masses are lazy and unintelligent; they have no love for instinctual renunciation and 
cannot be convinced by argument of its inevitability.
6. Civilization rests on labor, coercion, and instinctual renunciation that provoke 
resistance.
7. Religion first arose as early humans sought to explain nature's forces by imagining 
they were controlled by human-like gods.
8. The personification of nature allows humans to deal psychically with forces that 
would otherwise remain incomprehensible and terrifying.
9. Religion transformed from polytheism to monotheism, with a single father-like God 
believed to exist to protect his followers.
10. Religious ideas are illusions, consisting of dogmas and assertions about external 
and internal reality that claim credence without independent verification.
11. Religious concepts are transmitted in three ways: because primal ancestors 
believed them, because proofs have been handed down from antiquity, and because 
questioning their authenticity is forbidden.
12. Religious beliefs represent wish-fulfillment—fulfillments of the oldest, strongest, and 
most urgent wishes of mankind.
13. An illusion is a belief in which wish-fulfillment is a prominent factor in its motivation, 
disregarding its relations to reality.
14. The gods serve a threefold task: exorcising the terrors of nature, reconciling men to 
the cruelty of fate (especially death), and compensating them for the sufferings and 
privations of civilized life.
15. Religion is a projection of infantile helplessness and father-longing that humanizes 
nature, promises justice and an afterlife, and asserts authority without proof.
16. The mother, who satisfies the child's hunger, becomes its first love-object and first 
protection against undefined dangers—its first protection against anxiety.
17. In the protective function, the mother is soon replaced by the stronger father, who 
retains that position for the rest of childhood.
18. The libido follows paths of narcissistic needs and attaches itself to objects ensuring 
satisfaction of those needs.
19. When we become adults, we become aware of the fallibility of our fathers yet still 
have need for protection.
20. Humans create the idea of a Divine Father to take the place of the human father.
21. Religious belief is an infantile neurosis in need of a cure so the person can become 
a healthy mature adult.
22. Nature is a threat that civilization inadequately responds to; civilization's task is to 
defend us against nature, which seeks coldly, cruelly, and relentlessly to destroy us.
23. Religion serves as a method to end suffering and doubt, helping people find 
purpose in life.24. Religion helps formulate positive moral codes which allow communal existence of 
humanity.
25. Religious teachings lack evidentiary support, and it is impossible to independently 
verify their claims.
26. Religion exists to fulfill humanity's wishes to not feel insignificant in the scheme of 
the universe.
27. Religion should be understood as a universal obsessional neurosis with totemic 
roots.
28. Religion has been applied by leaders to control forces of nature and extract wealth 
from the masses.
29. Religion has been imposed by a minority using power and coercion to obtain 
possession and control.
30. Cultural prohibitions enforced through religion suppress people and the innate 
nature of human failure.
31. The concept of sin—being unworthy of love unless one behaves in a certain 
manner—is mainly used as a power of coercion.
32. Laws and moral precepts should be justified by social necessity rather than divine 
mandate.
33. Society would benefit from an "education to reality" and gradual reliance on reason 
and science.
34. The scientific spirit engenders particular attitudes toward worldly matters; before 
religious matters it pauses, hesitates, and finally crosses the threshold.
35. Science can eventually provide explanations that go beyond the limitations of 
religion.
36. Reason should replace faith in God as the basis for civilization.
37. Better alternatives to religion must exist for achieving social order and individual 
emotional fulfillment.
38. Religion can be removed from civilization without risk or harm to people.
39. If being devoutly religious were truly a sign of great intellect, there should be no 
harm caused by anti-religious arguments.
40. Religion has not done enough for humanity despite its contributions; it has failed to 
make people happier or more moral.
41. Human society can only advance by abandoning the collective neurosis that 
plagues societal development.
42. Giving up expectation of another world will motivate people to work harder to make 
this world better and happier.
43. Religion sanctions violations of fundamental principles of society through concepts 
like confession and absolution.
44. The philosophy of "as if"—behaving as if religious doctrines were true for practical 
reasons—is not far removed from "credo quia absurdum."
45. No personal revelation or state of ecstasy, however deeply moving to one individual,
 can establish the truth of religious doctrines for others.
46. Scientific illusions differ from religious illusions in that they can be altered, updated, 
or scrapped without penalty.
47. The transition from religious to scientific worldview will be slow and contested.
48. Psychoanalysis reveals the same motives for religious formation as the infantile 
contribution to manifest motives.
49. Religion keeps people in an infantile psychological state rather than promoting 
mature development.50. The conflict between religion and science is irreconcilable; they are mortal enemies.POSITION STATEMENT FREUD MOSES AND MONOTHEISM

1. Moses was not a Hebrew but an Egyptian, likely a nobleman or priest of high rank.

2. The name "Moses" is of Egyptian origin, derived from names like Thut-mose and Ra-
mose, meaning "child."

3. Monotheism is not of Jewish origin but an Egyptian discovery, originating with 
Pharaoh Akhenaten (Amenhotep IV).

4. Akhenaten established monotheism as his state religion in the form of exclusive 
worship of the sun-power, or Aten.

5. The Aten religion was characterized by exclusive belief in one God, rejection of 
anthropomorphism, rejection of magic and sorcery, and absolute denial of an afterlife.

6. Moses was a devoted believer in and follower of Akhenaten's Aten religion.

7. After Akhenaten's death and the collapse of Aten worship, Moses fled Egypt to 
preserve monotheism through a new people.

8. Moses chose the Israelite slaves as a people upon whom he could impose his 
monotheistic religious beliefs.

9. Moses imposed the Egyptian custom of circumcision upon the Israelites as part of 
their new religious identity.

10. The fact that Jews practice circumcision—an Egyptian custom—provides evidence 
that Moses was Egyptian and brought Egyptian religion to them.

11. Because of his tyrannical and domineering character and his desire to impose strict 
monotheism, Moses was murdered by his followers in a revolt.

12. The biblical account of Moses follows the structure of "the family romance"—a 
mythic pattern identified by Otto Rank in hero narratives.

13. The standard hero myth involves noble birth, exposure, and rescue by humble 
parents; Moses's story inverts this, suggesting the historical reality was itself inverted.

14. After Moses's murder, the Israelites repressed the memory of the deed and 
abandoned much of his strict religion.

15. The Israelites later joined with a Midianite tribe who worshipped a volcano god 
named Yahweh.

16. The monotheistic solar god Aten of the Egyptian Moses was eventually fused with 
Yahweh, the Midianite volcano god.

17. The deeds of the Egyptian Moses were ascribed to a Midianite priest also called Moses, creating a composite figure.

18. There is an analogy between collective phenomena in religion and the process of 
repression in individual development: trauma—defense—latency—neurotic 
illness—partial return of the repressed.

19. After a significant period of latency (centuries), there was a "return of the repressed"
 in which the strict ethical monotheism of Moses reasserted itself.

20. The return of the repressed shaped the distinctive character of the Jewish people 
and brought Judaism to its spiritual and ethical heights.

21. Repressed collective guilt stemming from the murder of Moses was passed down 
through generations.

22. This inherited guilt led Jews to neurotic expressions of legalistically religious 
sentiment to cope with their inheritance of trauma.

23. The Jewish prophets kept alive the sense of guilt, leading to the imposition of ever 
more instinctual renunciations.

24. These renunciations resulted in "ethical heights which had remained inaccessible to 
the other peoples of antiquity."

25. The concept of the Messiah emerged as a hope for the return of Moses—a desire 
for reconnection with the murdered father figure.

26. Jewish identity and character traits are transmitted phylogenetically and do not 
require religion to be sustained.

27. The concept of "the chosen people" catalyzes a collective narcissistic neurosis that 
contributes to anti-Semitism.

28. The hatred Jews have attracted is the price paid for the denial of parricide and the 
refusal to acknowledge it.

29. Christianity emerged as a way for humanity to deal with the sense of guilt arising 
from the murder of Moses (and the primal father).

30. The crucifixion of Jesus was a way to acknowledge and atone for original 
sin—which was actually the murder of the primal father.

31. Original sin, in its deepest meaning, refers to parricide—the killing of the primal 
father.

32. The Holy Communion represents the totem feast described in Totem and 
Taboo—the ritual consumption of the murdered father.

33. In Christianity, guilt (original sin) and parricide are recognized and assumed; in Judaism, parricide is not recognized but reappears in the feeling of guilt.

34. Christianity represents a "son religion" that replaced the "father religion" of Judaism.

35. Christianity acknowledged the killing of God (in Christ), while Judaism continues to 
deny the murder of Moses.

36. The Jewish religion exhibits "the progress of spirituality" (Fortschritt in der 
Geistigkeit)—an advance in intellectuality.

37. This advance in intellectuality is connected to the ban on idolatry and the 
dematerialization of God in monotheism.

38. The prohibition against making images of God represents a triumph of abstract 
thought over sensory perception.

39. The recognition of paternity over maternity represents a similar triumph of 
intellectuality—the father cannot be perceived directly but must be inferred.

40. One man, Moses, created the Jews; to him they owe their tenacity in life and much 
of the hostility they have encountered.

41. The distinctive character and survival of the Jewish people can be explained by the 
legacy of strict Mosaic monotheism.

42. Religions owe their compulsive power to the return of the repressed; they are 
reawakened memories of ancient traumatic events.

43. The structure of individual neurosis applies to the development of religious and 
cultural phenomena.

44. The sense of guilt that results from repression and renunciation of instincts is the 
foundation of civilization's development.

45. Moses and Monotheism represents a psychoanalytic history of the Jews, Judaism, 
and the Jewish psyche.

46. The work applies the interpretive methods used in individual psychoanalysis to 
reconstruct collective historical events.

47. Anti-Semitism is partly explained by the envy aroused by the Jewish claim to be the 
"chosen people."

48. Anti-Semitism is also connected to the fact that Jews have lived as minorities 
among other peoples.

FRAGMENT OF AN ANALYSIS OF A CASE OF HYSTERIA (DORA, 1905)
1. Hysterical symptoms are the expression of repressed sexual wishes and fantasies.
2. Dreams provide the most direct access to unconscious mental content.
3. Transference is the displacement of feelings originally directed toward earlier figures 
onto the person of the analyst.
4. Somatic symptoms have symbolic meaning corresponding to unconscious fantasies.
5. Resistance to analytic interpretation indicates proximity to repressed material.
6. Hysteria involves the conversion of psychical excitation into somatic symptoms.
7. Multiple determinants converge in the production of a single hysterical symptom.

ANALYSIS OF A PHOBIA IN A FIVE-YEAR-OLD BOY (LITTLE HANS, 1909)
8. The Oedipus complex is a universal phenomenon in childhood psychosexual 
development.
9. Phobias result from the displacement of anxiety from an internal source onto an 
external object.
10. Castration anxiety is central to male psychosexual development.
11. Children spontaneously develop sexual theories to explain procreation and 
anatomical differences.
12. Animal phobias in childhood represent displaced fear of the father.
13. Infantile sexuality exists and follows developmental stages.
14. The child's sexual researches are driven by the threat posed by a new sibling.

PSYCHO-ANALYTIC NOTES ON AN AUTOBIOGRAPHICAL ACCOUNT OF A CASE 
OF PARANOIA (SCHREBER, 1911)
15. Paranoia results from the defense against unconscious homosexual wishes.
16. Projection transforms unacceptable internal perceptions into perceptions of external 
origin.
17. Delusions are attempts at recovery and reconstruction of the world.
18. Megalomania represents a regression to primary narcissism.
19. The paranoiac's relation to the world is withdrawn and then reconstructed through 
delusion.
20. The transformation of homosexual love follows the formula: "I do not love him—I 
hate him—he hates me."
21. Persecutory delusions develop when projected hatred returns as external 
persecution.

FROM THE HISTORY OF AN INFANTILE NEUROSIS (WOLF MAN, 1918)
22. Infantile neurosis provides the template and nucleus for adult neurosis.
23. The primal scene—real or fantasied observation of parental intercourse—has 
traumatic significance.
24. Deferred action (Nachträglichkeit) operates such that early experiences acquire 
pathogenic meaning retroactively.
25. Obsessional neurosis is characterized by profound ambivalence toward the father.
26. Passive sexual aims in relation to the father underlie obsessional symptoms.
27. Phylogenetic inheritance may supply primal fantasies when individual experience is 
lacking.28. The wolf dream condenses multiple layers of infantile sexual experience.

ON NARCISSISM: AN INTRODUCTION (1914)
29. Primary narcissism is a normal developmental stage preceding object-love.
30. Libido can be directed toward the ego (ego-libido) or toward external objects (object-
libido).
31. The ego-ideal is formed through the internalization of narcissistic perfection.
32. Being in love involves an impoverishment of ego-libido transferred to the object.
33. Narcissistic object-choice is based on what one is, was, wishes to be, or once was 
part of oneself.
34. The distinction between ego-libido and object-libido is fundamental to libido theory.
35. Megalomania in psychosis represents the return of libido to the ego.

INSTINCTS AND THEIR VICISSITUDES (1915)
36. Instincts (Triebe) operate at the frontier between the mental and the somatic.
37. An instinct has four characteristics: source, aim, object, and impetus.
38. The four vicissitudes of instincts are: reversal into the opposite, turning around 
upon the subject, repression, and sublimation.
39. Love and hate are not simple opposites deriving from a single instinct.
40. The pleasure-unpleasure polarity is the fundamental regulator of mental life.
41. The transformation from activity to passivity and from sadism to masochism are 
instinctual reversals.
42. The object of an instinct is its most variable feature.

REPRESSION (1915)
43. Repression is a defense mechanism distinct from and preliminary to conscious 
condemnation.
44. Primal repression establishes an unconscious nucleus to which later repressions 
attach.
45. Repression proper (after-pressure) acts on derivatives of the primally repressed.
46. The repressed exerts continuous pressure toward consciousness requiring 
constant counter-cathexis.
47. Repression acts individually upon each ideational representative.
48. The return of the repressed occurs through symptom formation and other substitute 
formations.
49. Repression fails when substitute formations become too similar to the repressed 
content.

THE UNCONSCIOUS (1915)
50. The unconscious constitutes a distinct psychical system governed by its own laws.
51. Primary process—displacement and condensation—characterizes unconscious 
mental functioning.
52. The unconscious knows no negation, no doubt, and no degrees of certainty.
53. Unconscious processes are timeless and not ordered temporally.
54. Thing-presentations alone characterize the unconscious; word-presentations 
belong to the preconscious.
55. The censorship between unconscious and preconscious determines what reaches 
consciousness.
56. Unconscious ideas can only become conscious by linking with corresponding word-presentations.

MOURNING AND MELANCHOLIA (1917)
57. Mourning is the normal reaction to the loss of a loved object.
58. Melancholia involves identification with the lost object through narcissistic 
regression.
59. In melancholia, the shadow of the object falls upon the ego.
60. Self-reproaches in melancholia are actually reproaches directed at the introjected 
lost object.
61. Ambivalence toward the lost object is constitutive of the melancholic disposition.
62. The ego splits in melancholia: one part (the ego-ideal) attacks another (the ego 
identified with the object).
63. Mania represents the triumph of the ego over the lost object following its 
metabolization.
64. Object-loss may be ideal or unconscious even when the object has not actually 
died.POSITION STATEMENTS: FREUD - FRAGMENT OF AN ANALYSIS OF A CASE OF

**POSITION STATEMENTS - FREUD: PROJECT FOR A SCIENTIFIC PSYCHOLOGY 
(1895)**

1. The aim of psychology as a natural science is to represent psychical processes as 
quantitatively determined states of specifiable material particles (neurons).

2. What distinguishes mental activity from rest is to be regarded as a quantity (Q) 
subject to the general laws of motion.

3. The material particles in question that carry psychical quantity are the neurons.

4. The principle of neuronic inertia asserts that neurons tend to divest themselves of 
quantity—the nervous system seeks to reduce tension to zero.

5. Reflex movement is an established method for neurons to discharge quantity and 
keep themselves free from stimulus.

6. The neuronic system receives endogenous stimuli from the body itself which give 
rise to the major needs: hunger, respiration, and sexuality.

7. The organism cannot withdraw from endogenous stimuli as it does from external 
stimuli; they only cease when specific conditions are realized in the external world.

8. The neuronic system must abandon its original trend toward inertia and learn to 
tolerate a store of quantity sufficient to meet the demands for specific action (the 
exigencies of life).

9. There are two classes of neurons: permeable neurons (φ) which serve perception 
and retain nothing, and impermeable neurons (ψ) which offer resistance, retain quantity,
 and are the vehicles of memory and psychical processes.

10. Memory is represented by the facilitations (Bahnung) existing between the ψ-
neurons—the differences in the degree to which contact-barriers have been made 
more capable of conduction.

11. Facilitation depends on the quantity that passes through a neuron in the excitatory 
process and on the number of repetitions of that process.

12. One chief characteristic of nervous tissue is memory: a susceptibility to permanent 
alteration by a single process, contrasted with the behavior of material that returns to its 
former condition.

13. Pain consists in the irruption of large quantities into ψ—it sets both the φ-system and the ψ-system in motion and is the most imperative of all processes.

14. Pain leaves behind permanent facilitations in ψ—like a stroke of lightning—that 
may do away entirely with the resistance of contact-barriers.

15. The perceptual neurons (ω) are excited along with the others during perception but 
not during reproduction; their states of excitation give rise to conscious 
sensations—qualities.

16. The sense organs operate not only as screens against quantity but as sieves, only 
letting through stimuli with particular periods.

17. Unpleasure coincides with a rise in the level of quantity in ψ; pleasure is the 
sensation of discharge.

18. A few minutes after taking in a stimulus, one experiences exhilaration; the psychic 
effect consists of euphoria which does not differ from the normal euphoria of a healthy 
person.

19. The experience of satisfaction occurs when an extraneous helper carries out 
specific action in the external world on behalf of the helpless subject.

20. The original helplessness of human beings is the primal source of all moral motives.

21. The experience of satisfaction leads to facilitation between the memory-image of 
the object and the nuclear neurons cathected during the state of urgency.

22. When the state of wishing reappears, the cathexis will pass to the memories and 
activate them; the memory-image of the object will be the first to experience wishful 
activation.

23. Wishful activation in the first instance produces something similar to a 
perception—namely, a hallucination.

24. The experience of pain produces in ψ a large rise in the level of quantity felt as 
unpleasure, an inclination to discharge, and a facilitation between this inclination and 
the memory-image of the pain-giving object.

25. A wishful state produces positive attraction to the object of the wish; an experience 
of pain results in repulsion—a disinclination to keep the hostile memory-image 
cathected. These are primary wishful attraction and primary defense.

26. The ego is defined as the totality of ψ-cathexes at any given time; in these a 
permanent portion may be distinguished from a changing one.

27. A lateral cathexis from a neighboring neuron acts as an inhibition on the passage of 
quantity—the ego can inhibit psychical processes.

28. With help of a mechanism that draws the ego's attention to an imminent fresh cathexis of a hostile memory-image, the ego can inhibit the passage of quantity from 
the memory to the release of unpleasure.

29. Wishful cathexis carried to the point of hallucination and complete generation of 
unpleasure involving complete expenditure of defense are psychical primary processes.

30. Processes only made possible by a good cathexis of the ego, which represent a 
moderation of primary processes, are psychical secondary processes.

31. The secondary process requires correct exploitation of indications of reality, which 
is only possible when there is inhibition on the part of the ego.

32. It is the inhibition brought about by the ego that makes possible a criterion for 
distinguishing between a perception and a memory.

33. Judgement is a ψ-process only made possible by inhibition exercised by the ego, 
brought about by the difference between wishful cathexis of a memory and a similar 
perceptual cathexis.

34. Language describes the constant portion of the perceptual complex as a "thing" 
and the inconstant portion as its activity or attribute—its "predicate."

35. Judging is not a primary process; it presupposes a cathexis from the ego of the 
disparate portion of the complex.

36. It is on his fellow-creatures that a human being first learns to cognize—the fellow 
human-being was the subject's first satisfying object, first hostile object, and sole 
assisting force.

37. What we term "things" are residues that have evaded judgement.

38. The thought-process consists of the cathexis of ψ-neurons accompanied by a 
change in the previously operative facilitations brought about by lateral cathexis from 
the ego.

39. The secondary process is a repetition of the original course of excitation in ψ, but at 
a lower level and with smaller quantities.

40. The essential precondition of sleep is a lowering of the endogenous charge in the ψ-
nucleus, which renders the secondary function unnecessary.

41. In sleep the subject is in the ideal state of inertia, with the store of quantity 
discharged.

42. The will is the discharge of the total ψ-quantity.

43. Dreams are devoid of motor discharge; we are paralyzed in dreams because the 
neurons are uncathected and motor excitation cannot pass over the barriers.44. In dreams the compulsion to associate prevails—two cathexes simultaneously 
present must be brought into connection with each other.

45. The purpose and meaning of normal dreams can be established with certainty: 
dreams are the fulfilments of wishes—primary processes following on experiences of 
satisfaction.

46. Consciousness is not restricted to the ego but can be attached to any ψ-
process—this is a warning against identification of primary processes with unconscious 
ones.

47. The psychical course of excitation in dreams takes place in accordance with 
quantity, but it is not quantity that decides what shall become conscious.

48. Consciousness emerges during the passage of a quantity; it is not aroused by a 
constant cathexis.

49. An intense current of quantity is not favorable to the emergence of consciousness; 
consciousness is attached to the outcome of the current—to a comparatively quiescent 
persistence of cathexis.

50. The meaning of dreams as wish-fulfilments is concealed by a number of ψ-
processes which are characteristic of the pathological nature of neuroses.FREUD POSITION STATEMENTS: SPECIFIC MECHANISMS

==============
FETISHISM: THE PRECISE MECHANISM
      ==================================================================
==============

* The fetish is a substitute for the mother's penis—the penis the little boy once believed 
in and does not want to give up.
* The boy's discovery that the mother lacks a penis is experienced as a catastrophic 
threat to his own organ.
* Fetishism employs disavowal (Verleugnung), not repression: the boy simultaneously 
knows the mother has no penis and refuses to accept this knowledge.
* The fetish object is typically the last thing seen before the traumatic sight of the 
female genitals—the shoe, the undergarment, the fur.
* The fetish is both a memorial to castration and a token of triumph over the threat of 
castration.
* The fetish allows the boy to retain belief in the maternal phallus while also 
acknowledging (in another part of the mind) its absence.
* Fetishism involves a splitting of the ego: two incompatible attitudes persist side by 
side without influencing each other.
* The shine on the nose (Glanz auf der Nase) case demonstrates how a fetish can 
derive from a forgotten phrase preserved through linguistic transformation.
* The choice of fetish is not arbitrary but determined by the last impression received 
before the traumatic discovery.
* Fur and velvet fix the sight of pubic hair, which should have been followed by the 
longed-for sight of the female member.
* The foot or shoe owes its preference as a fetish to the circumstance that the boy 
peered at the woman's genitals from below, from her legs up.
* Pieces of underclothing crystallize the moment of undressing, the last moment at 
which the woman could still be regarded as phallic.
* The fetishist can tolerate the anatomical reality in some dissociated part of his mind 
while his sexual life depends on denying it.
* The fetish saves the fetishist from becoming homosexual by endowing women with 
the characteristic that makes them tolerable as sexual objects.
* Without the fetish, the fetishist could not achieve sexual satisfaction with women 
because their lack of a penis makes them unacceptable.
* The horror of castration sets up a memorial to itself by creating the fetish-substitute.
* Disavowal is always supplemented by an acknowledgment; two contrary attitudes 
always persist.
* In later life, the fetishist enjoys an advantage: the fetish is easily accessible, 
obtainable, and the sexual satisfaction connected with it comfortable.
* The fetish remains a token of triumph over the threat of castration and a protection 
against it.
* Cutting off the hair and the act of blinding are symbolic substitutes for castration, and 
the fear of them derives from castration anxiety.      ==================================================================
==============
DISAVOWAL VERSUS REPRESSION
      ==================================================================
==============

* Disavowal (Verleugnung) concerns external reality—specifically, the perception of the 
mother's lack of penis.
* Repression (Verdrängung) concerns internal instinctual demands and their ideational 
representatives.
* In disavowal, the perception is registered but its significance is refused; in repression, 
the ideational content is pushed out of consciousness.
* Disavowal results in the splitting of the ego; repression results in the return of the 
repressed through symptoms.
* Neurosis is the result of repression; fetishism and psychosis involve disavowal.
* The psychotic disavows reality and replaces it with delusion; the fetishist disavows a 
specific perception while otherwise maintaining reality-testing.
* Disavowal permits two contradictory beliefs to coexist without mutual influence.
* The splitting produced by disavowal is not the same as neurotic dissociation; it is a 
permanent structural division in the ego.
* The ego's defensive achievement in disavowal comes at the price of a rift in itself that 
will never heal.
* Repression defends against instinct; disavowal defends against perception.

      ==================================================================
==============
CASTRATION COMPLEX: DETAILED MECHANISM
      ==================================================================
==============

* The castration complex is the nucleus of all neuroses.
* The boy's castration anxiety is activated by the sight of female genitals, which confirm 
the threat he may have previously dismissed.
* Threats of castration, often delivered in response to masturbation, become 
retroactively significant after the sight of female genitals.
* The castration complex in boys takes the form of castration anxiety; in girls, it takes 
the form of penis envy.
* The boy enters the Oedipus complex through the castration complex; he renounces 
the mother to preserve his penis.
* The girl enters the Oedipus complex through the castration complex; she turns to the 
father in hope of obtaining the missing penis.
* Castration anxiety is not fear of a realistic danger but fear tied to the loss of an 
erotogenic zone.
* The boy phantasizes that the girl once had a penis and lost it through castration—a 
theory that preserves the universality of the penis.
* The sight of the female genitals may produce horror, contempt, or triumph, but always 
relates to the castration complex.
* The phallic phase is the period during which the child knows only one genital organ—the male one—and the castration complex develops.
* The mother is imagined as phallic before the traumatic discovery; afterward, the boy 
must either disavow, accept, or compromise.
* The father becomes the agent of castration even when mothers or nurses made the 
original threats.
* Fear of being eaten by the father is a regressive transformation of castration anxiety.
* Blinding, tooth extraction, decapitation, and hair-cutting in myths and dreams are 
symbolic equivalents of castration.
* The castration complex is phylogenetically inherited; threats merely activate a 
predisposition.

      ==================================================================
==============
PERVERSIONS: SPECIFIC MECHANISMS
      ==================================================================
==============

* Perversions are the negative of neuroses: what is repressed in neurosis is enacted in 
perversion.
* The pervert does directly what the neurotic accomplishes through symptom-formation.
* Perversions represent fixations at or regressions to component instincts that precede 
genital primacy.
* Sadism is the aggressive component of the sexual instinct become independent and 
exaggerated.
* Masochism is sadism turned against the self through reversal and turning round.
* Primary masochism is the death instinct remaining within the organism, bound with 
libido; secondary masochism is externalized aggression re-internalized.
* Voyeurism (scopophilia) is an active perversion; exhibitionism is its passive 
counterpart.
* Voyeurism is the eroticization of looking; its sublimation becomes intellectual curiosity.
* Exhibitionism always contains the unconscious wish to see the other's genitals in 
return.
* The three forms of masochism are erotogenic, feminine, and moral masochism.
* Moral masochism represents unconscious guilt seeking punishment; the superego 
becomes the sadistic agent.
* The polymorphous perversity of the child demonstrates that perversions are not 
degenerations but developmental arrests or regressions.
* Perverse sexuality becomes pathological when it excludes genital primacy, becomes 
fixed, and serves as the exclusive means of satisfaction.
* The component instincts—oral, anal, scopophilic, sadistic—normally become 
subordinated to genital primacy; in perversion, they dominate.
* Homosexuality is not a single condition but comprises multiple etiologies, including 
narcissistic object choice, fixation on the mother, and identification with her.
* In one common homosexual etiology, the boy identifies with the mother and takes 
himself as a love object, seeking young men to love as his mother loved him.
* The homosexual has not abandoned the mother; he identifies with her and seeks 
objects that represent his childhood self.
* Fetishism is the model perversion because it most clearly illustrates both the 
defensive function and the substitute-formation.      ==================================================================
==============
THE SPLITTING OF THE EGO
      ==================================================================
==============

* The ego, faced with an incompatible demand from reality and instinct, can split itself 
rather than choose.
* One part of the ego acknowledges reality; another part disavows it—both persist 
without synthesis.
* The split in the ego does not heal; it increases over time.
* The formation of a fetish is achieved through a process that recalls the halting of 
memory in traumatic amnesia.
* The boy's interest comes to a halt midway; the last impression before the uncanny 
traumatic one is retained as a fetish.
* The splitting of the ego is the price paid for the otherwise successful defense.
* Both the disavowal and the acknowledgment enter into the formation of the fetish.
* The split ego is not two full egos but one ego with a fault line running through it.
* The ego may employ splitting as a regular defensive process beyond fetishism—in 
psychosis, in certain character formations.
* The synthetic function of the ego fails in the face of certain developmental challenges; 
splitting is the result.

      ==================================================================
==============
ANXIETY: PRECISE FORMULATIONS
      ==================================================================
==============

* Anxiety is the ego's response to danger; it signals the presence of a threatening 
situation.
* Realistic anxiety is fear of an external danger; neurotic anxiety is fear of an internal 
instinctual demand.
* The prototype of all anxiety is the birth trauma—the infant's first experience of 
overwhelming stimulation.
* Castration anxiety is the specific form of anxiety that dominates the phallic phase.
* Fear of loss of love is the girl's equivalent of the boy's castration anxiety.
* The sequence of danger situations: birth, loss of the object, loss of the object's love, 
castration, superego condemnation.
* Anxiety is transformed libido; when libido cannot be discharged, it becomes anxiety.
* Later formulation: anxiety is not transformed libido but a signal produced by the ego to 
mobilize repression.
* Signal anxiety is a small dose of unpleasure used by the ego to prevent a larger 
unpleasure.
* Phobias represent anxiety displaced from an internal danger (the instinct) to an 
external one (the phobic object).
* Little Hans's horse phobia displaced castration anxiety (from the father) onto an 
external avoidable object.* The symptom is formed to avoid the anxiety-situation; the anxiety signals the danger.
* Free-floating anxiety indicates that repression has failed; the ego is overwhelmed by 
undischarged instinctual tension.
* The repetition of anxiety dreams contradicts the wish-fulfillment theory but serves the 
compulsion to repeat and the need to master trauma.

      ==================================================================
==============
DEFENSE MECHANISMS: SPECIFIC OPERATIONS
      ==================================================================
==============

* Repression proper excludes ideational representatives of instinct from consciousness.
* Primal repression is the initial exclusion of instinctual representatives that have never 
been conscious.
* After-pressure (Nachdrängung) is the ongoing effort required to maintain repression.
* Repression operates on the ideational representative, not on the affect; the affect may 
be transformed, displaced, or converted.
* Reaction formation transforms an impulse into its opposite: sadism into excessive 
gentleness, anal eroticism into orderliness.
* Isolation separates an idea from its affect; the obsessional remembers the traumatic 
event but feels nothing.
* Undoing attempts to annul an action or thought by performing a contrary 
action—magical thinking in motor form.
* Regression retreats from a later to an earlier libidinal position when the later position 
encounters obstacles.
* Projection attributes to another person impulses or qualities that the subject cannot 
accept in himself.
* Introjection incorporates external objects or their qualities into the ego.
* Identification with the aggressor adopts the attributes of the threatening figure to 
master anxiety.
* Turning against the self redirects aggression from an external object to oneself—the 
mechanism of depression.
* Reversal into the opposite transforms sadism into masochism, voyeurism into 
exhibitionism.
* Sublimation deflects instinctual energy toward non-sexual, socially valued aims 
without repression.
* Denial (Verneinung) allows repressed content to become conscious in negated form: "
It is not my mother."
* Rationalization provides pseudo-logical explanations for behavior whose true motives 
remain unconscious.

      ==================================================================
==============
SYMPTOM FORMATION: DETAILED MECHANISMS
      ==================================================================
==============

* The symptom is a compromise between the repressed wish and the repressing force.* Every symptom simultaneously expresses the forbidden impulse and the defense 
against it.
* Symptoms are overdetermined—they serve multiple unconscious purposes and 
derive from multiple sources.
* Hysterical conversion transforms psychical conflict into bodily symptoms; the 
symptom symbolizes the repressed.
* The hysterical symptom often represents both sides of a sexual act—the patient plays 
both roles.
* Anna O.'s paralyzed arm symbolized both the forbidden wish and the punishment for 
it.
* Obsessional symptoms employ displacement: the idea is conscious but the affect is 
displaced to a trivial substitute.
* The obsessional's ceremonial is a private religion; the symptom represents both 
temptation and expiation.
* Phobic symptoms bind anxiety to a specific external object that can be avoided.
* The choice of neurosis—hysteria, obsessional neurosis, paranoia—depends on 
fixation points and regression depths.
* Hysteria involves regression to the genital and phallic phases; obsessional neurosis 
to the anal-sadistic phase.
* The return of the repressed is the eruption of unconscious content through the barrier 
of repression in distorted form.
* Symptoms are memory-symbols; they commemorate traumatic experiences or 
forbidden wishes.
* The gain from illness (primary and secondary) makes symptoms resistant to cure.
* Primary gain is the intrapsychic advantage; secondary gain is the external advantage 
(attention, avoidance of responsibility).

      ==================================================================
==============
TRANSFERENCE: PRECISE FORMULATIONS
      ==================================================================
==============

* Transference is the displacement of feelings and attitudes from past 
figures—especially parents—onto the analyst.
* Transference is not an artifact of analysis; it is a universal phenomenon that analysis 
merely makes visible.
* Positive transference includes affectionate and erotic feelings; negative transference 
includes hostile feelings.
* The transference neurosis replaces the original neurosis; the patient's conflicts are 
now lived out in relation to the analyst.
* Transference is both the greatest obstacle and the most powerful therapeutic 
instrument.
* Resistance often manifests through transference—the patient acts out rather than 
remembers.
* The transference must be resolved, not gratified; gratification perpetuates rather than 
cures.
* The analyst becomes a screen upon which the patient projects figures from his 
internal world.* Counter-transference is the analyst's unconscious reaction to the patient's 
transference.
* Unanalyzed counter-transference distorts the analyst's perception and response; 
ongoing self-analysis is required.
* Transference love is genuine love but is provoked by the analytic situation and serves 
resistance.
* The patient who falls in love with the analyst is repeating an infantile pattern rather 
than generating new feeling.
* Negative therapeutic reaction is improvement followed by worsening, driven by 
unconscious guilt and masochism.
* Working through is the repeated confrontation with resistances that allows insight to 
produce lasting change.

      ==================================================================
==============
DREAMS: SPECIFIC MECHANISMS
      ==================================================================
==============

* The dream is the guardian of sleep; it permits the discharge of wishes that would 
otherwise wake the sleeper.
* The manifest content is the dream as remembered; the latent content is the 
unconscious thoughts behind it.
* The dream-work transforms latent into manifest through condensation, displacement, 
considerations of representability, and secondary revision.
* Condensation combines multiple latent elements into a single manifest element; every 
dream element is overdetermined.
* Displacement shifts psychical intensity from significant to trivial elements, disguising 
the true wish.
* Considerations of representability translate abstract thoughts into concrete visual 
images.
* Secondary revision imposes narrative coherence on the irrational products of the 
other mechanisms.
* The day's residue provides the material onto which unconscious infantile wishes 
attach.
* Every dream is a wish-fulfillment, though punishment dreams fulfill the wish of the 
superego.
* Dreams of the death of loved ones fulfill infantile death wishes that have been 
repressed.
* The navel of the dream is the point at which interpretation must stop—the connection 
to the unknown.
* Typical dreams (falling, flying, nakedness, examination) derive from universal human 
experiences and conflicts.
* The dream symbol is a constant relationship between a manifest element and its 
latent meaning.
* Symbols represent primarily the body, parents, birth, death, and sexuality.
* All elongated objects represent the male organ; all hollow objects represent the 
female.
* The dream employs archaic modes of thought that persist in the unconscious.* Dreams of infantile scenes may be constructions rather than memories—screen 
memories in dream form.

      ==================================================================
==============
THE OEDIPUS COMPLEX: COMPLETE FORMULATION
      ==================================================================
==============

* The Oedipus complex is the nucleus of every neurosis.
* The boy desires exclusive possession of the mother and wishes to eliminate the rival 
father.
* The complex is resolved through castration anxiety: the boy renounces the mother to 
save his penis.
* The dissolution of the Oedipus complex creates the superego through identification 
with the father.
* The girl's Oedipus complex begins with the discovery that she lacks a penis and 
blames the mother.
* The girl turns to the father hoping to receive from him the penis the mother failed to 
provide.
* The wish for a penis is transformed into the wish for a child, especially a male child.
* The girl's Oedipus complex is not destroyed by castration anxiety but lingers, slowly 
fading or remaining partially unresolved.
* The negative Oedipus complex involves desire for the same-sex parent and rivalry 
with the opposite-sex parent.
* The complete Oedipus complex is fourfold: positive and negative forms for both 
parents.
* Pre-Oedipal attachment to the mother is especially intense and formative in girls.
* The mother is the first seducer; her care of the infant's body produces erotic 
sensations.
* The Oedipus complex is not merely discovered but constructed through the 
intersection of desire and prohibition.
* Phylogenetic inheritance transmits the Oedipus complex; it recapitulates the primal 
crime of father-murder.
* The totemic meal commemorates the murder and incorporation of the primal father by 
the brother clan.
* Totem and Taboo traces the origin of religion, morality, and social organization to the 
Oedipal situation.

      ==================================================================
==============
THE DEATH INSTINCT AND BEYOND THE PLEASURE PRINCIPLE
      ==================================================================
==============

* Beyond the pleasure principle lies the compulsion to repeat, which serves the death 
instinct.
* The death instinct (Thanatos) seeks to return living matter to an inorganic state.
* The life instinct (Eros) seeks to bind, unite, and preserve life.* The two fundamental instincts are locked in perpetual conflict within every organism.
* Sadism is the death instinct deflected outward onto external objects.
* Masochism is the death instinct remaining within, bound with libido.
* The compulsion to repeat overrides the pleasure principle; patients repeat painful 
experiences.
* Traumatic neurosis demonstrates the compulsion to repeat experiences that were 
never pleasurable.
* The repetition compulsion is more primitive than the pleasure principle; it serves the 
instincts themselves.
* Children's repetitive play represents an attempt to master overwhelming experiences 
through active repetition.
* The fort-da game transforms passivity into activity: the child controls the mother's 
disappearance symbolically.
* The conservative nature of instinct is the tendency to restore an earlier state of things.
* If the goal of life is death, organisms seek to die in their own way—the detour to death.
* Civilization requires the renunciation and sublimation of both sexual and aggressive 
instincts.
* Aggression is the chief obstacle to civilization; guilt is civilization's method of 
controlling aggression.
* The superego turns aggression against the self, producing the sense of guilt that 
torments civilized humanity.FREUD COLLECTED PAPERS POSITION STATEMENT UNCANNY MEDUSA'S 
HEAD ETC

POSITION STATEMENTS
FREUD: SELECTED PAPERS

      ========================================
THE UNCANNY (1919)
      ========================================

1. The uncanny (unheimlich) is that class of frightening things which leads us back to 
what is known and familiar.

2. The German word heimlich contains within its semantic range a meaning that 
overlaps with its opposite unheimlich; what is homely and familiar also means what is 
concealed and hidden.

3. The uncanny is the mark of the return of the repressed—something familiar that has 
become alienated through the process of repression.

4. Intellectual uncertainty about whether something is animate or inanimate (automata, 
dolls, waxwork figures) contributes to uncanny feelings.

5. The fear of losing one's eyes, as in Hoffmann's "The Sandman," is a substitute for 
the dread of castration.

6. The figure of the double (doppelgänger) was originally an insurance against the 
destruction of the ego and a denial of the power of death.

7. When the stage of primary narcissism is surmounted, the double changes from an 
assurance of immortality into the uncanny harbinger of death.

8. The double embodies all the unfulfilled possibilities of our lives, suppressed acts of 
volition, and frustrated strivings of the ego.

9. Repetition of the same situations, events, or numbers produces uncanny effects by 
suggesting the operation of fateful forces.

10. The omnipotence of thoughts—the overvaluation of mental processes over 
reality—is a source of uncanny feelings when it appears confirmed.

11. There is a distinction between the uncanny as actually experienced and the 
uncanny as merely pictured or read about in fiction.

12. Fiction can produce more uncanny effects than real life because the author can 
control the conditions under which events occur.

13. Uncanny effects in fiction are limited when the author establishes a realistic setting 
that conforms to ordinary reality.14. The uncanny arises when infantile complexes that have been repressed are revived,
 or when primitive beliefs that have been surmounted seem confirmed.

15. The return of the dead, ghosts, and spirits produce uncanny effects because 
primitive fear of the dead persists despite intellectual surmounting.

      ========================================
CRIMINALS FROM A SENSE OF GUILT (1916)
      ========================================

16. Some individuals commit crimes because they are suffering from an oppressive 
sense of guilt of unknown origin that precedes the criminal act.

17. Criminal acts in such cases serve to attach the pre-existing, free-floating sense of 
guilt to something concrete and explicable.

18. The commission of a crime brings relief because the external punishment it invites 
is less severe than the internal torment of unconscious guilt.

19. The obscure sense of guilt that precedes crime derives from the Oedipus 
complex—from wishes to kill the father and have sexual relations with the mother.

20. This mechanism of crime from guilt feelings does not apply to criminals without 
moral inhibitions or those who rationalize their antisocial behavior.

21. Many minor infractions committed during analytic treatment can be understood as 
attempts to alleviate an unbearable sense of guilt.

22. The "pale criminal" described by Nietzsche can be understood psychoanalytically 
as one driven to crime by unconscious guilt seeking punishment.

23. Children often become naughty in order to provoke punishment, after which they 
become calm and satisfied.

24. The desire for punishment from parental figures can persist into adult life and 
motivate seemingly inexplicable criminal behavior.

      ========================================
NEGATION (1925)
      ========================================

25. Negation is a way of taking cognizance of what is repressed; it constitutes a lifting 
of repression, though not an acceptance of the repressed content.

26. When a patient says "It is not my mother" in reporting a dream figure, this negation 
is the surest indication that it is indeed the mother.

27. Negation allows repressed ideational content to reach consciousness on the condition that it is denied—separating intellectual function from affective process.

28. The outcome of negation is intellectual acceptance of the repressed while the 
essential aspect of repression persists emotionally.

29. Conquering the patient's negation and achieving intellectual acceptance does not 
automatically remove the repressive process itself.

30. Judgment has two functions: affirming or disaffirming that a thing possesses a 
particular attribute, and asserting or disputing that a presentation exists in reality.

31. The function of judgment concerning attributes derives from the original interplay of 
introjection and projection based on the pleasure principle.

32. The original pleasure-ego wants to introject all that is good and expel all that is bad, 
equating good with ego and bad with external.

33. The reality-ego develops the capacity to refind in perception an object that 
corresponds to what was once a source of real satisfaction.

34. Reality-testing determines whether something that exists as a presentation in the 
ego can also be rediscovered in perception.

35. Thinking possesses the capacity to bring before the mind something perceived in 
the past by reproducing it as a presentation without the external object.

36. Affirmation—as a substitute for uniting—belongs to Eros; negation—the successor 
to expulsion—belongs to the instinct of destruction.

37. The general wish to negate displayed by some psychotics may indicate a defusion 
of instincts through withdrawal of libidinal components.

38. There is no "no" in the unconscious; recognition of the unconscious by the ego is 
expressed in a negative formula.

      ========================================
CIVILIZED SEXUAL MORALITY AND MODERN NERVOUS ILLNESS (1908)
      ========================================

39. Civilization imposes sexual frustration that has a direct effect on the genesis of 
neuroses.

40. There are three stages of civilization with respect to sexual instinct: free exercise of 
sexuality, restriction to reproduction only, and restriction to legitimate reproduction only.

41. Modern civilized sexual morality demands abstinence until marriage and 
monogamous fidelity within it—standards most people cannot meet without damage.

42. Sublimation of sexual instinctual forces toward higher cultural aims can only be achieved by a minority, and then only intermittently.

43. The sexual instinct is not originally designed to serve reproduction but aims at 
obtaining particular kinds of pleasure (organ-pleasure).

44. Suppression of the sexual life of civilized peoples through conventional morality 
causes nervous illness on a large scale.

45. Women suffer particularly from civilized sexual morality because their legitimate 
sexual outlet within marriage is often unsatisfying.

46. Premarital sexual abstinence damages women's capacity for full sexual response 
and damages men's capacity for tender relationships.

47. The cultural gains achieved through sexual repression may be offset by the 
intellectual and creative losses caused by neurosis.

48. Neurotic illness represents a flight into illness when the ego cannot otherwise 
manage the demands of civilization upon sexuality.

49. Marriage under current conditions often fails to satisfy the sexual needs of either 
partner and prohibits the extramarital satisfaction that might compensate.

50. The strict sexual morality that injures individuals ultimately injures society by
