producing widespread neurosis and diminished productivity.

      ========================================
REPRESSION (1915)
      ========================================

51. Repression is a preliminary stage of condemnation, something between flight and 
judgment; the ego cannot escape from instinctual demands as it can from external 
stimuli.

52. The motive and purpose of repression is the avoidance of unpleasure; repression 
occurs when satisfaction of an instinct would produce unpleasure instead of pleasure.

53. Primal repression (Urverdrängung) is the first phase of repression in which the 
psychical representative of an instinct is denied entrance into consciousness.

54. Primal repression establishes a fixation; the repressed representative persists 
unaltered and the instinct remains attached to it.

55. Repression proper (or after-pressure) is a second phase affecting psychical 
derivatives of the repressed representative or associative chains connected to it.

56. Repression proper involves both attraction from what was primally repressed and 
repulsion from consciousness acting together.57. Repression is not a single event but requires continuous expenditure of force to 
maintain; its removal results in an economic saving.

58. The repressed exercises continuous pressure toward consciousness, which must 
be balanced by unceasing counter-pressure (anticathexis).

59. Derivatives of the repressed can gain access to consciousness if they are 
sufficiently distorted or distant from the original repressed material.

60. Repression acts individually on each derivative of the repressed; each must 
undergo its own fate depending on its degree of distortion.

61. It is not repression itself that produces symptoms, but rather the return of the 
  repressed—symptoms are indicators of such return.

62. In anxiety hysteria, repression is initially successful but fails as excitation spreads to 
substitute formations (phobias) requiring fresh efforts of repression.

63. In conversion hysteria, repression may succeed in disposing of affect entirely, but 
the ideational content finds expression through somatic innervation.

64. In obsessional neurosis, the initial repression is followed by failure, return of the 
repressed, and secondary defensive measures (reaction formations, obsessive actions).


      ========================================
INSTINCTS AND THEIR VICISSITUDES (1915)
      ========================================

65. An instinct (Trieb) is a stimulus to the mind arising from within the organism, 
operating as a constant force that cannot be escaped through flight.

66. An instinct has four characteristics: pressure (motor force), aim (satisfaction 
through removal of stimulation), object (means to the aim), and source (somatic 
process).

67. The object of an instinct is its most variable characteristic; it is not originally 
connected with the instinct and becomes attached through its fitness for satisfaction.

68. The two primary groups of instincts are the ego or self-preservative instincts and 
the sexual instincts.

69. Sexual instincts are distinguished by their ability to change objects readily and their 
capacity for sublimation.

70. The four vicissitudes (fates) that instincts may undergo are: reversal into the 
opposite, turning round upon the subject's own self, repression, and sublimation.

71. Reversal into the opposite involves either a change from activity to passivity (in aims) or a reversal of content (transformation of love into hate).

72. Turning round upon the subject's own self involves changing the object while the 
aim remains the same—as when sadism becomes masochism.

73. In the transformation from sadism to masochism, there is a change of object (from 
another to the self) and a change of aim (from active to passive).

74. The preliminary narcissistic stage intervenes between sadistic and masochistic 
phases, where the self becomes the object.

75. Voyeurism and exhibitionism represent parallel transformations: looking at an 
external object, looking at one's own body, being looked at.

76. Masochism is actually sadism turned round upon the subject's own ego, and the 
masochist shares in the enjoyment of the assault upon himself.

77. Love and hate are not related to each other as simple opposites; they have 
complex and independent developmental histories.

78. Hate as a relation to objects is older than love; it derives from the narcissistic ego's 
primordial repudiation of the external world and its stimuli.

79. At the preliminary stages of love (incorporating, mastering), love is hardly 
distinguishable from hate in its attitude toward the object.

80. The three great polarities governing mental life are: subject (ego) versus object 
(external world), pleasure versus unpleasure, and active versus passive.

      ========================================
SOME CHARACTER TYPES MET WITH IN PSYCHO-ANALYTIC WORK (1916)
      ========================================

81. "The Exceptions" are those who feel entitled to special privileges in compensation 
for early injury or suffering they experienced as innocent children.

82. Exceptions believe their early undeserved suffering exempts them from the 
renunciations that others must accept as part of normal life.

83. Richard III, as depicted by Shakespeare, exemplifies the Exception who claims the 
right to be unjust because Nature was first unjust to him.

84. Women often feel entitled to make exceptional demands because they regard 
themselves as having been damaged through no fault of their own (castration).

85. "Those Wrecked by Success" are people who fall ill precisely when a deeply-rooted 
wish comes to fulfillment—illness replaces the expected happiness.

86. The paradox of wrecked by success shows that internal forces of conscience can prohibit enjoyment of what has been legitimately attained.

87. Lady Macbeth exemplifies one wrecked by success, falling ill when she achieves 
the crown she desired, destroyed by the very fulfillment of her ambition.

88. Rebecca West in Ibsen's Rosmersholm similarly collapses when marriage to 
Rosmer becomes possible, as if success revealed forbidden Oedipal wishes.

89. The collapse upon success occurs because achieving the forbidden wish makes 
the underlying Oedipal guilt conscious and intolerable.

90. Unconscious guilt can remain dormant while a wish remains unfulfilled but becomes 
overwhelming when external obstacles are removed.

      ========================================
SOME NEUROTIC MECHANISMS IN JEALOUSY, PARANOIA, AND 
HOMOSEXUALITY (1922)
      ========================================

91. Jealousy is one of those affective states that may be described as normal; its 
absence in someone indicates severe repression and great unconscious significance.

92. There are three layers of jealousy: competitive or normal jealousy, projected 
jealousy, and delusional jealousy.

93. Normal jealousy is compounded of grief over the lost loved object, narcissistic 
wound, and hostile feelings toward the successful rival.

94. Normal jealousy is rooted in the Oedipus complex and sibling rivalry; it also draws 
on unconscious homosexual attraction to the rival.

95. Projected jealousy derives from actual unfaithfulness on the subject's part or from 
repressed impulses toward unfaithfulness.

96. In projected jealousy, subjects who resist their own temptations to infidelity perceive 
their partner as unfaithful instead—a projection onto the other.

97. Social convention permits a degree of coquetry and flirtation in marriage, and 
projected jealousy interprets these within the measure of one's own temptations.

98. Delusional jealousy is an acidulated (soured) homosexuality and rightly takes its 
place among the classical forms of paranoia.

99. Delusional jealousy represents an attempt at defense against an unduly strong 
homosexual impulse through the formula: "I do not love him—she loves him."

100. In male paranoid delusions, the proposition "I (a man) love him (a man)" is 
contradicted in various ways producing persecution, erotomania, or jealousy.101. Male homosexuality frequently develops through the boy remaining fixated on his 
mother and later identifying with her, seeking objects resembling himself.

102. The homosexual man loves as he wished his mother to love him; his object choice 
proceeds by way of narcissism toward youth and physical resemblance.

103. Intense jealousy combined with strong homosexual impulses can transform into 
paranoid persecutory delusions through successive denials and projections.

104. Analysis reveals that behind all these forms of jealousy there is also a mourning-
like grief at the loss of an object and the blow to narcissism.

105. The treatment of projected jealousy requires not disputing the patient's evidence 
but helping them view the situation differently.

I have the research. Here are the position statements:

      ========================================
THE LOSS OF REALITY IN NEUROSIS AND PSYCHOSIS (1924)
      ========================================

1. In neurosis, the ego in its dependence on reality suppresses a piece of the id 
(instinctual life); in psychosis, the ego in service of the id withdraws from a piece of 
reality.

2. For neurosis the decisive factor is the predominance of the influence of reality; for 
psychosis it is the predominance of the id.

3. Both neurosis and psychosis involve two steps: in neurosis, the ego first represses 
an instinctual impulse, then attempts to compensate for the damage to the id.

4. In psychosis, the first step drags the ego away from reality, while the second tries to 
make good the damage by creating a new reality.

5. Every neurosis disturbs the patient's relation to reality; in its severe forms it signifies 
a flight from real life.

6. Neurosis does not disavow reality—it only ignores it; psychosis disavows reality and 
tries to replace it.

7. In neurosis, a piece of reality is avoided by a sort of flight; in psychosis, reality is 
remodeled.

8. The psychotic reaction to unbearable reality would be a disavowal of the fact itself, 
whereas the neurotic reaction is repression of the instinctual wish aroused by that fact.

9. The world of phantasy plays the same role in neurosis as the "storeroom" from which 
materials for the new reality are drawn in psychosis.10. Normal or healthy behavior combines features of both reactions: it disavows reality 
as little as neurosis but exerts itself like psychosis to alter that reality through work on 
the external world.

11. In psychosis, the initial flight from reality is succeeded by an active phase of 
remodeling; in neurosis, initial obedience is succeeded by a deferred attempt at flight.

12. The newly constructed world of psychosis follows the wishful impulses of the id, 
while the reason for the break with reality was the frustration of these same wishes.

13. The loss of reality in psychosis affects the perception of external reality; the loss of 
reality in neurosis affects the relation between ego and id.

14. Delusions and hallucinations in psychosis are attempts to create a substitute reality 
that better satisfies the id's demands.

      ========================================
FETISHISM (1927)
      ========================================

15. The fetish is a substitute for a particular and quite special penis—the mother's 
phallus which the little boy once believed in and does not want to give up.

16. The boy refuses to take cognizance of the fact that a woman has no penis because 
this would mean acknowledging that his own possession of a penis is in danger.

17. The fetish is not simply a substitute for any penis but specifically for the mother's 
penis that the boy believed in and now disavows.

18. In creating a fetish, the boy has retained his belief in the maternal phallus but has 
also given it up—both attitudes persist side by side.

19. The fetish represents a compromise between the perception of maternal castration 
and the counter-wish that denies it.

20. The creation of a fetish involves a process akin to the stopping of memory in 
traumatic amnesia—interest is frozen at the last moment before the traumatic discovery.


21. The fetish is a token of triumph over the threat of castration and a protection 
against it.

22. The fetish saves the fetishist from becoming homosexual by endowing women with 
the characteristic that makes them tolerable as sexual objects.

23. The fetishist enjoys an advantage: the fetish is easily accessible, not withheld from 
him, and he can readily obtain the sexual satisfaction attached to it.

24. Affection and hostility in the treatment of the fetish run parallel with the disavowal and acknowledgment of castration, mixed in varying proportions.

25. Some fetishes represent both the disavowal and the assertion of castration 
simultaneously—as when a woman's underclothing can be viewed either as castrated 
or as bearing concealed genitals.

26. In cases involving disavowal, one current of mental life recognizes reality while 
another simultaneously denies it—producing a split in the ego.

27. The concept of disavowal (Verleugnung) describes the fate of the idea, as distinct 
from repression (Verdrängung), which describes the fate of the affect.

28. Disavowal is not the same as complete erasure (scotomization); the perception 
persists but is simultaneously affirmed and denied.

29. Probably no male human being is spared the fright of castration at the sight of a 
female genital; why some become homosexual, some create fetishes, and most 
surmount it, we cannot explain.

      ========================================
MEDUSA'S HEAD (1922)
      ========================================

30. To decapitate equals to castrate; the terror of Medusa is thus a terror of castration 
linked to the sight of something.

31. The terrifying decapitated head of Medusa represents the castrated female genital, 
specifically the mother's genital surrounded by hair.

32. The occasion for castration terror is when a boy catches sight of the female 
genitals—probably those of an adult, essentially those of his mother.

33. The hair upon Medusa's head, frequently represented as snakes, is derived from 
the castration complex.

34. Though the snakes are frightening in themselves, they serve to mitigate the horror 
because they replace the penis whose absence causes the horror.

35. The multiplication of penis symbols (the many snakes) paradoxically signifies 
castration—this is a technical rule confirmed by the Medusa image.

36. The sight of Medusa's head makes the spectator stiff with terror, turns him to 
stone—and becoming stiff means an erection.

37. The stiffening offers consolation to the spectator: he is still in possession of a penis, 
and the erection reassures him of this fact.

38. Displaying the erect penis (or its substitute) is a way of intimidating evil 
spirits—saying "I am not afraid of you, I defy you, I have a penis."39. The virgin goddess Athena wears the symbol of horror (Medusa's head) on her 
dress, thus becoming a woman who is unapproachable and repels all sexual desire.

40. Athena displaying the terrifying genitals of the Mother represents a woman who 
repels desire because she exhibits the proof of castration.

41. The Greeks, being in the main strongly homosexual, inevitably represented woman 
as a being who frightens and repels because she is castrated.FREUD DEVIL AS FATHER SUBSTITUTE ETC POSITION STATEMENT

Here are **100 Position Statements** drawn from Freud’s papers **“The 
Unconscious”** (1915), **“Negation”** (1925), and **“The Devil as Father Substitute”** 
(1923), presented as a concise list of core theoretical claims.

---

**From “The Unconscious” (1915):**
1. The unconscious is a distinct mental system with its own processes.
2. Unconscious mental processes are timeless and not ordered chronologically.
3. The unconscious is governed by the primary process (condensation, displacement).
4. Unconscious ideas are not subject to negation or contradiction.
5. Unconscious material is indestructible—it persists unchanged over time.
6. Repression is the mechanism by which material becomes unconscious.
7. Unconscious material can only enter consciousness indirectly (e.g., in dreams, slips).
8. The unconscious contains instinctual drives (Triebe) seeking discharge.
9. Unconscious ideas are hypercathected with psychic energy.
10. The unconscious knows no logic, morality, or reality testing.
11. Consciousness is only a small part of mental life.
12. The preconscious mediates between the unconscious and consciousness.
13. Unconscious mental contents can affect behavior without awareness.
14. Resistance prevents unconscious material from reaching consciousness.
15. Psychoanalysis aims to make the unconscious conscious.
16. Symbolism in dreams is a disguised expression of unconscious content.
17. Unconscious fantasies can shape symptoms.
18. Unconscious mental activity operates according to the pleasure principle.
19. Unconscious material can be organized around primal fantasies.
20. The unconscious is structured like a language (condensation/displacement).
21. Unconscious affects can be displaced or transformed.
22. Unconscious conflict is at the root of neurosis.
23. Unconscious content can be inferred through interpretation of symptoms.
24. The unconscious is not accessible to introspection.
25. Unconscious processes are exempt from the laws of time and space.

---

**From “Negation” (1925):**
26. Negation is an intellectual function that allows repressed content to enter 
awareness in a denied form.
27. Saying “I did not think that” is a way of acknowledging unconscious content while 
disavowing it.
28. Negation is a defense mechanism that permits partial acceptance of repressed 
material.
29. Through negation, the ego can consider what it has repressed without accepting it.
30. Negation is a step toward intellectual acceptance, but not emotional acceptance.
31. Denial in language (“not”) reveals what is unconsciously affirmed.
32. The content of a negation is always unconsciously true for the subject.
33. Negation preserves repression while allowing intellectual exploration.34. The judgment of existence (“this is real”) precedes the judgment of attribution (“this 
is good/bad”).
35. Negation is a way the ego tests reality while defending against anxiety.
36. Repression can be lifted in the form of negation.
37. Negation is a primitive form of defense, originating in the oral stage (expulsion/
incorporation).
38. Saying “no” is a symbolic act of expulsion.
39. The function of judgment develops from the original pleasure-ego’s acceptance/
rejection of stimuli.
40. Affirmation (Einhaltung) is symbolic of incorporation.
41. The capacity to negate is a developmental achievement.
42. Negation allows the subject to acknowledge unpleasant realities in a tolerable way.
43. In analysis, negation signals proximity to repressed material.
44. Negation reveals the structure of repression.
45. The “no” of the patient often means “yes” unconsciously.
46. Negation is a compromise between the conscious and unconscious.
47. The ego uses negation to avoid the full emotional impact of repressed content.
48. Negation preserves the ego’s sense of control over forbidden thoughts.
49. Intellectualization often accompanies negation.
50. Negation is a form of resistance in analysis.

---

**From “The Devil as Father Substitute” (1923):**
51. The figure of the Devil is an externalization of repressed hostility toward the father.
52. The Devil represents the ambivalent feelings toward the paternal authority.
53. The Devil is a father substitute in folklore and religion.
54. The projection of hostility onto the Devil allows the subject to maintain love for the 
real father.
55. The Devil embodies repressed rebellious and aggressive impulses toward the 
father.
56. This projection is a defense against castration anxiety.
57. The Devil often appears in dreams and fantasies as a disguised paternal figure.
58. The Devil myth preserves the father’s idealized image by externalizing his negative 
traits.
59. Satanic temptation symbolizes unconscious rebellion against paternal prohibition.
60. The pact with the Devil represents a forbidden alliance with repressed instincts.
61. The Devil is a cultural representation of the id’s aggressive and sexual urges.
62. Belief in demons is a regression to animistic thinking.
63. Demonology is a projection of internal conflict onto supernatural beings.
64. The Devil serves as a scapegoat for human evil.
65. Rituals of devil-worship express repressed defiance of paternal authority.
66. The Devil is often portrayed with phallic and animalistic traits, linking him to primal 
instincts.
67. The struggle between God and Devil reflects internal conflict between super-ego 
and id.
68. The Devil figure allows for symbolic expression of oedipal rivalry.
69. The fear of the Devil is a displaced fear of the father’s punitive power.
70. The Devil’s seductiveness reflects forbidden infantile desires.71. The concept of possession by the Devil represents the ego overwhelmed by id 
impulses.
72. Exorcism rituals attempt to externalize and expel repressed aggression.
73. The Devil myth serves to manage guilt by attributing evil to an external source.
74. The father’s death or absence can intensify Devil imagery.
75. Satanic temptation narratives dramatize the conflict between morality and instinct.
76. The Devil embodies the “bad father” archetype.
77. The Devil is a cultural container for projected patricidal wishes.
78. Religious demonization is a form of collective defense against ambivalence toward 
authority.
79. The Devil is a symbolic compromise between love and hate for the father.
80. Art and literature about the Devil reveal universal psychodynamic themes.
81. The Devil figure varies across cultures but consistently represents forbidden 
rebellion.
82. The fantasy of selling one’s soul to the Devil reflects unconscious guilt and self-
punishment.
83. The Devil as trickster embodies deceitful aspects of the unconscious.
84. The Devil’s role in witch hunts illustrates mass projection of repressed aggression.
85. Clinical cases of “demonic” symptoms can be interpreted as expressions of 
repressed conflict.
86. The Devil often appears in the dreams of patients with authoritarian fathers.
87. The Devil myth provides a socially sanctioned outlet for repressed hostility.
88. The Devil’s association with animals (goat, serpent) symbolizes untamed instinct.
89. Belief in the Devil diminishes with resolution of oedipal conflict.
90. Psychoanalysis interprets devil-figures as symbolic representations of internal 
objects.
91. The Devil can also represent maternal seduction in some variants.
92. The figure of Lucifer as fallen angel reflects idealization followed by denigration.
93. The Devil’s punishment in hell symbolizes unconscious fear of paternal retribution.
94. The Devil is used in moral tales to reinforce super-ego demands.
95. Children’s fantasies of monsters often prefigure devil-imagery.
96. The Devil is a culturally constructed “container” for projected aggression.
97. In psychosis, the Devil may be experienced as a hallucinated persecutor.
98. The Devil’s role in religion serves to unify groups through shared projection.
99. The Devil as father substitute helps explain the persistence of supernatural belief 
systems.
100. Analysis of devil-fantasies can uncover early parental conflicts and repressed 
aggression.

FEMALE SEXUALITY 

Here are **100 Position Statements on Female Sexuality** drawn from Freud's works, 
including *Three Essays on the Theory of Sexuality*, *Female Sexuality*, *Some 
Psychical Consequences of the Anatomical Distinction Between the Sexes*, 
*Femininity*, and related texts.

---

### **Freud’s Positions on Female Sexuality**1. Female psychosexual development is more complex and obscure than male 
development.
2. Both sexes initially assume the presence of a penis; girls begin with a “masculine” 
phase.
3. The clitoris is the primary erotogenic zone for girls in early childhood.
4. Girls experience penis envy upon realizing their anatomical difference.
5. Penis envy is a central, organizing moment in female psychosexual development.
6. The discovery of anatomical difference leads to castration anxiety in boys, but 
castration *realization* in girls.
7. The girl holds her mother responsible for her “lack” of a penis.
8. The girl turns away from her mother in disappointment and resentment.
9. This turn from mother to father is a pivotal shift in object-choice.
10. The Oedipus complex in girls is preceded and motivated by penis envy.
11. The female Oedipus complex is often not fully resolved, persisting into adulthood.
12. The shift from clitoral to vaginal pleasure is a developmental task for women.
13. The vagina becomes the primary erotogenic zone only after puberty.
14. Immature sexuality is “masculine” (clitoral); mature sexuality is “feminine” (vaginal).
15. Women who retain clitoral dominance are fixated at an infantile stage.
16. The wish for a penis is later transformed into the wish for a baby.
17. A baby symbolizes a substitute for the missing penis.
18. Motherhood fulfills the displaced penis wish.
19. Female sexuality has a double orientation: active (masculine) and passive 
(feminine).
20. Passivity is the dominant sexual attitude in mature femininity.
21. Masochism is a natural component of female sexuality.
22. Women have weaker super-ego development due to less resolved Oedipal conflict.
23. The female super-ego is less severe, more personal, and more influenced by 
emotional ties.
24. Women’s moral sense is more relational and less abstract than men’s.
25. The shift from active to passive aims is central to feminine development.
26. The girl must change both her erotogenic zone (clitoris to vagina) and her object 
(mother to father).
27. This double change makes female development more precarious.
28. Female sexuality is more repressed and less visible than male sexuality.
29. Women are more prone to hysteria, which originates in repressed sexual wishes.
30. The female sexual drive is more diffuse and less urgently localized than the male 
drive.
31. Women’s sexuality is more influenced by narcissism.
32. Vanity and shame are typical feminine traits rooted in penis envy.
33. Jealousy in women is more social and relational than sexual.
34. Women have a weaker sense of justice, stemming from lesser castration anxiety.
35. The female Oedipus complex is often maintained unconsciously throughout life.
36. The girl’s pre-Oedipal attachment to the mother is longer and more intense than the 
boy’s.
37. This pre-Oedipal phase is decisive for later feminine attitudes.
38. The mother is the girl’s first love object, just as she is for the boy.
39. The girl’s hostility toward the mother is more enduring than the boy’s.
40. The feminine turn toward the father is an attempt to obtain the penis symbolically.41. The fantasy of seduction by the father is common in women’s unconscious life.
42. The wish for a penis can manifest as competitiveness with men (“masculinity 
complex”).
43. Some women never fully relinquish the wish for a penis and remain in a “masculine” 
stance.
44. The bedipal wish for a baby from the father is a normal phase of feminine 
development.
45. Menstruation is unconsciously perceived as a wound confirming castration.
46. Defloration fantasies are linked to castration anxiety and masochistic submission.
47. The female castration complex can lead to sexual inhibition or frigidity.
48. Frigidity is a failure to transfer erotic sensitivity from clitoris to vagina.
49. Lesbianism can result from a refusal to accept castration and a clinging to 
masculine identification.
50. Homosexuality in women is a regression to the early attachment to the mother.
51. The “family romance” fantasy in girls often involves idealized paternal figures.
52. Women’s dreams more frequently contain symbols of penetration and enclosure.
53. The vagina is often symbolized in dreams by containers, rooms, or hollow objects.
54. Female masturbation in childhood is primarily clitoral and is often repressed.
55. Repression of masturbation can lead to later sexual anesthesia.
56. The onset of menstruation reawakens early sexual theories and anxieties.
57. Pregnancy fulfills an unconscious wish for a penis but also stirs envy of male 
creative power.
58. Childbirth is unconsciously associated with castration and payment of a debt.
59. The menopause can precipitate neurosis due to loss of reproductive capacity.
60. Aging is psychologically harder for women because it diminishes sexual value.
61. The “beauty” ideal in women is a displacement from the valued penis to the whole 
body.
62. Women are more susceptible to shame because the genitals are “deficient.”
63. The female genitalia are perceived as a “wound” in the unconscious.
64. The girl’s early sexual researches are dominated by the question of the penis.
65. The sight of male genitals can trigger sexual trauma or fixation.
66. The primal scene is interpreted by the girl as an act of submission and injury.
67. The fantasy of being raped is a common masochistic elaboration in women.
68. The wish to be a man persists in the unconscious of many women.
69. “Normal” femininity is a difficult developmental achievement.
70. Many women display a “masculinity complex” in intellectual or professional 
ambitions.
71. The desire for knowledge in women can be a sublimation of penis envy.
72. Women’s social subordination is unconsciously accepted as punishment for 
castration.
73. The female psyche is more inclined toward neurosis due to developmental 
complexities.
74. Hysterical symptoms often express conflicted feminine wishes in disguised form.
75. The fantasy of having a penis can be expressed through symptoms like vaginal 
pain or headaches.
76. The female super-ego is more tolerant of pregenital impulses.
77. Women are more likely to use repression rather than sublimation.
78. The shift from active to passive aims can result in general passivity in life.
79. The girl’s aggression toward the mother is often turned inward as self-reproach.80. Depression in women is linked to unsatisfied Oedipal longings.
81. The female ideal of “being loved” replaces the active wish “to love.”
82. Women’s sexual pleasure is more dependent on emotional intimacy.
83. The vaginal orgasm represents full psychosexual maturity.
84. Clitoral orgasm is considered immature and infantile.
85. The latency period in girls is less distinct and more permeable.
86. The girl’s Oedipus complex is gradually abandoned rather than shattered.
87. The woman’s attachment to the father is transferred to the husband.
88. The husband is always a substitute for the father.
89. Disappointment with the husband revives earlier disappointment with the father.
90. The wish for a penis can be satisfied symbolically through male achievement or 
male children.
91. The birth of a son is a triumph over castration.
92. The birth of a daughter can renew penis envy.
93. The mother-daughter relationship is fraught with ambivalence and identification.
94. The girl’s identification with the mother is both desired and resisted.
95. The super-ego in women is modeled more on the mother than the father.
96. Female masochism is eroticized as a means of obtaining love.
97. The fantasy of being beaten is common in women and rooted in Oedipal guilt.
98. Women’s sexual fantasies often involve submission and punishment.
99. The “dark continent” metaphor acknowledges the incomplete psychoanalytic 
understanding of women.
100. Psychoanalysis can only describe feminine development; it cannot prescribe what 
women should be.

 

MALE SEXUALITY 

Here are **100 Position Statements on Male Sexuality** drawn from Freud’s works, 
including *Three Essays on the Theory of Sexuality*, *The Dissolution of the Oedipus 
Complex*, *Civilization and Its Discontents*, and related texts.

---

### **Freud’s Positions on Male Sexuality**

1. Male psychosexual development follows a more straightforward path than female 
development.
2. The penis is the primary erotogenic zone for boys from infancy onward.
3. Boys experience castration anxiety upon seeing female genitalia, interpreting the 
absence as evidence of possible loss.
4. Castration anxiety is the central organizing anxiety of male psychosexual 
development.
5. The Oedipus complex in boys is resolved through fear of castration by the father.
6. The boy desires exclusive possession of the mother and views the father as a rival.
7. The threat of castration forces the boy to abandon his incestuous desire for the 
mother.
8. Resolution of the Oedipus complex leads to identification with the father.9. This identification forms the basis of the super-ego in males.
10. The male super-ego is typically more severe, rigid, and abstract than the female 
super-ego.
11. Male sexuality is more outwardly directed and object-oriented than female sexuality.
12. The libido in males is more localized, urgent, and genitally focused.
13. Male sexual aim is predominantly active and penetrative.
14. Sadism is a natural component of male sexuality.
15. The phallic phase (ages 3–6) is marked by heightened narcissistic investment in 
the penis.
16. Masturbation in childhood is universal and centered on the penis.
17. Prohibition of masturbation by parents reinforces castration anxiety.
18. The fantasy of the phallic mother is typical in early male development.
19. The boy initially believes all people have a penis.
20. Discovery of sexual difference precipitates the castration complex.
21. The sight of female genitalia is traumatic and can lead to sexual aversion or 
fetishism.
22. Fetishism arises as a defense against castration anxiety by disavowing female “lack.
”
23. Male homosexuality may result from an unresolved Oedipus complex with strong 
identification with the mother.
24. Inversion (homosexuality) can stem from narcissistic object-choice.
25. The latency period in boys is more pronounced, involving repression of sexual 
impulses.
26. Puberty reawakens Oedipal desires, which must be redirected toward non-
incestuous objects.
27. The first non-incestuous love object is often a substitute for the mother.
28. Men tend to split the image of woman into “madonna” (mother) and “whore” (sexual 
object).
29. This madonna/whore complex reflects unresolved incestuous attachment.
30. Male sexual potency can be impaired by unresolved Oedipal guilt.
31. Premature ejaculation and impotence are often psychogenic, rooted in anxiety.
32. The male sexual drive is more easily separated from affection than the female drive.
33. Men are more prone to perversions due to the rigidity of the Oedipal resolution.
34. Perversions represent a fixation or regression to pregenital stages.
35. The scopophilic drive (pleasure in looking) is stronger in males.
36. Exhibitionism is a typically male perversion rooted in phallic narcissism.
37. Voyeurism satisfies the desire to see the “missing” female penis.
38. The primal scene (witnessing parental intercourse) is interpreted by the boy as an 
act of violence by the father.
39. This interpretation reinforces castration anxiety and sadistic impulses.
40. The boy’s aggression toward the father is repressed and transformed into 
identification.
41. Unresolved aggression can manifest as sadism or antisocial behavior.
42. The male super-ego internalizes paternal authority and societal prohibitions.
43. Guilt in men stems largely from unconscious parricidal wishes.
44. The fear of the father’s retaliation drives moral development.
45. Men have a stronger tendency toward sublimation of sexual energy into cultural 
achievements.
46. Male creativity is often a sublimation of phallic narcissism.47. The drive for mastery and control is an extension of active sexual aims.
48. Men are more prone to obsessional neurosis, characterized by reaction-formation 
against aggressive impulses.
49. Obsessive rituals often symbolize undoing of unconscious guilty wishes.
50. The fantasy of beating or being beaten is common in men, linked to Oedipal guilt.
51. Masochism in men is considered a reversal of sadism and a form of guilt expiation.
52. The death instinct is more readily turned outward as aggression in males.
53. War and violence are partially rooted in sublimated male aggression.
54. Male friendship and group bonding are desexualized homosexual impulses.
55. Social structures (like fraternities, militaries) channel homoerotic energy into 
collective aims.
56. The taboo against homosexuality strengthens male social bonds through 
repression.
57. The male fear of femininity (passivity) is a defense against castration anxiety.
58. Passivity is equated with castration in the male unconscious.
59. Male anxiety about performance reflects castration anxiety.
60. The concept of “virility” is a cultural idealization of phallic potency.
61. Men are more likely to develop phobias as displacement of castration anxiety.
62. Animal phobias (e.g., fear of horses) often symbolize fear of the father.
63. The hero myth represents the son’s triumph over the father in symbolic form.
64. Religion projects the father-figure onto God, satisfying both reverence and rebellion.
65. Male atheism can be an unconscious rebellion against the paternal authority of God.
66. The son’s identification with the father allows internalization of cultural norms.
67. Failure to identify leads to delinquency or psychosis.
68. Psychosis in men may involve a break with paternal law (foreclosure of the Name-
of-the-Father).
69. Paranoia in men often stems from repressed homosexual desire.
70. The delusion of persecution is a defense against unconscious homosexual love.
71. Jealousy in men is more sexual and possessive than in women.
72. Men are more prone to fetishistic attachment to objects (foot, hair, clothing).
73. Fetish objects symbolically represent the missing maternal phallus.
74. Transvestism in men is a fetishistic denial of female castration.
75. The male sexual response is more automatic and genitally focused than the female.
76. Men are less likely to develop conversion symptoms (e.g., hysterical paralysis).
77. Male hysteria exists but is rarer, often disguised as hypochondria.
78. The male climacteric (midlife crisis) involves anxiety over diminishing potency.
79. Fear of aging in men is linked to loss of phallic power.
80. The wish for a son represents a narcissistic extension of the self.
81. The birth of a son mitigates castration anxiety through symbolic immortality.
82. The birth of a daughter may unconsciously represent castration.
83. Father–son rivalry can reawaken Oedipal dynamics in later life.
84. The successful father must permit the son to surpass him.
85. Male mentorship is a sublimated form of paternal identification.
86. The incest taboo is more severe for men culturally because of stronger Oedipal 
prohibition.
87. Polygamous impulses are remnants of primitive male sexual freedom.
88. Monogamy requires repression of polygamous tendencies, causing discontent.
89. Civilization demands the renunciation of instinctual male aggression and 
promiscuity.90. Men pay for civilization with neurosis and instinctual frustration.
91. The male “work ethic” is a sublimation of aggressive and sexual drives.
92. Ambition is often a displaced Oedipal rivalry.
93. Male humor often contains aggressive or sexual content as release.
94. Jokes allow expression of repressed sexual and hostile impulses.
95. Male fantasies of rape and domination are common but repressed.
96. Pornography appeals to male scopophilic and sadistic impulses.
97. The male ideal of beauty is based on early maternal images.
98. Love for a woman is always a substitute for the original mother-love.
99. Disillusionment in love repeats early disappointment with the mother.
100. Complete sexual maturity in men means freeing love from incestuous fixation and 
integrating affection with sensuality.

 
HOMOSEXUALITY

Here are **100 Position Statements on Homosexuality** drawn from Freud’s works, 
including *Three Essays on the Theory of Sexuality*, *Leonardo da Vinci and a Memory 
of His Childhood*, *The Psychogenesis of a Case of Homosexuality in a Woman*, *On 
the Mechanism of Paranoia*, and related correspondence.

---

### **Freud’s Positions on Homosexuality**

1. Homosexuality is not an illness, but a variation of sexual development.
2. It originates in childhood psychosexual development, not from later choice or 
corruption.
3. Constitutional factors interact with early experience to produce inversion.
4. Freud distinguished between “inversion” (object-choice) and “perversion” (aim).
5. Homosexuality is not inherently pathological; it becomes a problem only when it 
causes conflict.
6. The sexual instinct is originally polymorphous and undirected.
7. All humans are constitutionally bisexual.
8. Homosexual and heterosexual tendencies exist in everyone.
9. In some individuals, homosexual inclinations are suppressed or sublimated.
10. Repressed homosexuality can manifest as neurotic symptoms.
11. Paranoia often arises from repressed homosexual desire.
12. The formula: “I love him” becomes “I hate him” (via repression) or “He hates me” 
(via projection).
13. Homosexual feelings can be transformed into social or artistic sublimations.
14. Many cultural achievements are fueled by sublimated homosexual libido.
15. Homosexuality is more visible and accepted in certain historical periods (e.g., 
ancient Greece).
16. Social condemnation of homosexuality exacerbates psychic conflict.
17. Inversion may be a result of fixation at a narcissistic stage of development.
18. Narcissistic object-choice involves loving someone like oneself.
19. The homosexual object-choice can be a substitute for the self.
20. In some cases, the mother is overly cherished, blocking the path to other women.21. A boy’s intense attachment to the mother may lead to identification with her and 
desire for men like himself.
22. In the “negative Oedipus complex,” the boy takes a passive, feminine attitude 
toward the father.
23. Homosexual men may identify with the mother and seek the father in their partners.
24. In lesbian cases, a strong identification with the father can lead to desire for women.
25. Penis envy in women may contribute to homosexual object-choice as a rejection of 
castration.
26. The “masculinity complex” in women can underlie female homosexuality.
27. Female homosexuality is not simply the inverse of male homosexuality; it has 
distinct dynamics.
28. Homosexual women may reject the passive role assigned by heteronormative 
culture.
29. The girl’s disappointment with the father can turn her toward women.
30. Homosexuality can be a retreat from Oedipal conflict.
31. In some cases, homosexuality is a defense against incestuous desire for the 
opposite-sex parent.
32. The fear of castration in men may lead to avoidance of women and turning to men.
33. Fetishistic attachments in homosexuals may symbolize the phallus.
34. Homosexual fantasies and dreams reveal unconscious conflicts about gender and 
authority.
35. The primal scene may be interpreted differently by homosexuals—as a scene of 
submission or identification.
36. Homosexuals often have rich fantasy lives involving idealized same-sex figures.
37. Sublimation of homosexuality contributes to friendship, mentorship, and 
camaraderie.
38. Male bonding in groups is partly desexualized homosexuality.
39. Intense same-sex friendships in adolescence may have a latent homosexual 
component.
40. The latency period represses homosexual as well as heterosexual urges.
41. Puberty reactivates early homosexual feelings, which may be integrated or 
repressed.
42. Adolescence is a critical period for the consolidation of sexual orientation.
43. “Latent homosexuality” refers to unconscious same-sex desires in ostensibly 
heterosexual individuals.
44. Jealousy and rivalry among men may mask homosexual attraction.
45. Homosexual panic (acute anxiety about same-sex feelings) is a clinical 
phenomenon.
46. Religious and moral condemnation of homosexuality can produce severe neurosis.
47. Conversion therapy is harmful and unsupported by psychoanalytic understanding.
48. Homosexuality is not caused by seduction in childhood, though seduction may 
influence its expression.
49. Seduction theory was revised; Freud later emphasized fantasy and internal conflict.
50. Some homosexuals show heightened creativity due to flexibility in object-choice.
51. Leonardo da Vinci’s homosexuality was linked to his artistic sublimation and 
scientific curiosity.
52. The fantasy of the phallic mother is common in the unconscious of male 
homosexuals.
53. The homosexual man may seek the phallus in his partner, representing the lost maternal phallus.
54. The “mother’s phallus” fantasy denies castration and women’s lack.
55. In lesbianism, the fantasy of possessing the phallus may be enacted symbolically.
56. Butch/femme dynamics can reflect internalized gender identifications.
57. Homosexual object-choice is overdetermined—no single cause explains all cases.
58. Psychoanalysis can help homosexuals understand their desires, not change them.
59. The goal of analysis is not to “cure” homosexuality but to resolve associated 
conflicts.
60. Many homosexuals live healthy, productive, and emotionally fulfilling lives.
61. Social stigma can lead to internalized homophobia and self-hatred.
62. Homosexual dreams often contain symbolism of union, mirroring, or same-sex 
genitalia.
63. Dream symbols of homosexuality may be disguised as non-sexual same-sex 
activities.
64. In analysis, resistance may appear around uncovering homosexual material.
65. Transference in homosexual patients may involve same-sex erotic feelings toward 
the analyst.
66. Countertransference requires the analyst to remain neutral and non-judgmental.
67. Freud corresponded supportively with homosexual parents and professionals.
68. He advocated for decriminalization and humane treatment of homosexuals.
69. Freud’s letter to an American mother (1935) states homosexuality is “nothing to be 
ashamed of.”
70. However, Freud’s views evolved and sometimes reflected cultural biases of his time.
71. He classified homosexuality under “inversion” but did not label it a perversion in the 
pathological sense.
72. Perversion, for Freud, referred to the fixation on pregenital aims, not object-choice.
73. Some homosexual practices (e.g., anal sex) were seen as pregenital fixations.
74. But Freud did not reduce homosexuality to a single sexual act.
75. He distinguished between “absolute” inverts, “amphigenic” inverts, and “contingent” 
inverts.
76. Absolute inverts are exclusively homosexual in object-choice.
77. Amphigenic inverts are bisexual.
78. Contingent inverts turn to same-sex objects only under specific circumstances.
79. Constitutional predisposition sets the stage, but experience shapes expression.
80. The Oedipus complex is not resolved in the same way in homosexuals as in 
heterosexuals.
81. In some cases, the Oedipus complex is “negative”—desire for the same-sex parent.
82. The homosexual may identify with the opposite-sex parent and desire the same-sex 
parent.
83. This identification can be partial, leading to varied gender expression.
84. Homosexuals may display “inverted” gender traits, but not necessarily.
85. Freud rejected the idea that homosexuals are “trapped in the wrong body.”
86. Transvestism and homosexuality are distinct but may overlap.
87. Effeminacy in men and masculinity in women are not synonymous with 
homosexuality.
88. Social roles and sexual object-choice are separate dimensions.
89. Homosexual love can be as profound and “mature” as heterosexual love.
90. Jealousy, passion, and attachment operate similarly in both orientations.
91. The “family romance” fantasy in homosexuals may involve idealizing same-sex parental figures.
92. Some homosexuals repeat early parental dynamics in their relationships.
93. Fear of intimacy in homosexuals may stem from early rejection or shame.
94. The “coming out” process has psychological parallels to individuation.
95. Reparative therapy is not psychoanalytically valid.
96. Freud did not support surgical or hormonal “treatments” for homosexuality.
97. Psychoanalytic training should not discriminate against homosexual candidates.
98. Homosexuality is compatible with high functioning in work, love, and community.
99. Cultural attitudes shape the subjective experience of being homosexual.
100. Psychoanalysis continues to revise its understanding of homosexuality beyond 
Freud’s initial models.

 
OEDIPUS COMPLEX 

Here are **100 Position Statements on the Oedipus Complex** drawn from Freud’s 
works, including *The Interpretation of Dreams*, *Three Essays on the Theory of 
Sexuality*, *Totem and Taboo*, *The Ego and the Id*, *The Dissolution of the Oedipus 
Complex*, and related texts.

---

### **Freud’s Positions on the Oedipus Complex**

1. The Oedipus complex is the central organizing conflict of human psychosexual 
development.
2. It typically arises between the ages of 3 and 6 (the “phallic phase”).
3. The core dynamic: the child desires exclusive possession of the opposite-sex parent 
and views the same-sex parent as a rival.
4. In boys, this takes the form of sexual desire for the mother and hostility toward the 
father.
5. In girls, it involves desire for the father and hostility toward the mother.
6. The Oedipus complex is universal, though its expression and resolution vary.
7. It is the “nuclear complex of the neuroses”—the root of later psychological conflicts.
8. The complex emerges from the child’s evolving libidinal attachments.
9. Early attachment to the mother is initially pre-Oedipal and non-sexual.
10. The shift to Oedipal desire marks the child’s entry into object-love.
11. The father intervenes as a prohibitive figure, introducing the law (“No”).
12. This paternal prohibition is internalized as the super-ego.
13. Castration anxiety in boys forces the dissolution of the Oedipus complex.
14. The boy fears the father will castrate him as punishment for desiring the mother.
15. This fear leads to repression of incestuous wishes and identification with the father.
16. Identification with the father allows the boy to internalize paternal authority.
17. The internalized father becomes the super-ego.
18. In girls, the Oedipus complex is preceded by penis envy.
19. The girl blames the mother for her “lack” of a penis and turns to the father.
20. She desires a baby from the father as a substitute for the penis.
21. The female Oedipus complex is often not fully resolved, persisting into adulthood.
22. Girls do not experience castration anxiety but “castration realization.”23. This realization leads to resentment toward the mother and Oedipal longing for the 
father.
24. The female super-ego is less severe due to the different pathway of Oedipal 
resolution.
25. The Oedipus complex can take a “negative” form: desire for the same-sex parent 
and rivalry with the opposite-sex parent.
26. Most children experience both positive and negative Oedipal strivings.
27. Bisexual tendencies are integral to the Oedipus complex.
28. The complete Oedipus complex involves ambivalence toward both parents.
29. Unresolved Oedipal conflict can lead to neurosis in adulthood.
30. Symptoms are disguised expressions of repressed Oedipal wishes.
31. Dreams are the “royal road” to unconscious Oedipal material.
32. Dream symbols often represent Oedipal desires (e.g., kings/queens as parents).
33. The “family romance” fantasy is a later elaboration of Oedipal wishes.
34. In this fantasy, the child imagines being adopted or descended from noble parents.
35. The fantasy allows expression of hostility toward real parents and idealized longing.
36. The primal scene (witnessing parental intercourse) is interpreted through an 
Oedipal lens.
37. The child may see intercourse as an act of violence by the father.
38. This interpretation reinforces castration anxiety in boys and penis envy in girls.
39. The Oedipus complex is culturally universal but shaped by family structure.
40. In *Totem and Taboo*, Freud theorizes that the Oedipus complex originates in the 
primal horde’s parricide.
41. The myth of Oedipus (Sophocles) dramatizes the universal unconscious wish.
42. Hamlet’s hesitation reflects an unresolved Oedipus complex.
43. Religion projects the Oedipal father onto God.
44. God the Father is a sublimated representation of the ambivalent paternal figure.
45. The incest taboo is society’s defense against acting on Oedipal desires.
46. Taboos reinforce repression of incestuous wishes.
47. The super-ego’s severity corresponds to the intensity of repressed Oedipal 
aggression.
48. Guilt is the emotional residue of repressed parricidal wishes.
49. The need for punishment arises from unconscious Oedipal guilt.
50. Criminal behavior may be an attempt to provoke punishment for Oedipal sins.
51. The Oedipus complex shapes later object-choice in love.
52. Men often seek partners who resemble their mothers.
53. Women often seek partners who resemble their fathers.
54. Disappointment in love repeats early Oedipal disappointment.
55. Jealousy in relationships often re-enacts Oedipal rivalry.
56. Sibling rivalry is an extension of Oedipal dynamics.
57. The birth of a sibling can intensify Oedipal conflicts.
58. The “family triangle” is the prototype for all later triangular relationships.
59. The Oedipus complex is not merely sexual—it involves love, hate, jealousy, and 
identification.
60. It structures the child’s entry into language, law, and culture.
61. The “paternal metaphor” (Lacan) refers to the father’s symbolic function as 
prohibitor.
62. The Oedipus complex mediates between nature and culture.
63. Failure to resolve the Oedipus complex can lead to psychosis.64. In psychosis, the symbolic father is “foreclosed,” leading to a break with reality.
65. Perversions represent a disavowal of castration and a refusal of Oedipal law.
66. Fetishism denies maternal castration, preserving the fantasy of the phallic mother.
67. Homosexuality can be an outcome of a “negative” Oedipus complex.
68. The homosexual boy may identify with the mother and desire the father.
69. The homosexual girl may identify with the father and desire the mother.
70. The latency period follows the dissolution of the Oedipus complex.
71. Latency involves repression of Oedipal desires and sublimation into learning and 
play.
72. Puberty reawakens Oedipal strivings, which must be redirected outside the family.
73. Adolescence involves a second Oedipal crisis.
74. Successful adolescence requires detachment from parental objects.
75. The Oedipus complex can be revisited in adulthood during analysis.
76. Transference in analysis re-activates Oedipal feelings toward the analyst.
77. The analyst occupies the position of the Oedipal parent in the transference.
78. Working through the transference helps resolve residual Oedipal conflicts.
79. Resistance in analysis often defends against uncovering Oedipal material.
80. Dreams of the analyst may contain Oedipal symbolism.
81. The Oedipus complex is not limited to Western nuclear families.
82. Variations in family structure (e.g., single-parent, same-sex parents) modify but do 
not eliminate Oedipal dynamics.
83. The complex operates in all cultures because it arises from the triangulation of 
desire.
84. Myth, folklore, and literature universally reflect Oedipal themes.
85. The hero’s journey often involves overcoming a paternal figure and winning a 
maternal figure.
86. Tragedies revolve around forbidden desires and familial conflict.
87. Comedy often resolves Oedipal tensions through marriage and reconciliation.
88. Art and creativity are sublimations of Oedipal energy.
89. Intellectual curiosity originates in the child’s sexual researches during the Oedipal 
phase.
90. The “riddle of the Sphinx” symbolizes the child’s questions about sex and origins.
91. The child’s theory of “penis birth” is an Oedipal-sexual fantasy.
92. The fantasy of seduction by a parent is a common Oedipal distortion.
93. Actual seduction (abuse) complicates but does not replace the Oedipal dynamic.
94. The Oedipus complex is fundamentally a psychic structure, not a historical event.
95. It is constructed through fantasy, not memory.
96. The complex is timeless in the unconscious—it does not fade with age.
97. Aging parents can rekindle Oedipal feelings in adult children.
98. The death of a parent often brings unresolved Oedipal conflicts to the surface.
99. Mourning a parent involves working through Oedipal attachment and ambivalence.
100. The legacy of the Oedipus complex is the structure of desire, guilt, and identity in 
every human subject.


SUBLIMATION 

Here are **100 Position Statements on Sublimation** drawn from Freud’s works, 
including *Three Essays on the Theory of Sexuality*, *Civilization and Its Discontents*, *Leonardo da Vinci and a Memory of His Childhood*, *The Ego and the Id*, and related 
texts.

---

### **Freud’s Positions on Sublimation**

1. Sublimation is the process by which sexual or aggressive drives are redirected 
toward non‑sexual, socially valued aims.
2. It is a defense mechanism, but one considered healthy and adaptive.
3. Sublimation transforms libidinal energy (Eros) into cultural, intellectual, or artistic 
achievements.
4. It is a key mechanism in the development of civilization.
5. Sublimation reduces internal conflict by channeling unacceptable impulses into 
acceptable outlets.
6. The energy used in sublimation comes from the id.
7. The ego mediates sublimation, serving as the executive function that redirects drive 
energy.
8. Sublimation does not require repression; the original impulse is acknowledged but 
transformed.
9. Unlike repression, sublimation reduces neurotic tension and increases psychological 
flexibility.
10. Sublimation is essential for normal psychological development.
11. It is a sign of psychosexual maturity.
12. The capacity to sublimate varies among individuals.
13. Artists, scientists, and intellectuals are often highly skilled at sublimation.
14. Leonardo da Vinci is Freud’s prime example of successful sublimation of 
homosexual libido into artistic and scientific curiosity.
15. Sublimation explains the “neutralization” of aggressive and sexual drives into 
productive work.
16. It is a form of desexualization—the aim and object of the drive change, but the 
energy remains.
17. The original object‑cathexis is replaced by a non‑sexual cathexis.
18. Sublimation is a compromise between the pleasure principle and the reality 
principle.
19. It allows partial discharge of drive energy while adhering to social norms.
20. Neurotics have a diminished capacity for sublimation; their energy remains bound 
in conflict.
21. Perversions represent a failure of sublimation—the drive remains fixated on its 
original aim and object.
22. Sublimation is a key factor in the development of character traits.
23. Kindness, altruism, and morality may arise from sublimated sadistic or aggressive 
drives.
24. The “anal character” (orderliness, parsimony, obstinacy) results from sublimation of 
anal‑erotic impulses.
25. The “oral character” (dependency, optimism/pessimism) stems from sublimated oral 
impulses.
26. The “phallic character” (vanity, pride) arises from sublimated phallic narcissism.
27. Sublimation can be partial—some energy remains attached to the original object.28. Complete sublimation is rare; most activities retain traces of their instinctual origins.
29. Humor is a form of sublimated aggression.
30. Jokes allow the expression of hostile or sexual impulses in a socially acceptable 
form.
31. Play and games in children are early forms of sublimation.
32. Education encourages sublimation by redirecting curiosity (sexual researches) into 
learning.
33. Intellectual curiosity is sublimated scopophilia (the urge to look).
34. Scientific research is sublimated infantile sexual curiosity.
35. Artistic creation is sublimated exhibitionism and voyeurism.
36. Music and dance may subliminate rhythmic bodily pleasures.
37. Religious devotion can be a sublimation of infantile longing for the father.
38. Altruistic love is sublimated erotic love.
39. Patriotism is sublimated group narcissism.
40. Moral strictness is sublimated sadism turned inward.
41. The super‑ego can demand sublimation as a form of moral ideal.
42. Sublimation is necessary for the development of a cohesive ego‑ideal.
43. Civilization depends on the collective sublimation of individual instincts.
44. Social institutions (law, education, religion) organize and direct sublimated energies.
45. Without sublimation, civilization would be overwhelmed by aggressive and sexual 
drives.
46. Sublimation is a source of cultural progress but also of individual discontent.
47. The “discontent” in civilization arises from the instinctual renunciation required for 
sublimation.
48. The more civilization demands sublimation, the more neurosis increases.
49. Sublimation is not without cost—it can lead to emotional dryness or 
  over‑intellectualization.
50. The artist pays for sublimation with personal suffering or eccentricity.
51. Sublimation can be unstable; under stress, desublimation may occur.
52. Desublimation is the return of sublimated energy to its original instinctual aim.
53. War, riots, and social breakdown are mass desublimation.
54. Love relationships can involve temporary desublimation of tender feelings into 
sexual passion.
55. Sublimation is more easily achieved in some drives than others.
56. Aggressive drives are harder to sublimate than sexual drives.
57. Sublimation of aggression often takes the form of competitive sports, debate, or 
ambition.
58. Sadistic impulses may be sublimated into surgical professions or law enforcement.
59. Masochistic impulses may be sublimated into asceticism or endurance sports.
60. Sublimation is not inherently virtuous—it can serve destructive ideologies (e.g., 
militarism).
61. The direction of sublimation depends on cultural values and ideals.
62. Sublimation is taught and encouraged through parenting and education.
63. The Oedipus complex resolution is a major catalyst for sublimation.
64. After the Oedipus complex is dissolved, libidinal energy is freed for sublimation.
65. Sublimation is a lifelong process, not confined to childhood.
66. Midlife crises may reflect a breakdown of earlier sublimations.
67. Aging may diminish the capacity for sublimation, leading to irritability or rigidity.
68. Sublimation is reversible in psychoanalysis—through interpretation, the original instinctual roots can be uncovered.
69. Analysis aims not to undo sublimation, but to make its operation more flexible.
70. Blocked sublimation can lead to symptom formation.
71. Writers’ block or creative inhibition may stem from conflicts around sublimation.
72. Sublimation requires a degree of ego strength and integrative capacity.
73. Psychotics have impaired sublimation ability due to ego fragmentation.
74. Borderline individuals may show erratic sublimation—alternating between high 
creativity and impulsive acting‑out.
75. Trauma can disrupt sublimation, fixing energy to the traumatic event.
76. Mourning involves the slow sublimation of libido attached to the lost object.
77. After mourning, the energy invested in the deceased is redirected into new activities.
78. Sublimation is a key mechanism in psychotherapy—turning insight into constructive 
change.
79. The therapeutic relationship itself can be a sublimated expression of transference 
love.
80. Freud saw psychoanalysis as a science that sublimates the curiosity of the primal 
scene.
81. Dream interpretation is a sublimated form of sexual and aggressive fantasy 
analysis.
82. Symbolism in dreams and art is the language of sublimation.
83. Condensation and displacement in dreams are primitive forms of what becomes 
sublimation in waking life.
84. Sublimation is a higher‑order process compared to other defenses like repression or 
denial.
85. It is the only defense mechanism that directly enriches the ego and society.
86. Sublimation increases self‑esteem by aligning the individual with cultural ideals.
87. The ego‑ideal is built from sublimated identifications with admired figures.
88. Sublimation is necessary for the development of a coherent identity.
89. Gender identity may involve sublimation of bisexual tendencies into socially 
sanctioned roles.
90. Sublimation is not the same as reaction formation—the latter involves behaving 
opposite to the impulse, while sublimation transforms it.
91. Sublimation preserves the energy of the impulse; reaction formation consumes 
energy in suppression.
92. Sublimation can be collective, as seen in cultural rituals, festivals, and artistic 
movements.
93. National myths and heroes are collective sublimations of ancestral aggression and 
idealization.
94. Technology and invention are sublimations of the human drive for mastery and 
control.
95. Environmental activism may be a sublimation of nurturing or aggressive drives.
96. Sublimation is limited by biology—there is only a finite amount of libidinal energy 
available.
97. Excessive sublimation can lead to emotional depletion or “burnout.”
98. Optimal mental health involves a balance between direct gratification and 
sublimation.
99. Sublimation is a uniquely human capacity, arising from the prolonged dependency 
and educability of the human child.
100. The future of civilization depends on the successful sublimation of destructive instincts into creative and cooperative endeavors.

OBSESSIONAL NEUROSIS (OCD)

Here are **100 Position Statements on Obsessional Neurosis** (Freud’s formulation of 
OCD) drawn from his case studies, especially *Notes Upon a Case of Obsessional 
Neurosis* (the “Rat Man”), *Inhibitions, Symptoms and Anxiety*, *Totem and Taboo*, 
*The Problem of Anxiety*, and related works.

---

### **Freud’s Positions on Obsessional Neurosis (OCD)**

1. Obsessional neurosis arises from unresolved conflicts in the anal‑sadistic stage of 
psychosexual development.
2. It is characterized by intrusive thoughts (obsessions) and repetitive behaviors 
(compulsions).
3. Obsessions are disguised expressions of repressed aggressive or sexual impulses.
4. Compulsions are acts designed to neutralize anxiety or “undo” the forbidden impulse.
5. The primary conflict in obsessional neurosis is between sadistic urges and reactive 
love.
6. Ambivalence—simultaneous love and hate toward the same object—is central to the 
disorder.
7. Obsessional patients often exhibit extreme doubt, indecision, and “mental rumination.
”
8. The obsessional’s super‑ego is exceptionally harsh and punitive.
9. Guilt is pervasive, often unconscious, and drives the compulsive rituals.
10. Obsessional symptoms serve as a defense against awareness of repressed 
patricidal or incestuous wishes.
11. The Rat Man’s case illustrated the connection between obsessions and repressed 
sadistic fantasies.
12. The rat torture fantasy symbolized both anal eroticism and fear of paternal 
punishment.
13. Obsessional thoughts are often transformations of aggressive impulses through 
reaction formation.
14. Reaction formation leads to exaggerated politeness, cleanliness, or morality.
15. The obsessional’s “isolation of affect” separates distressing ideas from their 
emotional charge.
16. Intellectualization is used to avoid feeling the full impact of unconscious conflicts.
17. Magical thinking—the belief that thoughts can influence reality—is prevalent.
18. Compulsive rituals are private ceremonies with symbolic meaning.
19. Rituals often involve counting, checking, washing, or repeating actions.
20. Washing compulsions may represent a desire to cleanse oneself of guilty (sexual or 
aggressive) thoughts.
21. Checking rituals often relate to unconscious fears of having caused harm.
22. Doubt reflects a conflict between the ego and the super‑ego’s impossible demands.
23. Obsessional neurosis can be understood as a “private religion” with strict rules and 
penances.
24. The disorder typically begins in late childhood or adolescence.25. It is more common in individuals with high intelligence and strong moral sensitivity.
26. Obsessional patients often have a history of strict, religious, or moralistic upbringing.
27. Early sexual experiences or aggressive fantasies are repressed and return in 
symptomatic form.
28. The Oedipus complex is central—obsessions often relate to forbidden sexual or 
hostile wishes toward parents.
29. Fear of paternal retaliation (castration anxiety) is transformed into fear of external 
punishment or disaster.
30. The obsession “I might kill my father” disguises the wish “I want to kill my father.”
31. Compulsive rituals are attempts to ward off the feared punishment.
32. The “undoing” mechanism involves performing an action that symbolically cancels 
out a hostile thought.
33. For example, touching something “bad” may be followed by touching something 
“good” to neutralize it.
34. Obsessional neurosis is a regression to the anal‑sadistic phase under pressure from 
Oedipal conflicts.
35. Anal eroticism is sublimated into obsessional traits: orderliness, parsimony, 
stubbornness.
36. Money often becomes a symbolic equivalent of feces in obsessional thinking.
37. Dirt and contamination fears relate to anal disgust displaced onto moral or physical 
“filth.”
38. Time becomes a source of anxiety—rituals are often bound to specific times or 
sequences.
39. Symmetry and exactness reflect a need to control chaotic unconscious impulses.
40. Obsessional patients are often preoccupied with death, disease, or moral impurity.
41. Religious scrupulosity—excessive concern with sin and virtue—is common.
42. The obsessional’s super‑ego is modeled on a harsh, punitive father‑figure.
43. The ego is caught between the id’s aggressive impulses and the super‑ego’s cruel 
demands.
44. Obsessional symptoms are compromises: they allow partial expression of the 
forbidden wish while punishing it.
45. The punishment is built into the symptom itself—the suffering of the ritual.
46. Obsessional thinking is over‑determined; each symptom has multiple unconscious 
meanings.
47. Dreams in obsessional patients often contain symbols of punishment, dirt, or 
forbidden acts.
48. Transference in analysis is intense and ambivalent—the analyst becomes both 
loved protector and feared punisher.
49. Resistance in obsessional patients takes the form of intellectualization, doubt, and 
“yes, but...” reasoning.
50. The patient may use free association to evade rather than reveal unconscious 
material.
51. Obsessional neurosis is distinct from hysteria—the latter converts conflict into 
physical symptoms, the former into mental rituals.
52. It is also distinct from psychosis—reality testing remains intact in obsessionals.
53. Obsessional patients often have insight into the irrationality of their symptoms but 
feel compelled to perform them.
54. The “obsessional character” may exist without full‑blown neurosis: orderliness, 
rigidity, perfectionism.55. Such character traits are sublimations of anal‑sadistic drives.
56. Obsessional neurosis can be chronic, with periods of exacerbation and remission.
57. Stress, loss, or moral dilemmas can trigger flare‑ups.
58. Marriage or sexual intimacy may provoke symptoms due to unresolved incestuous 
guilt.
59. The death of a parent can unleash repressed patricidal wishes and severe 
obsessions.
60. Freud linked obsessional rituals to prehistoric ritual practices in *Totem and Taboo*.
61. Taboo prohibitions and ritual observances in “primitive” societies resemble 
individual obsessions.
62. Both serve to manage ambivalence toward sacred/prohibited objects (e.g., the 
totem animal as father‑substitute).
63. The “omnipotence of thoughts” — the belief that thinking something makes it 
happen — is shared by obsessionals and “primitive” magic.
64. Children also exhibit magical thinking, which persists in the obsessional 
unconscious.
65. Obsessional patients fear their own hostile thoughts will cause real harm.
66. This fear leads to “thought‑action fusion”—confusing a thought with a deed.
67. Compulsions are attempts to prevent the imagined harm from occurring.
68. The content of obsessions often reflects the patient’s moral values—the more taboo 
the thought, the more distressing.
69. A devout person may have blasphemous obsessions; a gentle person may have 
violent obsessions.
70. The ego’s defense is to turn the aggressive impulse against the self—hence 
self‑punishing rituals.
71. Masochism is a component of obsessional neurosis—suffering relieves guilt.
72. Obsessional patients may provoke punishment in relationships to alleviate 
unconscious guilt.
73. The need for symmetry or “just right” feelings reflects a deeper need for psychic 
balance.
74. Incomplete rituals cause severe anxiety because they fail to neutralize the 
forbidden impulse.
75. Obsessional symptoms can become so time‑consuming they impair functioning.
76. Freud noted that obsessionals often delay seeking treatment due to ambivalence.
77. Treatment requires uncovering the childhood conflicts behind the symptoms.
78. Analysis aims to make the unconscious meaning of rituals conscious, reducing their 
magical power.
79. As insight grows, the ego gains strength against the super‑ego’s harsh demands.
80. However, obsessional neurosis is often resistant to treatment because the patient 
“clings to the illness.”
81. The secondary gain of illness—protection from life’s demands—can be strong.
82. Obsessional patients may use therapy as another ritual to avoid real change.
83. Freud cautioned against a “wild” interpretation of symbols—understanding must be 
built gradually.
84. The Rat Man’s recovery involved working through his ambivalence toward his 
father and his fiancée.
85. Freud saw obsessional neurosis as a “dialect” of the universal language of the 
unconscious.
86. It represents a “regression to omnipotence of thought” when faced with insoluble conflict.
87. Obsessional thinking is hyper‑rational as a defense against irrational impulses.
88. The disorder reflects a struggle between Eros (love) and Thanatos (aggression).
89. In severe cases, obsession can merge with paranoia—fears of being watched or 
punished.
90. Freud distinguished between “true” obsessions and “obsessional phobias” (e.g., 
fear of contamination).
91. Phobias involve avoidance; obsessions involve repetitive mental or behavioral 
rituals.
92. Obsessional neurosis can co‑exist with other diagnoses (e.g., depression, hysteria).
93. It is more common in men, according to Freud’s clinical observations.
94. The pre‑disposition may be constitutional—an innate intensity of anal‑erotic and 
sadistic drives.
95. However, environment (parental strictness, moral education) shapes its expression.
96. Obsessional symptoms can be understood as a private mythology defending 
against Oedipal guilt.
97. With successful treatment, symptoms may diminish, but character traits often 
remain.
98. Freud believed that some level of obsessionality is normal—it becomes 
pathological only when it causes suffering or dysfunction.
99. Civilization itself relies on collective “obsessional” rituals and prohibitions to 
manage aggression.
100. Obsessional neurosis is, in essence, a battle between the individual’s murderous 
wishes and his conscience, fought on the battlefield of ritual and thought.


SEXUALITY IN PEOPLE WITH OCD 

Here are **100 Position Statements on Sexuality in People with OCD** based on 
Freud’s theories of obsessional neurosis, defense mechanisms, unconscious conflict, 
and psychosexual development.

---

### **Sexuality in Obsessional Neurosis (OCD): Freudian Positions**

1. Obsessional neurosis is rooted in conflicts from the **anal‑sadistic stage** of 
psychosexual development.
2. Sexual and aggressive drives are fused in the unconscious of obsessionals.
3. Obsessions often represent **disguised sexual wishes** that have been repressed.
4. Sexual thoughts become **ego‑dystonic**—experienced as intrusive and 
unacceptable.
5. The obsessional’s super‑ego is especially harsh regarding sexual impulses.
6. **Ambivalence**—simultaneous sexual desire and disgust—is central.
7. Sexual obsessions often focus on **taboo** themes: incest, pedophilia, bestiality.
8. These taboo themes reflect unresolved **Oedipal conflicts**.
9. The thought “I might be sexually attracted to my parent” is a defense against the 
repressed wish “I am sexually attracted to my parent.”
10. Obsessive doubts about sexual orientation often mask earlier homosexual urges or Oedipal confusion.
11. Compulsive rituals may be designed to **undo** or **neutralize** unacceptable 
sexual thoughts.
12. **Washing compulsions** can symbolize a need to cleanse oneself of “dirty” sexual 
thoughts.
13. Sexual guilt is often displaced onto concerns about contamination, disease, or 
moral impurity.
14. The obsessional’s **reaction formation** leads to exaggerated purity, chastity, or 
prudishness.
15. Some obsessionals avoid sex entirely due to unconscious incestuous guilt.
16. Others engage in sex but with rigid, ritualized behaviors that drain it of spontaneity.
17. **Magical thinking** extends to sexuality: “If I have this sexual thought, I will 
become a monster.”
18. Obsessionals may believe that sexual thoughts can cause harm to others.
19. **Fear of loss of control** during sexual arousal is common.
20. Sexual arousal itself may trigger anxiety because it signals loss of rational control.
21. The obsessional’s ego attempts to **intellectualize** or **isolate** sexual feelings.
22. Sexual desire is split off from emotion—experienced as a mechanical urge.
23. Partners may be treated as objects in a compulsive ritual rather than loved subjects.
24. **Doubt** extends to sexuality: “Do I really love my partner?” “Was that arousal 
normal?”
25. Obsessionals may repetitively seek reassurance about their sexual adequacy or 
normality.
26. Sexual performance can become a compulsive “checking” ritual.
27. **Scrupulosity** about sexual morality may lead to confession rituals (real or mental)
.
28. In religious obsessionals, sexual thoughts are equated with sin and require 
penance.
