85. The analyst becomes a surrogate for the super-ego during treatment.
86. Resistance in therapy is the ego’s defense against uncovering repressed material.87. Working through helps integrate repressed material into the ego.
88. Childhood experiences shape the structure of the super-ego.
89. The super-ego can be influenced by cultural and societal norms.
90. The ego must negotiate between individual desires and social constraints.
91. The id’s demands are insistent and timeless.
92. The ego delays gratification in service of reality.
93. The super-ego can cause moral anxiety even when no actual wrongdoing occurs.
94. The ego may develop false self-representations to please the super-ego.
95. Narcissism involves libidinal investment in the ego.
96. Healthy development involves balancing narcissistic and object libido.
97. The ego can use humor to discharge tension.
98. Art and creativity are outcomes of sublimation.
99. Religion may be seen as a collective neurosis rooted in super-ego demands.
100. Civilization imposes restrictions on instinctual drives, leading to inherent 
discontent.

OCD and Philosophy
Obsessive-compulsives and philosophers have a lot in common. First if all, they are 
both compulsive doubters. They doubt the obvious. They rationalize their doing so by 
saying that they are trying to be rigorous and that they are trying not to believe things 
that are false. But that is, indeed, a mere rationalization. Both the obsessive-
compulsive and the philosopher are impotent, being too fearful to engage the world. 
And to the extent that they have potency, it lies in their ability to manipulate linguistic or 
otherwise symbolic proxies of reality. So instead of dealing with the world on its own 
terms---instead of dealing with the world in the world—they try to internalize as much of 
the world as possible into the sphere of their own thought, where they are dominant. 
And they are dominant there both because they are more inclined towards thought than 
towards action and also, more importantly, because it is their own mind where, of 
course, they ipso facto have total power.
Or at least the illusion thereof—more on this in a moment.
Then there is the fact that both the philosopher and the obsessive-compulsive are 
unduly preoccupied with logical niceties and fine points of argumentation. This is at 
least partly a derivative of their sense of impotence: when one has no material of one’s own to work on— which is the case whenever one is too afraid of the world to venture 
out into it and get material--- one preoccupies oneself with secondary material—with 
matters of grammar, logic, argumentation and the like.
Plus, by conditionalizing the acceptance of hypotheses on subtleties of proof and 
argumentation, they are able to rationalize continuing to remain in their own heads; 
they are able to rationalize commandeering debates, and holing them up within their 
own thought-scape, pending the resolution of niceties of logic and dialectical protocol 
which, of their very nature, are incapable of being definitely resolved.
There is another, somewhat less invidious dimension to the just-discussed similarity 
between the philosopher and the obsessive-compulsive. Neither the philosopher nor 
the obsessive-compulsive has any real doubts, even of a purely intellectual variety, as 
to the contents of the external world. In any case, neither has any more such doubts 
than the next person. But both are indeed more doubtful than the next person. They are 
more doubtful, and with good reason, as to the contents of the internal world. With the 
both the philosopher and the obsessive- compulsive, their supposed doubts about the 
external world are displaced, projected doubts about the internal world. They are both 
people whose minds are veritable caldrons of primal desires but who, being moral, do 
their best to suppress and repress their basal urges. The result is that, while their 
understanding of the external world is in fact perfectly good, notwithstanding their 
asseverations to their contrary, they are quite in the dark as to the internal world, 
believing or half-believing themselves to be logic-driven, Cartesian ratiocinators, when, 
in fact, their Cartesianism is only a veneer, which holds only of the thinnest and most 
uppermost mental stratum of theirs, the rest of their respective psyches being 
Nietzschean cesspools.
And both of these types of people fancy themselves ‘rational actors’—people who have 
gotten the better of the base side of themselves---when in reality they are anything but 
rational actors, the truth being that they are driven by rage, narcissism and fear. Indeed,
 their intellectualism is a reaction to, and attempt to cloak their own darkly retrograde 
psychological condition.
And their insistence on the rationality of man is merely an idealization—a projection of 
a self-image that they half-have and are 100% trying to have. And the Lockean/
Cartesian
 
insistence on the non-existence of the unconscious—on the brazenly spurious idea that 
mind extends only as far consciousness---is merely another tendentious rationalization, 
and a decidedly more sterile and superficial one than their false, but not entirely 
misconceived insistence on the primacy within people of rationality.
There is a second similarity between the obsessive-compulsive and the philosopher. 
They are both pathologically concerned with ethics—with questions of right and wrong 
and good vs. evil. In both cases, this is because they are crypto-criminals—people 
whose fundamental orientation with respect to others is anti-social—this being reflected 
in their solipsism and skepticism---but who also have strong, nay draconian, superegos 
and thus do everything in their power to suppress their criminal side. And to this end 
they enlist their pre-existing penchant for dwelling on technicalities of logic; which, 
when transferred from the sphere of knowledge-theory into the domain of ethics, results 
in their dwelling on technicalities of a legalistic nature. And this is why many an 
  obsessive-compulsive, and also many a philosopher, goes into law.
Philosophers tend to be extremely sensitive to, and intelligent about, niceties of 
language.This is obviously true of some obsessive-compulsives, but it is unknown whether it is 
categorically true of them. But supposing that it is, this, I think, is a derivative of their 
fear-based retreat from the external world, which is the realm of action, into the internal 
world, which is the realm of thought. After all, sensitivity to linguistic niceties being cut 
from much the same cloth as sensitivity to logical niceties, and the latter being, as we 
have already seen, a by-product of the philosopher’s and the obsessive-compulsives 
replacing of reality with cognitive proxies thereof.
One last point: I have noticed that a hugely disproportionate number of philosophers, 
including myself, are professional level musicians. Around 40% of philosophy 
professors fall into this category. I don’t know whether the same is true of the class of 
obsessive compulsives— it presumably isn’t. But even though there is not, in all 
likelihood, a categorical connection between OCD and musicality, there may be a sub-
categorical connection, as in, there may be a relatively comprehensive sub-set of the 
class of obsessive-compulsives whose members are also musical and whose being 
musical is connected to their OCD. In any case, it seems that musicianship and 
philosophical ability are related in that both involve decrypting and then re- encrypting 
information; they are both about organizing information—and doing so with obsessional 
concern for the logical interrelations holding among bits of information organized, and a 
proportionate disregard for the correspondence of that information to external reality.
 
What are Some Characteristics of OCD in Children?

A listener of mine, Emma, asked me: What are some early signs of OCD in children?
Here is my answer: First of all, all children are OCD. In fact, OCD is actually a 
prolongation into adulthood of the child's 'binary' emotional relation to the world. When 
you send a child to camp, he kicks and screams and cries. Then he has to come home, 
he cries again. Also, children, much like obsessive-compulsives, are soothed by 
repetition and like familiarity. They like to be told the same story at bedtime, night after 
night, and sleep with the same stuffed animal. That said, children who are actually 
OCD--i.e. who will be OCD as adults--do have some distinctinve characteristics. The 
main difference between them and non-OCD children is that they tend to have rituals 
and peculiarities that are specific to them. All children like to sleep with same stuffed 
animal night after and be told the same story night after night. But only OCD children 
have to sleep with a glass of water next to the bed or have to put their left shoe on 
before their first. Also, OCD children tend to be more cholicy and more emotionally 
intense and extreme than others children---more prone to bouts of depressive 
withdrawal. But I stress that OCD is never itself the issue, and OCD is not eliminated by 
being confronted head-on. OCD is about withdrawal from a frightening world into a safe 
space of thought and ritual, and the only way to conquer OCD is to conquer fear. Which 
one does by developing strengths of character and intellect. OCD is given rise to by 
emotional vulnerability, which in turn is given rise to by actual vulnerability--by 
defenselessness. And the way to defeat OCD is to give proper, non- neurotic defenses 
to the person in question.
 
The Failure of Political Philosophy to Engage Reality
I wrote an audio-article in which I said that both philosophy—specifically, philosophical 
skepticism---and OCD were about retreating from the exigencies of external actual 
reality into the safety of internal pseudo-reality. And in keeping with this, I said that both 
OCD and philosophy are intellectualized impotence.In any case, a listener of mine who goes by the name of L and H Productions, made 
the following point in response to my article. He said: “What about political 
philosophers? Don’t they engage the world? Aren’t they a counter-example to your 
point, that philosophy is about autistic non-engagement?”
Yes and no. Mostly no. Consider Plato, as he is a paradigm case of political 
philosophers. Plato’s system was completely a priori. Yes—he was talking about how 
to reform the world. But really, that isn’t what he was doing. He really talking about how 
to replace the world with a world of his own—a world that had little reality, and little 
justification, outside of his own thought-space. So Plato was really talking about 
replacing the actual world his thought-world.
He wasn’t in any real way engaging the actual world.
And the absurdity of what Plato was doing comes out in a lot of ways. In, for example, 
the rigidity of the society that he was proposing; in the stereotyped—and in that respect 
decidedly OCD---protocols governing both education and, more generally, interactions 
between different people; and, moreover, in the absurd idea, put forth in this dialogue 
The Laws, that even the most minor violations of law should be punished by death; and,
 finally, in the incoherent idea that society should be ruled by a philosopher 
king—incoherent, because societies are to be run by sapient doers, not sapient 
thinkers; societies are to be run by people who do not flee from the world into 
intellectual constructions and, therefore, are not to be run by philosophers, granting, of 
course, that they are to be run by people who are philosopher-like to the extent, but 
only to the extent, that they are intelligent.
Or consider Marx, another textbook case of a philosophical philosopher. Yes—he was 
talking about how to change the world. But really, he was talking, quite explicitly, about 
destroying the existing social structure and replacing it with one that had no real basis---
other than a totally fabricated one	in reality, and which, when it was implemented, led 
to loss of life
and opportunity on an unprecedented scale.
Now, not all political philosophers are as guilty as Marx and Plato of failing to engage 
actual political realities. Some political philosophers do not simply propose that existing 
political realities be replaced with their childish a priori fantasies.
For example, John Locke made a number of extremely reasonable points about law 
and economics that, when implemented, were enormously successful. And much the 
same is true, though in some respects to an even greater degree, of Adam Smith and 
David Hume, who, along with Locke, created the discipline of modern free-market 
economics.
But Adam Smith was really an ex-philosopher who left the discipline of philosopher in 
order to start a new discipline. And Locke and Hume were hard-core empiricists: their 
whole agenda, as philosophers, was to empiricize philosophy. Their criticism of existing 
philosophy was that it failed to engage the world, and their objective was to reinvent the 
discipline in such a way that it did engage the world.
So, yes, their political philosophies—which were very good	did engage the world; but
to the extent that they did, they were almost anti-philosophical political philosophies.
That said, political theory can engage the world, and that does represent a limitation on
 
the scope of my point about philosophy categorically being in the nature of an autistic 
withdrawal from reality.
But there is one last point to make, which does to some extent buttress my conception 
of philosophy as autistic retreat. Hume and Locke were arch-empiricists, meaning that, in their view, you don’t know it if you don’t see it (or otherwise have direct sensory 
evidence of it).
Now, empiricism collapses into the view that we can of nothing except our own 
conscious states; for the empiricist rejects all rules of inference would enable us to 
posit the existence of anything-
--the empiricist says that we can know it only if it is a datum of consciousness and, 
therefore, that if we have to posit it, meaning infer its existence, then it doesn’t exist.
So in their attempt to make philosophy engage the world, Locke and Hume empiricized 
philosophy. But in empiricizing philosophy, Locke and Hume made philosophy collapse 
into pure skepticism, pure skepticism being the most compete conceivable autistic 
withdrawal from reality into one’s own mind. And what this shows is that, even when 
philosophers try to make philosophy not be an autistic retreat, it ends up being 
precisely that. Which does rather confirm my conception of philosophy as autistic 
retreat.
 
How to Get Rid of OCD


A listener of mine asked me the following question: Is there an effective treatment that 
can eliminate OCD for good?

Here is my answer:
There is only one way to 'get rid' of OCD, and that is to find a calling in life in which 
one's obsessive compulsive tendencies are an asset, as opposed to a liability. OCD is 
not so much an illness as it is a distinctive way of processing emotional information. In 
the wrong environment, that psychological architecture can hold one back; in right one, 
it can push one forward. Many writers, composers, mathematicians, lawyers, 
philosophers and creative and analytical people of many stripes (e.g. Beethoven, 
Einstein, Dr. Johnson) have OCD; such people have found callings in which their OCD 
works for them. So it's not about eliminating it; it's about putting it to one's advantage. I 
myself have done that in a very big way. I am being OCD right now, in writing such a 
thorough answer, but in being so thorough in this context, I am putting to my obsessive-
compulsive tendencies to non-pathological use, instead of letting them express 
themselves as OCD. And that is point: one doesn't get rid of mental illnesses by getting 
rid of them; one chooses to work in areas in which those mental illnesses are functional 
and, therefore, not mental illnesses
 
What are Some Characteristics of OCD in Children?
A listener of mine, Emma, asked me: What are some early signs of OCD in children?
Here is my answer: First of all, all children are OCD. In fact, OCD is actually a 
prolongation into adulthood of the child's 'binary' emotional relation to the world. When 
you send a child to camp, he kicks and screams and cries. Then he has to come home, 
he cries again. Also, children, much like obsessive-compulsives, are soothed by 
repetition and like familiarity. They like to be told the same story at bedtime, night after 
night, and sleep with the same stuffed animal. That said, children who are actually 
OCD--i.e. who will be OCD as adults--do have some distinctinve characteristics. The 
main difference between them and non-OCD children is that they tend to have rituals 
and peculiarities that are specific to them. All children like to sleep with same stuffed 
animal night after and be told the same story night after night. But only OCD children have to sleep with a glass of water next to the bed or have to put their left shoe on 
before their first. Also, OCD children tend to be more cholicy and more emotionally 
intense and extreme than others children---more prone to bouts of depressive 
withdrawl. But I stress that OCD is never itself the issue, and OCD is not eliminated by 
being confronted head-on. OCD is about withdrawl from a frightening world into a safe 
space of thought and ritual, and the only way to conquer OCD is to conquer fear. Which 
one does by developing strengths of character and intellect. OCD is given rise to by 
emotional vulnerability, which in turn is given rise to by actual vulnerability--by 
defenselessness. And the way to defeat OCD is to give proper, non- neurotic defenses 
to the person in question.
 
OCD: The Philosopher’s Illness


A philosopher is somebody who thinks but does not act. He is someone who withdraws 
into his own mind. Philosophical skepticism is simply an excuse for the philosopher to 
remain holed up in his own mind.
Skepticism is the view that we cannot know to what extent, if any, our sense- 
perceptions are accurate.
The skeptic’s position collapses into the absurdity it is no less rational to believe that 
our sense-perceptions are uncaused and therefore inexplicable than it is to believe that 
they are caused and therefore explicable.
So the fact that people are drawn to skepticism cannot be understood in terms its 
merits, given that it has none, and is to be understood in terms of its emotional appeal.
	And its emotional appeal is that it allows people to crawl into a safe space inside their 
heads. It allows them to deny the reality of threats with which the external world is 
teeming.
A consequence is that the skeptic doesn’t have to act. If there is no external reality, and 
there are no external threats, then it isn’t necessary to do anything. If all there is to life 
is what is inside the impregnable fortress of one’s own consciousness, then there is 
nothing to do: all there is to do is watch.
But there is a big problem with skeptic’s retreat from reality into thought. Thought 
inevitably refers one back to the external world. And the only way to prevent it from 
doing as much as to stop engaging in it. So if the skeptic is to remain in his safe haven 
of pure consciousness, he must keep retreating: it is not enough for him to hole himself 
up inside his consciousness. For if he is holed up there but he lets himself think then he 
will start chipping away at the walls of his self-isolation. So he must put himself within a 
fortress inside that fortress, and so on. Which means that he has to paralyze his own 
thought-processes, since, when left to follow their own course, they push him back into 
the external world.
Notice that skeptic’s main weapon—his main way of preserving his aloofness from 
reality—is doubt. And this is the main weapon of the obsessive-compulsive. Whenever 
it comes time to act, the obsessive compulsive is afflicted by action-crippling doubt.
He consciously experiences this doubt as unpleasant. But that is neither here nor there, 
since it has no effect on the effect of that doubt, which is to keep the obsessive-
compulsive inside his safe haven of thought—of thought which doesn’t engage the 
world and eventually, for that very reason, collapses in on itself.
And the reason the obsessive-compulsive obsesses is that he has nothing else to do. 
He has to obsess since he has prevented himself from acting. He is holed up inside his consciousness, and the only objects on which he can act are ideas; and the only way 
left to him to act on them is to pour his consciousness on them—after all, 
consciousness, along with this ideas, are all he has left. And what is to obsess except 
to stream one’s consciousness onto some idea?
As for his compulsions: these are his mind’s way of pushing him back out into the world.
 The sane part of the obsessive-compulsive’s mind wants him to rejoin the living and 
thus to get out of his own head. The only way to do that is to find a way to get his 
obsessiveness to be converted into action. After all, obsessiveness is all he has. And 
compulsiveness is obsessiveness about behavior.
So the obsessive-compulsive’s compulsiveness represents the sane side of him. 
True— the compulsions themselves are insane. But they are insane only because, as 
an insane person, he
 
needs an insane pretext to act sanely. The sane part of the obsessive-compulsive—the 
part of him that wants him to act---is like a parent who is trying to feed a toddler who 
refuses to eat, and the insane part of the obsessive-compulsive—the part of him that 
doesn’t want to act---is like the toddler. And the sane part of the obsessive-compulsive 
is, as it were, pretending that the spoon in his hand is an airplane and that the peas on 
the spoon are little passengers; and, in this way, by speaking the toddler’s 
language—his delusion-embodying language---the parent gets the toddler to do 
something good for himself.
So there it is. OCD is about refusal to act. It is about defensively holing oneself up 
inside consciousness. And getting the obsessive-compulsive to act---and thus to stop 
being obsessive-compulsive—is like a taking a cat out of a tree. It takes a lot of time 
and a lot of sensitivity. And the way to do is by exerting a firm and steady, but gentle 
pressure.
There is good news, thought. This cat in a tree that the obsessive-compulsive is---is 
sane. He knows that he is on a tree; he knows, deep down, that he has to get out of the 
tree. But he’s a bit gun-shy.
The obsessive-compulsive cat in a tree is not like the schizophrenic cat in a tree. The 
latter doesn’t know that he is in a tree or that he is a cat. And getting him to come down 
from three is dramatically more difficult, since, precisely because he doesn’t grasp 
reality as it is, there is no language in common between him and the person who is 
trying to talk him down.
But obsessive-compulsive sees things as they are. His problem is not deludedness; it is 
fearfulness. And since he does see reality as it is, one can use reason on him, as long 
as one does so gently, so as to not intensify his fears.
 
The Obsessive-compulsive Must Accept his Own Sadistic Sexuality
An essential part of the treatment of the obsessive-compulsive is getting him to accept 
his own sexuality, which invariably has an extremely sadistic and/or masochistic 
character. If he does not accept his sexuality, there will be a continuation of the 
alienatedness of thought from feeling that is responsible for OCD. And he does accepts 
his sexuality, this will effectuate an OCD-eliminate reconciliation of thought and feeling.
Let me put these points into context.
All mental illness involves some kind of withdrawal from reality. But there are two 
different ways of withdrawing from reality and thus two different kind of mental illness. 
There are mental illnesses that effectuate this withdrawal by destroying the coherence 
of one’s ideation: these are known as psychoses.And there are mental illnesses that effectuate this withdrawal not by diminishing the 
coherence of one’s thinking but by paralyzing one’s will. These are known as neuroses. 
Neuroses do not destroy the coherence of one’s thought.
Or rather---they do not the coherence of one’s intellection, that is, of one’s mental 
activity so far as it is concerned with the discerning of logical relations. But there is a 
respect in which they diminish the coherence of one’s mentation: they estrange 
intellection from its ordinary emotional concomitants. So the neurotic can think, and he 
can also feel, but his thoughts are alienated from his feelings, the result being that his 
will is weakened. And the reason for this weakening of will is that, as philosophers have 
long known, action is not solely about thought, nor solely about feeling, but about the 
two operating jointly and symbiotically; and in the neurotic, this symbiosis has been 
disrupted.
In the psychotic, this symbiosis has not been disrupted; but the psychotic’s intellection 
is incoherent, so that, even though his will is intact, it is led on so many fool’s errands 
by his compromised powers of reasoning.
The obsessive-compulsive’s intellection is coherent. Nay, hyper-coherent. Indeed, it is 
made hyper-coherent by the fact that it is estranged from the usual emotional 
concomitants of thought and can thus pursue its own course without being weighed 
down by the usual emotional resistances. But the obsessive-compulsive’s will is as 
atrophied as his intellect is hypertrophied--
-and for the very same reason, this being the just-mentioned alienatedness of thought 
from feeling. And the restoration of the obsessive-compulsive’s personality consists 
entirely in restoring this thought-feeling balance.
And this is done by two doing two things. First, by finding a profession for the 
obsessive- compulsive that is congenial to him and that enables him to flourish without 
having to perform the impossible the impossible feat of changing his own psychological 
architecture.
Second, by reconciling him to his own sexuality, which, precisely because his affect, 
and therewith his sexuality, is so alienated from, and thus not tempered by, his 
judgement, assumes particularly extreme forms and, consequently, sadistic or 
masochistic forms. And the reintegration of his sexuality is accomplished by reconciling 
him to urges and ceasing to regard them as an abomination.
And the truth is that his sexuality is no more abominable than anyone else’s; it only 
seems that way because, in him, the sadistic and masochistic undercurrents of human 
sexuality are exposed, owing to the fact that his sexuality has not been absorbed into 
the usual narratives and rationalizations. And the reason for this non-absorption is, of 
course, that the thought-feeling balance responsible for these narratives and 
rationalizations has been disrupted.
 
Be it noted that the obsessive-compulsive, having as he does a strong and intact 
conscience, never actually carries out acts that are actually, as opposed to symbolically 
sadistic or masochistic; so there is no danger in reconciling him to his own sadism and/
or masochism.
 
Institutional Psychopathy
A psychopath is someone who has no conscience. There is a phenomenon known as 
'institutional psychopathization', this being the process whereby an entire institution 
becomes psychopathic, in the sense that the institution as a whole is without principle 
and also in the sense that the people composing it are without principle. Once an institution becomes just slightly psychopathized, it quickly succumbs to wholesale 
psychopathization. And a number of institutions and professions have become, or are 
in the process of becoming, psychopathic. This main reason for this is that, owing to 
technological advances, many institutions are not only obsolete and therefore serve no 
positive function, but actually thwart progress and prevent people doing what those 
same institutions are supposed to do. As a result, those institutions are fraudulent, and 
therefore psychopathic, and the same is therefore true of the people composing them, 
who have no choice but to being stone-psychopaths themselves if they want to be 
employed by those institutions. Many university-departments and academic disciplines 
are not completely and utterly psychopathic. This is glaringly where the humanities and 
social sciences are concerned. I believe that some branches of psychotherapy have 
become psychopathized, this being why psychiatrists are proverbially likely to make 
their patients’ problems even worse.
Because psychopathy is now being spread by psychopathic institutions and 
professions, it is dramatically more widespread than it used to be. Consequently, the 
old saw that only 1%-3% of the population consists of psychopaths is now pathetically 
outdated and also presupposes a narrow as well as antiquated conception of what a 
psychopath is. Most psychopaths in our time are not slick con-men but are rather 
bureaucrats, who discharge their psychopathy not by running Madoff-style swindles but 
by misdeploying educational and professional resources. I am hosting an ‘Ask me 
anything’ session on psychopathy, where I will answer any questions that you have on 
psychopathy. Please go to www.amafeed.com and type ‘psychopathy’ in the search bar.
 I’m the first result that pops up.
 
The Psychology of the Bureaucrat


People are internally conflicted only when they are autonomous—only, in other words, 
when they decide for themselves how to act and what to think. To be internally 
conflicted is to have two incompatible decision-making protocols. And if one has no 
such protocols—in other words, if one does not decide for oneself what to think or 
do—then one cannot have two incompatible such protocols and thus cannot be 
internally conflicted.
A bureaucrat is somebody who has surrendered all of his decision-making 
responsibilities to the bureaucracy that employs him. So long as a given person’s 
decision-making protocol is capable of being in conflict with the bureaucracy that hosts 
him, that person is not a bureaucrat. And so long as the condition obtains, he can be 
internally conflicted, since, under that circumstance, he has, or at least can have, two 
decision-making protocols, one corresponding to the bureaucracy in question and one 
that is his own.
It is only when those two protocols merge, and it is therefore only when his own 
decision- protocol is absorbed into and therefore eliminated in favor, the institution’s 
decision-protocol, that the person in question becomes a bureaucrat.
One is either completely a bureaucrat or completely not a bureaucrat. If somebody is 
only half a bureaucrat, then he can to some extent make decisions for himself and 
therefore is not a bureaucrat at all. What can happen is that a person may be weak and,
 without crossing the line, so to speak, that separates non-bureaucrats from 
bureaucrats, may in contexts like a bureaucrat and may, by virtue of being obviously 
weak, be tolerated by bureaucrats. But even this arrangement is unstable and will work only if the non-bureaucrat in question is an utter ‘bump on a log.’
Given that bureaucrats are not internally conflicted, they are not neurotic or otherwise 
psychodynamic. They can be—and indeed categorically are---broken, and the breaks 
within them may be in located along the psychical fault lines that, in a psychodynamic 
person, would be the meeting-points of two opposed emotional forces. In someone who 
is psychodynamic, these fault lines corresponding to mind-internal tensions; but in the 
bureaucrat, they correspond to veritable schisms---to rents or cracks. In a 
psychodynamic person, these fault-lines are places where there is too much psychical 
activity—where there is a plurality of mutually opposed psychical forces; and because 
these fault-lines are still psychical hot-spots in the psychodynamic person---because 
intra-psychical communications, as it were, pass through them and along them
—their existence is not an existential threat to the integratedness of the psyche in 
question.
But in the bureaucrat, these fault-lines are indeed schisms---they are crevasse-like 
gaps or canyons---which are to no degree populated by psychical forces, and their 
existence therefore does pose an existential threat to the psyche in question.
And when a bureaucrat is made consciously aware of these fault-lines within his own 
psyche, he responds, not with psychological resistance, but with abject fear. 
Bureaucrats do not have resistance-reactions. Resistances are about internal conflict: 
one part of the person’s mind accepts what he is being told about himself, but another 
opposed part refuses to accept it.
Bureaucrats are not internally conflicted and therefore do not have resistance-reactions.
 A bureaucrat will hiss and seethe like a wounded viper when a spot-light is shined on 
the cracks in his psyche: but this hissing and seething is not about resistance: it is 
about pure, non-conflicted, utter hatred of the person who is shining that light.
The bureaucrat’s psychological architecture is not identical with, and is not easily
 
understood in terms of, that of a psychodynamic person . The bureaucrat’s architecture 
is to be understood in terms of that of the psychopath. The psychopath, like the 
bureaucrat, is incapable of internal conflict, since internal conflicts are mediated by 
conscience, and the psychopath has no conscience. The difference between the 
bureaucrat and the psychopath is that because the bureaucrat was once a person---
because he was a person before his own decision-protocol was replaced by that of the 
corresponding institution---his psychological architecture, though non- psychodynamic 
and therefore effectively dead, has a formal similarity to that of the psychodynamic 
person: the bureaucrat’s psyche is the skeleton of a once existent but now dead and 
gone psychodynamic psyche. By contrast, the psychopath’s psyche does not even 
have a skeletal or formal similarity to that of a normal person.
There is no one way that bureaucrats look or sound. But they often have a distinctively 
nasal sound---a distinctly monotonic nasal sound. It sounds like this

          https://www.podomatic.com/podcasts/jmkuczynski13629/episodes/2018-02- 
06T20_06_58-08_00

      https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=9uoIOM72Z80

This sound is easily imitated and often is imitated, usually in comedic contexts. In any 
case, this sound, with its distinctive lack of resonances has to do with the bureaucrat’s 
impoverished emotional architecture: the missing resonances correspond to the emotional gaps— the dead zones or empty spaces—within the bureaucrat’s psyche.
 
Psychopaths are Rogue Bureaucrats And Bureaucrats are Non-rogue Psychopaths

The reason psychopaths can pass lie detector tests is that psychopaths don’t believe 
anything. So they don’t have to lie. In the psychopath, rationalizations have replaced 
beliefs. In other words, what it is convenient to believe has replaced what is actually 
believed. So nothing is believed. But certain propositions are accepted, as long as it is 
convenient to accept them.
The bureaucrat does not believe anything. But he accepts certain propositions because 
the bureaucracy that employs him requires him to accept them. In other words, he 
accepts those propositions because it is convenient to accept them.
Thus, in the bureaucrat, what it is convenient to believe has replaced what is actually 
believed. Rationalizations have replaced beliefs. Institutional pseudo-reality has 
replaced actual reality.
So what is the difference between the bureaucrat and the psychopath?
Ultimately, there is no difference. But so far as there is a difference, it is this: the 
bureaucrat is part of an organized collective of psychopaths, whereas the so-called 
psychopath is not a part of such a collective. So the psychopath is a rogue bureaucrat, 
and the bureaucrat is a non-rogue psychopath.
But if the bureaucrat were removed from his host-institution and were not quickly 
embedded in another bureaucracy, his behavior would immediately become that of the 
psychopath. The bureaucrat’s appearance of propriety and decency is just that—an 
appearance. There is nothing there: the bureaucrat is just as amorphous and 
insubstantial as the psychopath, but he has the external reinforcement of an institution. 
So it is this external reinforcement that gives the bureaucrat’s character any semblance 
of legitimacy or cohesiveness. And he knows this. He knows that, without this external 
reinforcement, he would turn into a protoplasmic blob of amorphous sub-humanity. So 
he knows that he has to march in lock-step with his host- institution, lest he turn into 
such a blob.
Thus, the bureaucrat depends not just for his income but for his very identity on his 
following rules and protocols.
 
How Double-think is Possible?
How is double-think possible? How is it possible for a person to have a given belief at 
one time and then believe the opposite a moment later? The answer: It isn’t possible.
But double-think is possible?
So how is that possible? How can double-think be possible if it is not possible to shift 
freely between opposed and incompatible beliefs?
It is possible because double-think is about not believing anything.
Double-think is not about shifting between veritable beliefs. It is about shifting between 
institutionally or socially mandated acceptances that are in some respects functionally 
similar to beliefs but are not beliefs.
Who is it that engages in double-think? Bureaucrats. Bureaucrats are the ones who 
engage in double-think.
And what is a bureaucrat?
Someone who believes whatever his institutional tells him to believe.
In other words, someone who believes nothing. Someone who has no beliefs at all. 
Someone who, although he doesn’t believe anything, nonetheless pretends to have certain beliefs, depending on what the institution in question requires of him.
But so far as a person is able to engage in double-think, it is because he doesn’t 
believe anything.
So far as a person is able to keep track of his various lies, it is because, underneath his 
lies, there is a core of veritable beliefs. But in the bureaucrat, this core of true belief has 
been infiltrated by double-think. Since double-think is to real belief what plastic flowers 
are to real flowers, this means that the bureaucrats ability to believe anything has been 
rotted out. And this in turn means that the bureaucrat’s agency has been rotted out, 
agency being the ability to decide for oneself what to think and do. This is because in 
order to decide for oneself what to do one has to have beliefs as to how one ought to 
act. And if one cannot have beliefs, then one cannot have beliefs as to how one ought 
to act. And if one cannot have beliefs as to how one ought to act, one cannot act. One 
can only engage in reflexive pseudo-actions.
And this is the bureaucrat’s situation: it isn’t that the bureaucrat isn’t doing what he 
knows he should do. It is that he doesn’t know anything, since he doesn’t believe 
anything, and he therefore can’t do anything. When the bureaucrat says that his ‘hands 
are tied’, he is telling the truth. The truth is that his hands are tied: he can’t do anything 
at all.
Of course, when the bureaucrat says ‘my hands are tied’, he is claiming that the 
institution is not letting him act as he wants to do. But the truth is that he can’t do 
anything. He can only reflexively respond to institutional dictates.
So is isn’t that the institution isn’t letting him do such and such. It’s that the institution 
hasn’t ordered him to do such and such. For the bureaucrat, there is no difference 
between being allowed to do such and such and being required to such and such. This 
difference exists only for people who can decide for themselves what to do, and the 
bureaucrat has no such ability.
 
Bureaucratic Bloat Responsible for OCD-spike
When I was a graduate student, I gave a paper to one of my professors to look at. I still 
half-believed that my so-called ‘professors’ were in fact professors, as opposed to the 
arch- bureaucrats that they proved to be (and that I already semi-consciously knew 
them to be). When he and I were discussing my paper, he would refuse to weigh in on 
any statement that fell outside of his artificial so-called ‘specialization.’ He would say: 
‘ah, you’d have to ask so and so about that: you see, he’s the philosophy of mind 
expert in the department; my specialization is meta- ethics.’ In fact, he wouldn’t even 
reference so and so by name; he would just point in the direction of his office. It was 
the most utterly cowardly and small thing one could imagine. And it is artificial and 
absurd in the extreme to hold, as this professor did, that within a discipline like 
philosophy, there could legitimately exist such rigidly mutually exclusive sub-discipline 
or that intellectual labor could with any legitimacy be subdivided in a way that validated 
such subdivisions.
And this professor’s ‘work’ for lack of a better, like that of everyone else in the 
department, was a mere caricature of actual intellectual work, as it had been 
completely drained of meaning and realness by submission to the just-mentioned 
institutional, bureaucratic protocols that were masquerading as legitimate intellectual or 
scientific protocols.
There is a psychology professor at Harvard, by the name of Howard Gardner, who 
claims that intelligence is subdivided in the seven rigidly defined mutually isolated 
types: verbal, musical mathematical, social, etc.This is radically absurd as well as destructive in the extreme of human intellectual 
potential. One mark of truly intelligent people is that they are able to reinvent 
themselves intellectually—that their intelligence, though functionally encapsulated, at 
least within limits, is able to migrate about and embody itself in new aptitudes, nay, in 
aptitudes that are not even in the same discipline, or even in some cases in the same 
kind of discipline, as the aptitudes that preceded them.
And I know this from my own case. I was told that I was a moron. Then after 
reinventing myself—or simply being placed in a new environment that triggered 
intellectual development---I was told, either by a person or a test-score, that I was 
genius of such and such a kind, but that I was moron of thus and such a kind. Then, 
after another such revolution had occurred, I was told that I was moron in such and 
such a respect but a genius in thus and a respect. And so on.
My abilities were changing; I was acquiring new ones and letting others lie fallow. But 
what was happening was that a core of intelligence was putting on new hats and taking 
off old ones; it wasn’t that there was rigid pre-determined intellectual template that my 
intelligence was doomed to inhabit; it was that I was in fact intelligent and, owing to 
changing exigencies and to changing incentive and disincentive-structures, my 
intelligence was repositing itself in different skills and interests and, indeed, 
intelligences.
When a person sustains damage to a certain part of his brain, the functions previously 
discharged by that brain-center will come to be discharged by other brain-centers. To 
some extent, those other brain-centers are merely performing analogues of that 
function; in some cases, however, to at least some degree, they seem to be assuming 
the very same function. In any case, it is clear that intellectual recovery, and intellectual 
growth in general, depend on intelligence’s being able to migrate around, from module 
to module. This is clear, I stress, both in cases involving brain-damage and also in 
cases, such as my own, of garden-variety intellectual growth.
And Howard Gardner’s theory automatically rules out all of that growth as impossible.
 
And it thereby strips educational and psychiatric institutions of any reason to recognize 
it, and it gives them every possible reason to deny it when it happens and, therefore, to 
thwart the corresponding cases of intellectual growth.
Gardner’s theory is a pitiful and brazen rationalization of rigid and artificial purely 
institutional sub-divisions---of bureaucratic divisions of labor—that have only the most 
superficial correspondence to facts about the structure of intelligence. But educational 
and mental health-related institutions can certainly do quite enough to thwart 
intellectual growth in people as to make it seem as though Gardner’s pseudo-theory is 
correct. And I refer to it as a pseud-theory because that is what it is: it is a bureaucratic 
action that is dressed up as a psychological theory, with ruinous consequences.
And this is why OCD is on the rise. OCD has always has existed; that much is clear, 
given what a well-defined constellation of symptoms it involves. But OCD has definitely 
been on the rise in recent years.
Why is this?
OCD is what happens when one’s acceptance of legalistic, logical or pseudo-logical 
protocols is used to tamp down one’s emotions---one’s aggression, one’s sexuality, 
one’s vitality. And a spreading educational and legal bureaucracy—which is supported 
by fake experts, as Howard Gardner, with their fake and undermining theories, such as 
Gardner’s theory of intelligence---are making it increasingly difficult for people of great 
potential, and of correspondingly strong passions, to discharge those passions in a way that is constructive or, indeed, that is anything other than blind rage. And this rage is 
filtered through internal legalistic and pseudo-logical protocols that mirror the 
institutional protocols of the educational system in which the person in question is 
trapped. So, yes, there has been a rise in OCD. And that rise is in lock-step with the 
spread of cancerous educational and professional bureaucracies, and it is exacerbated 
by Howard Gardner-style theories, which are nothing but a broken educational 
bureaucracy’s rationalizing itself.
To sum up: More bureaucracy equals more suppressed rage. More suppressed rage, 
coupled with half-acceptance of bureaucratic protocols, equals rage that is expressed 
in the distinctively, bureaucratically repetitive and detail-oriented way that is the 
hallmark of OCD.
 
Submitting to Feminism Makes It More Psychopathic
A psychopath is someone who does not have a functioning superego, and psychopathy 
is the condition of not having a functioning superego. The main difference between 
men and women is that men have internalized superegos and women have 
externalized superegos. A man’s superego is a relation between that man and himself. 
A women’s superego is a relation between that woman and other people. A woman’s 
superego cannot function autonomously; it cannot function unless it is given external 
reinforcement. And that reinforcement comes from the internalized superegos of the 
men in her life. For a woman, manly, firm, and morally centered men are the very 
embodiment of conscience. Therefore, when men are not manly or firm or centered, 
and when instead they are weak and effeminate and uncentered, women’s superegos 
have no external reinforcement and thus become defunct.
Here is a concrete way of thinking about this. Suppose that Smith is the President of 
your country. If there is a national crisis, and Smith responds by being indecisive and 
weak and effeminate, then the country will rightly become irate; and it will respond 
either by disintegrating into a condition of nation-level psychopathy or it will replace 
Smith with somebody who is strong and centered and decisive. In other words, if that 
country is to avoid degenerating into a condition of psychopathy, it must replace its 
current weak and effeminate leader with a real and therefore manly leader. And the 
reason for this is that, although an effeminate person can have conscience, such a 
conscience is not self-contained; in order to function, an effeminate person’s 
conscience depends on some other conscience. Therefore, in contexts where there are 
no true men, there are no functioning consciences, and the result is a condition of 
complete psychopathy.
Consequently, when a self-described feminist woman is being hysterically psychopathic,
 the worst way to respond is by attempting to appease her or by submitting to her. Her 
psychopathy is a product of the absence of a functioning male conscience in her life. 
And if you respond to her in a weak and submissive manner, you are showing her that 
you lack a functioning conscience, in consequence of which her psychopathy will rage 
on. In fact, it will rage on even more virulently than before since, now that she has 
discovered that you have no conscience, she has discovered there to be one less 
functioning superego in the world to resist her own lack of a functioning superego.
By contrast, if you are firm, manly, and centered, then you are giving her superego the 
needed reinforcement of a functioning superego, in consequence of which her 
superego will then become operational.
Being firm does not mean being violent. Being violent is a way of being weak and 
effeminate, just as being submissive is a way of being weak and effeminate. Being violent and being submissive are two sides of the same feminine coin. The appropriate 
way to act is like a man: firm but not violent, decisive but not rigid, and principled but 
not judgmental. If men acted in this way, then even the most psychopathic and 
hysterical of feminists would begin to act with genuine integrity. In a way, the feminist 
woman’s psychopathic hysteria is her petulant and childish way of asking men to be 
men and therefore to take control, just as the misbehavior of a young boy is his way of 
asking his father to be a father and therefore to be a source of structure and discipline.
 
What is the Prognosis for People with OCD?
Somebody on Quora.com asked the following question:
What is the prognosis for patients with Obsessive–compulsive disorder (OCD)? Here is 
my answer:
People who have OCD have it because it provides them with relief from anxiety—from 
anxiety so intense as to be almost inconceivable to people who have not had OCD. But 
OCD is just a defense-mechanism. And as is the case with most defense-mechanisms, 
there comes a time in the subject’s life when it starts to do more harm than good. At 
that point, the obsessive- compulsive has to make a decision: What do I want? Do I 
want mere freedom from anxiety? Or do I want to live and grow? It is 100% up to him 
what he chooses. If he chooses to grow, he will, and his OCD, without exactly 
vanishing, will assume productive forms. If he chooses not to grow, he won’t.
OCD, though an affliction, is ultimately a voluntary one. If the obsessive-compulsive 
chooses to remain in his defensive bubble of isolation from the world, then his OCD will 
defeat him. If he chooses to re-engage the world, then his OCD will assume non-
pathological and in fact productive forms, as it did with Beethoven and Einstein.
OCD, though an affliction, is an affliction of the will—and is thus unlike cancer or 
asthma, which have nothing to do with the integrity of one’s will. And OCD is eliminated,
 or at least made to assume adaptive forms by duly exercising one’s will in growth-
oriented ways.
The symptoms of OCD are involuntary only in a relative sense. Relative to a 
determination to remain within his defensive bubble of non-engagement, there is 
nothing much that the obsessive-compulsive can do about those symptoms. He can 
engage in a bit of ‘cognitive-behavioral therapy’, but the effects of such therapy will be 
shallow and short-lived, absent a determination on the obsessive-compulsive’s part to 
grow and change himself in a way that makes his OCD irrelevant. On the other, if he 
does choose to leave his defensive bubble and
re-engage the world, then, indeed, his OCD will become irrelevant. So relative to a 
determination on his part to re-engage the world, the symptoms of OCD are voluntary.
So ultimately OCD is indeed voluntary, and if one sees it for the voluntary condition that 
it ultimate is, then the prognosis is 100% positive. But if one sees it as a garden-variety 
pathology, then the prognosis is negative. A garden-variety condition is one that does 
not afflict one’s will and that falls outside the scope of the one’s will. OCD is an affliction 
of one’s will; it is a weakening of one’s will, in consequence of a withdrawal from the 
world. And by choosing to go back out into the world, the obsessive-compulsive 
strengthens his will and causes one’s OCD to assume benign if not productive forms.
 
The Way to Beat OCD is to Have a Purpose in Life

Charles Manson’s followers gave him everything: money, sex, labor, drugs, adoration. 
And what did give them? Nothing. Or almost nothing. He gave them one thing---and it was the most important thing of all: a purpose. He made them believe that they were 
part of an important cause. A person with a cause and no money has almost everything.
 A person with all the money in the world and no cause has almost nothing. And 
Manson gave his minions a cause, and they gave Manson everything else.
Jim Jones’ followers gave him everything. They provided him with slave labor. They 
barely slept. And at least until the last few months, they did all of this voluntarily. And 
what did Jim Jones give his followers? Nothing. Or just one thing—he gave them a 
sense of purpose. He gave them a cause that was their own.
These were profoundly evil men—consummate manipulators. But it was for that very 
reason that they knew exactly what people need. If they hadn’t known as much, they 
couldn’t have been such successful predators. And what people need, as these two 
knew, is a purpose.
People will put up with pain and strife if they have a purpose. And they won’t if they
don’t.
Getting rid of OCD is hard. If a given obsessive-compulsive has a purpose in life that his
OCD is preventing him from fulfilling, then he will get over his OCD. Otherwise he won’t.
 OCD is simply too painful to get rid of unless one really has to. And one really has to if, 
and for the most part only if, OCD is interfering with one’s purpose in life.
But there is deeper connection between having a purpose in life and getting rid of OCD. 
OCD is about having a bifurcated psychological structure. It is about being of two 
minds—not having a single hegemonic set of values to which all psychical activity is 
subordinate and, for this reason, not having any way of resolving the internal conflicts 
that are wrenching one’s psyche apart.
If one has a grand purpose in life, one has a way of resolving these conflicts; for if one 
has a master plan to which everything else in one’s mind, including one’s internal 
conflicts, are subordinated, in which case these conflicts can and will be resolved. And 
therewith one’s OCD will go away. If one has no master plan, there is no way to resolve 
one’s internal conflicts. And except in the unlikely event that those internal conflicts 
simply fade away, one’s OCD will persist.
The good news is that one wouldn’t have OCD in the first place unless one did have 
some grand purpose in life, the reason being that OCD is what happens when one is 
estranged from one’s own purpose. OCD is about alienatedness from one’s true self 
and one’s true purpose, and the way to get over it is to find that purpose and actualize 
it.
 
Is OCD a Disease?


When a condition is described as a ‘disease’, it is usually implied that the person who 
has it cannot simply ‘will it’ away. That person may be responsible for getting it, as is 
the case with someone who get lung cancer because of smoking, but once has it, so is 
it assumed, his continuing to have it is not about his simply failing to make the right 
effort of will.
Given that OCD very clearly is an illness of sorts, it seems to follow that someone who 
has OCD is ‘stuck with it’, in the way in which someone who has lupus or asthma is 
simply stuck with it—that, although his condition may be treated, as asthma can be 
treated, and even eliminated, it is not a question of simply willing it away.
But here is the problem: OCD, though indisputably a disease, is a disease that afflicts 
the will itself. It is an affliction of the will. A person with osteoporosis is somebody with porous bones, not a porous will. A person with cancer is somebody with damaged cells, 
not with a damaged will. But somebody with OCD is somebody with a damaged will. 
And the only way to fix a damaged will is by exercising that will. And that is what the 
obsessive-compulsive must do. And if he doesn’t do that, he is quite as responsible for 
his condition as is a fat person who refuses to stop overeating.
Let me put these points into context.
People with OCD can be willful---in fact, they are categorically so—and they can also, 
in some contexts, be legitimately highly determined—in fact, they are unusually likely to 
be capable of being determined to an exceptionally high degree. But in other respects, 
their will, though functional, has been ruptured: their will is like that of an exceedingly 
strong person who has pulled a muscle: that person is still strong and, in some context, 
can exercise that strength— but his ability to exercise his strength is more much 
context-dependent than it would be if he didn’t have a ruptured muscle.
Somebody with OCD is somebody whose psyche has been bifurcated into two a Dr.
Jekyll half and Mr. Hyde half—into an id-half and a superego-half. And these two 
halves are at war with each other: the bestial, criminal side of the obsessive-
compulsive wants to come out, but the angelic morality-driven side won’t let it; so the 
criminal side has to leak out in symptoms. And, in any case, the paralysis of will that is 
the hallmark of OCD is the result of a stalemate between these two sides.
But at the end of the day, it is entirely up to the obsessive-compulsive what he does. 
He isn’t like a paralytic who simply can’t move; he is somebody who, although he can 
exercise his will, won’t. He won’t because of the anxiety he will experience. But he still 
can. If he were offered a million dollars, he would, regardless of the anxiety.
To be sure, the obsessive-compulsive is diseased; he does have a very real 
psychopathology. But his being diseased consists not in his being unable but rather in 
his being unwilling to do the right thing. And getting over OCD involves strengthening 
the obsessive- compulsive’s atrophied willingness to act; and the way to strengthen it is 
to get him to act—first a little, then a lot.
That is not all there is to getting over OCD: the conflicts underlying the obsessive- 
compulsive’s paralysis of will must be addressed. But in order for them to be addressed 
meaningfully, they must be accompanied by veritable doing on the part of the 
obsessive- compulsive. The purpose of the psychoanalytic interpretations must be to 
direct the will—to indicate pathways that it should follow—but unless it actually follows 
those pathways, those
 
analytic constructions will be so much theory. And psychoanalysis is not about theory; it 
is practice: it is about getting the patient out of the bubble of his mind, which, ultimately, 
can only be done by doing it.
 
Social Anxiety is about Rage


People who have social anxiety don’t want to be around people. And they don’t want to 
be around people because they hate people, or at least are angry at them.
And notice that the effect of social anxiety is to keep the person who has it away from 
people. When he is around people, he is upset. When is alone again, he stops being 
upset. He tells himself that he fears others. And there is a certain truth in this. But really 
it is more that he fears that he might do them; he is afraid of the danger that his own 
anger towards others might put him in.In this respect, social anxiety is similar to shyness. Shyness is ultimately about intense 
rage towards others. This becomes immediately obvious to anyone who has actually 
entered into serious communication with a shy person: what becomes clear---because 
they explicitly affirm as much—is how intense their hatred is of others.
In any case, people with social anxiety are not necessarily wrong to hate people. 
People with social anxiety are often highly intelligent and sensitive people who are 
misunderstood by others and have been wronged by them. So it is not necessarily 
incumbent on someone with social anxiety to change his views or feelings about others.
 But if he wants to function in society, it is incumbent on him to recognize why he has 
social anxiety. And once he acknowledges that he has it because of anger, his anger 
will become conscious. And once it becomes conscious, he can put it in its proper 
place. When anger is unconscious, it widens its target; it spreads indiscriminately, and 
people become the object of it who would otherwise be targeted by it. But when anger 
becomes conscious, it is confined to its rightful target--to the specific individuals who 
have wronged on---instead of being inappropriately applied to all people.
There are two ways for someone with social anxiety to get over it. The first is to acquire 
power. Hatred is about impotence, and it tends to vanish as one acquires power. The 
second is to accept that one has anger towards others and not to take oneself to task 
for it. Instead of beating oneself for one’s misanthropic tendencies, one should accept 
them and draw strength from them. Much great accomplishment is the result of 
misanthropy that has been consciously accepted and then duly sublimated.
 
Structural Therapy
The preferred way of treating OCD these days is known as “cognitive behavioral 
therapy.” CBT be called “behavioral therapy” since there is nothing cognitive about it, 
as it consists entirely in changing the obsessive-compulsive’s behavior directly, and not 
way of changing the emotional conflicts responsible for his behavior.
Obviously, the obsessive-compulsive’s behavior does have to be changed; indeed, 
getting him to become at least minimally functional, and therefore getting him to not be 
completely crippled by his compulsions, is a prerequisite for him engaging in any sort of 
meaningful therapy or, indeed, in his having a meaningful life.
But taken by itself, behavior modification is not enough. Indeed, behavior modification, 
unless supplemented by treatment of underlying emotional disturbances, fails of its own 
purpose, as in, it fails even to modify behavior, since those emotional conflicts will 
always find new ways of pathologizing behavior.
The only real way to treat OCD is through structural therapy. Structural therapy is the 
process of changing the obsessive-compulsive’s mentality from that of someone who is 
reality- avoidant, and therefore fantasy-oriented, to being someone who is reality-
oriented, and therefore fantasy-avoidant. The obsessive-compulsive’s obsessionality is 
about his preferring symbolic representations of reality to reality and, consequently, his 
preferring thought to action. And his compulsions are merely externalized obsessions 
and therefore represent overt expressions of his reality-avoidance.
In order to effect this change of orientation within the obsessive-compulsive, it is 
necessary to modify his behavior; and cognitive behavioral therapy is therefore a 
component of structural therapy. But it is a subordinate component, the main 
component being the just- described reversal in the obsessive-compulsive’s mentality.
 
Effective Therapists Must Have and Project Inner StrengthI recently joined linked in, and I immediately connected with a number of OCD- 
therapists, all of whom claimed, in their about me sections, that they were looking for 
opportunities to broaden their therapeutic horizons and learn more about OCD. And I 
reached out to them, telling them to check out m website, OCD and philosophy and 
give me their feedback. And I invited a few to do podcasts with me, in which I would 
give them a chance to discuss their views. Not one responded meaningfully; they all 
responded by saying ‘thanks!’, and nothing else. In other words, they hit the auto-reply 
‘thanks’ button at the bottom of the message.
And when I took a second look at them, I saw that they were timid looking, 
unadventurous, cautious and scared people. Not bad people, by any means. But not 
people who projected strength; not people whose presence suggested that they had 
ever overcome anything; not focused or centered people. But rather people who living 
life defensively, playing by a pre- existing rule-book, and just trying to keep their heads 
above water.
And that is understandable. But it means that as OCD-therapists, their batting rate is 
going to be zero. The essence of any therapeutic relationship is confidence on the 
patient’s part in the therapist; it is belief, in the competence and integrity, on the part of 
the analysand in the analyst. The analyst’s words affect the analysand by way of the 
rapport that exists between them, and that rapport is based on the analysand’s 
confidence in the analyst. And if that confidence isn’t there, the analyst’s words are not 
going to mobilize restorative energies within the patient. The analyst will retain the 
power to hurt the patient with his words, but he will not be able to do much in the way of 
helping the patient.
Psychoanalysis and psychotherapy are not exercises in computation. Obviously, 
intelligence and knowledge on the part of the therapist can help enormously; but they 
help only on the condition that there is an empathic rapport through which they can 
help. And the essence of that rapport is a belief—or a feeling that is deeper than 
belief—on the patient’s part in the analyst’s power to heal. And that belief will be 
possible to sustain only if the analyst is somebody who not only talks the talk but walks 
the walk. And a few meaningless degrees and certifications will not suffice. There is a 
singularity of outlook the therapist must have if he is to help the patient, and there is no 
way he can have such an outlook unless has conquered his own demons. And it seems 
to me that many a so-called mental health ‘expert’ is an expert only in getting through 
credentialing programs that are exercises in evading one’s demons and in failing to 
acquire just the sort of inner strength that is the cornerstone of any effective analyst’s 
practice.
 
Reading-related Difficulties in People with OCD


People with OCD are generally unusually intelligent, and they tend to do well on 
standardized tests, especially tests of reading comprehension. But OCD often makes it 
extraordinarily difficult for such people to read. When reading, their obsessionality flairs 
up: they have to ritualistically re-read words or phrases—not because they don’t 
understand what they’ve read, but because their OCD compels them to. Or upon 
turning the page, they are stricken with a morbid fear that there was some kernel of 
wisdom that they didn’t take in on the previous page; and they go back and re-read it, 
then turn the page again—only to be stricken once again by the same fear. And so on.Two questions arise: What is this about and how is it to be treated? The answer to the 
second question is clear: They must not back down; they must not not read. They must 
simply re- train their brains in such a way that their OCD finds different prey. The 
written is much too valuable to be alienated from—and it is especially value to people 
with OCD, given their intellectual bent and given also the nature of the disturbances 
underlying OCD. Which brings us to the answer to the first question, which is:
These reading disturbances are text-book cases of resistance: The very reason the 
obsessive-compulsive has problems reading is that, when he is reading, he is in danger 
of reading something that will shed light on his own emotional disturbances.
Be it noted that the very obsessive-compulsives who have the most severe reading-
related problems only have them with certain kinds of material. More specifically, they 
only have them with ‘good’ books and articles—with material that is rich in content. 
They generally don’t have them with ‘pulp’---with things that are easy to read and not 
informative. And they have them the most with works that specifically concern 
psychological maladies. Oftentimes, a given
obsessive-compulsive who practically goes into convulsions when forced to read 
psychology can read mathematical logic without any problems. In fact, the sanitary of 
nature of such material may make reading it a kind of haven for him from his OCD.
In this connection, I suspect that oftentimes, when people go into psychologically (but 
not logically) sterile fields, such as logic and physics, it is, in part (though not entirely, 
obviously), because of the absence of troubling emotional resonances in work in those 
disciplines.
And this may explain why the very obsessive-compulsives who have the most severe 
reading-related difficulties may do the best on reading-comprehension tests: for the 
material on such tests tends to be sterile; it tends to be narrowly factual and thus not to 
arouse psychological resistances.
But it is very important to resolve these difficulties early in the obsessive-compulsive’s 
life. For if he becomes habituated to not being able to read—which often happens---
then he will lose out on both the professional and personal benefits of being able to 
absorb information through the written. And for him, those benefits are especially great. 
The obsessive-compulsive is a thinker by nature, and he is also a loner, which means 
that he has a double need for the ‘companionship’ of the written word.
 
The Limitations of CBT in the Treatment of OCD
There is a method of treating OCD known as ‘cognitive-behavioral therapy’ (CBT). CBT 
involves getting obsessive-compulsives to do the things that they are phobic about 
doing. So if a given obsessive-compulsive is afraid of touching dirty surfaces with his 
bare hands, then his CBT may involve him shaking hands with homeless people and 
not washing hands afterwards.
Cognitive-behavioral therapy really just be called ‘Behavioral therapy’, since there is 
nothing cognitive about it. In fact, it is anti-cognitive: all cognitive issues are ignored or 
addressed only by way of their behavioral derivatives.
In any case, it is patently obvious that getting over OCD involves changing one’s 
behavior. If a given obsessive-compulsive showers 20 times a day, then the first thing 
he must do is to stop showering twenty times a day. If he spends 10 hours a cleaning, 
then the first thing he must do is stop spending so much time cleaning.
So there is nothing wrong with behavioral therapy—or ‘cognitive’ behavioral therapy, if 
you want to call it that. In fact, behavioral therapy is, very obviously, an ineliminable 
part of the restoration of the obsessive-compulsive’s psychological well-being.But here is the problem. OCD is extremely productive, as in, it produces symptoms with 
almost limitless energy and creativity. If one symptom is squashed down, without its 
underlying causes being addressed, some other symptom will take its place.
This does not mean that all is lost for the obsessive-compulsive. All is most definitely 
not lost. It just means that the underlying cause of the obsessive-compulsives various 
obsessions and compulsions must be addressed.
And ultimately there is always one such cause. That cause is that the obsessive- 
compulsive has fled from reality into a world of symbolic proxies of reality. His 
obsessions have ideas for their objects, not the external realties for which ideas stand. 
And his compulsions are merely obsessions that are externalized in behavior.
The cause of this flight from reality into symbolic proxies thereof varies from case to 
case. So the cause of the cause of OCD varies from person to person. But the cause of 
OCD does not so vary. It is, in each case, a fear-driven flight from reality into a safe 
internal world in which ideas have taken the place of realities and in which thinking has 
taken the place of doing.
And ultimately the only way to improve the obsessive-compulsive’s condition is to get 
him out of this bubble of pseudo-reality. Getting him out of this bubble involves getting 
him to behave normally. There is no way to get him to rejoin the living without breaking 
the back of his various compulsions. For these compulsions---for example, the endless 
showering and hand- washing, the cleaning, the various repetitive and stereotyped 
behaviors to which OCD gives rise-
--are, as previously stated, externalized obsessions; and they thus represent the 
encroachment into the external world of the obsessive-compulsive’s replacement of 
real realities, as it were, with symbolic realities. The obsessive-compulsive’s various 
compulsives actions serve as a kind of veil between himself and reality, that prevents 
him from directly engaging it. And he has to engage it.
Plus, he has to function-which he won’t do, as long as he is compulsive—and he also 
has to enjoy leave---which he also won’t do so long as he is compulsive.
But the essence of the restoration of the obsessive-compulsive’s sanity is getting him to 
engage reality. If his behavior is treated without addressing this alienatedness from 
reality, the obsessions and compulsions will simply assume new form. On the other 
hand, if his behavior is treated as part of a larger program of reconciling him to reality, 
then there will be genuine
 
structural improvement, as opposed to the mere replacing of one set of symptoms of 
with another.
 
Turn OCD Against Itself


If you put a two-year old in ocean-water, it will start crying. But then it gets used to the 
water. And when you try to take it out two hours later, it starts crying again!
Children are OCD like in many respects. They need comforting routines. They need to 
be told certain bedtime stories over and over again. They need to sleep with specific 
stuffed animals. And so on.
But this clinginess of theirs can be turned against itself. Right now, they need to sleep 
with the snoopy stuffed animal, and they will howl and scream if you try to get them 
switch to their Dumbo stuffed animal But then, after an initial period of protest, the 
clinginess they had towards Snoopy will transfer over to Dumbo.With OCD it’s the same way. It’s the same way, because it’s the very same thing. OCD 
isn’t just like the just-described obsessive clinginess of children: it is that clinginess. 
Like all mental illnesses, OCD is regressive: it is the mind’s returning to a modus 
operandi from its earlier days.
Put another way, OCD is the persistence into adulthood of a childhood defense- 
mechanism. And that childhood defense-mechanism is one of clinging to certain habits, 
so as to insulate oneself from a frightening world within a safe little autistic bubble.
And that is what OCD is. OCD is about clinging to certain rituals and habits that make 
one feel safe. It is the presence, in an adult, of a defense-mechanism that, in other 
people, ceases to be operative during early childhood.
The other side of the coin is that, underlying the obsessive-compulsive’s rigidity is the 
same neural plasticity—the same transferability of clinginenss—that is present in the 
child—that is, indeed, the hallmark of a child’s mind.
The obsessive-compulsive is binary: he is all about all or nothing. Either he loves or 
hates.
Either he does no exercise or he compulsively exercises. Either he has to have 
bananas in his morning cereal or he has to not have them in his cereal.
So the obsessive-compulsiveness obsessive-compulsiveness—his clingness—is about 
binariness; it is about all or nothing-ism. And inherent in his all or nothing-ism is a child-
like ability to change---to do 180 degree shifts. So, paradoxically, underlying the 
obsessive- compulsive’s rigidity is non-rigidity—is neural plasticity.
And the obsessive-compulsive’s greatest asset, therefore, is his OCD itself! He must 
use the binariness, and concomitant ability to change, to combat the rigidity and fixity of 
habits to which his OCD disposes him.
Let me be more concrete. There is always something that the obsessive-compulsive 
has to do that he is afraid of doing---in the same way that the child is afraid of getting 
into the bathtub. And the obsessive-compulsive must confront this fear, and do it 
quickly and unblinkingly. And then what will happen is that he will be as obsessive-
compulsive about doing this new thing—the thing he had to do but was afraid to do—as 
he was about not doing it before. Just as the child, once he settles into his bath, will 
kick and scream when he has to get out of the bath tub.
So turn OCD against itself, by using the all-or-nothingism inherent in, and the neural 
plasticity inherent in and hidden in that all-or-nothingins, to effectuate growth-critical 
changes in oneself.
 
How do I get over my OCD?
Somebody on Quora.com asked: How do I get over my OCD? Here is my response:
The right question is not ‘how do I get over OCD?’ so much as it is ‘in what sort of 
context is my OCD an asset, as opposed to a liability?’ People with OCD are capable of 
intense thought and prolonged concentration. They are able to be mindful of details 
without losing sight of the big picture. Their ‘cognitive wiring’ is capable of being an 
enormous asset. At the same time, in some contexts, that same cognitive wiring—that 
same intellectual acuity and emotional sensitivity—-may be liability. In contexts, in 
which the obsessive-compulsive is being subjected to extreme and untoward emotional 
pressures, his finely tuned psyche and hyper-responsiveness may result in his 
defensively withdrawing behind a veil of obsessions and compulsions, the ultimate 
purpose of which is to insulate him from a threatening and toxic external reality. There 
are thus two components to dealing with OCD: (i) Identifying contexts in which one’s 
OCD is a strength, as opposed to a weakness and (ii) taking advantage of such contexts to build up one’s self-confidence so that, in adverse contexts, one does not 
become fearful and withdraw into a protective (but dangerously self-limiting) cloud of 
obsessions and compulsions.
 
Intrusive Thoughts

Why People with OCD Have Them


If you have a goal, it is easy not to be lazy. But if you don’t have goal. It is hard not to 
be lazy, since there is no point in not being lazy. Similarly, if you have a goal, it is easy
