not to succumb to neurotic compulsions. But if you don’t have a goal, it is hard not to do 
so since there is no point in tolerating the anxiety brought on by not doing so.
As for obsessions, these are always displacements: one obsesses about such and 
such as a way of not confronting thus and such. And when one has a goal, one’s 
obsessional energies are naturally channeled in the right direction, since, under that 
circumstance, one’s mind stands to gain more in the way of relief from anxiety by not 
obsessing than by obsessing. And when doesn’t have a goal, one’s mind stands to gain 
more in the way of anxiety-relief by obsessing than by not obsessing, and one is 
therefore inclined to keep on obsessing.
As for intrusive thoughts, these are one’s mind’s way of telling one—in a compressed, 
crude, and distorted way---what one has to do. Notice that one’s intrusive thoughts 
concern sex or violence or sexual violence. This is because the people who have such 
thoughts are in the process of castrating themselves—they are in the process of trying 
to become faceless bureaucrats---and their minds are recoiling at this violence that is 
being done to them, and they are shouting at one’s conscience mind, trying to tell it not 
to surrender. Intrusive thoughts are not the problem; the person who is making a 
eunuch of himself is the problem; the intrusive thoughts are the last gasp of the still 
robust part of one’s mind. In any case, the way to get over obtrusive thoughts is to have 
a goal in life. So long as one is drifting, one’s mind will be unsatisfied and will hiss and 
buck, sometimes by flooding one’s consciousness with intrusive thoughts or with 
compulsions, sometimes in other ways: but so long as one is drifting, it is just a matter 
of time before one’s psyche begins to disintegrate. And one can be free from 
psychopathology only so long as a one has a goal that one’s mental energies can 
organize themselves around and that one’s drives can discharge themselves through.

All people are dominated by compulsions. In the case of the non-obsessive-compulsive,
 those compulsions are unconscious. In the case of the obsessive-compulsive, they are 
conscious. And that is what it is to have OCD: it is to consciously experience the 
compulsions that others only experience unconsciously. Thus, where the non-
obsessive-compulsive has the illusion of freedom, the obsessive-compulsive has the 
illusion of its absence. In actuality, the obsessive- compulsive, by virtue of being aware 
of the compulsions to which he is subjected, has the ability to liberate himself from 
those compulsions, whereas the non-obsessive-compulsive, by virtue of not being thus 
aware, does not.
 
Physical vs. Mental Illness
Almost all physical illnesses are simply deficits—as in, they don’t do those who have 
them any good. And most mental illnesses are simply deficits. But OCD—though functionally a deficit—is an asset in two important ways. First, those who have it are 
highly intelligent, meaning that they are good at processing intellectual or cognitive 
information, and highly sensitive, meaning that they are good at processing affective or 
emotional information.
Philosophical aside: What we refer to as ‘sensitivity’ is a form of internal awareness—of 
inwardly directed intelligence: it is about different parts of the mind being in good 
communication with each other. And what we refer to as “intelligence” is outwardly 
directed intelligence; it is about being aware of dependence-relations holding among 
mind-external entities. In any case, the obsessive-compulsive has both kinds of 
intelligence; indeed, his condition is about those two forms of intelligence being hyper-
operative---being over-deployed. So, yes, so long as they are over-deployed, he is 
seriously impaired; but his impairment has to do with the mutually cancelling effects of 
powerful cognitive forces, not, as is the case with most psychological impairments, with 
an absence of such forces.
And this relates to the second respect in which obsessive-compulsives are at an 
advantage.
Obsessive-compulsives are psycho-dynamic. They are internally conflicted. Which, 
though not an asset in and of itself, shows that they have ideals and, moreover, that 
they are taking themselves to task for falling short of those ideals. Where there is no 
conception of right and wrong—no conception of how, morally and otherwise, things 
should be—there is no internal conflict. And where there is a conception of right and 
wrong, there is ipso facto a willingness to try to make things right. And where such 
willingness exists, it can always be used by the person who has it to make himself 
better. In the case of the obsessive-compulsive, this willingness is not being optimally 
deployed; but it is there, which is what matters, since, so long as it exists, it can always 
be redeployed.
So the obsessive-compulsive, though in a very unpleasant and in some ways 
objectively bad situation, is best thought as being in a situation not of sheer minuses, 
but of inverted pluses--
-of assets that are being turned against themselves, not of mere deficits. And this 
means that in the right hands, the obsessive-compulsive stands not only to make a 
good recovery but to have a positively good life.
 
How to Treat Compulsions that are not Accompanied by Obsessions?



One of my listeners, Earth Pet, asked the following question. I quote:
“How would you advise attenuating or controlling compulsions for those without the 
obsessive component?”
Here is my answer.
Obsessions are thwarted compulsions. In other words, when one has a compulsion that 
one is prevented from acting on, one is left with an obsession. Equivalently, 
compulsions are obsessions that are expressed in behavior.
So the relationship between the obsession and the compulsion parallels the 
relationship between intention and subsequent action.
And just as there is always an intention whenever there is an action, so there is always 
an obsession whenever there is a compulsions. Of course, the obsession does not 
become an object of consideration in its own right so long as it is discharged sufficiently quickly in action, just as an intention does not become an object of attention in its own 
right if sufficiently quickly discharged in action. But just as there is an intention 
whenever there is action, so there is an obsession whenever there is a compulsive 
action.
So the situation presupposed by the question does not in fact exist.
I will answer all questions in real time, and I will answer them in audio/video form, as I 
did with this question.
And please visit me at my website www.freudinstitute.com where you can pose 
questions and actually chat in real time about matters OCD-related matters with 
clinicians and scholars.
 
Why Psychopaths Overachieve


I was going for my nightly walk and, as is my wont, thinking about the various ventures 
that I am trying to get off the ground. And, as is also my want, I was plagued with 
doubts—with self-doubts. And it then occurred to me why psychopaths are so 
successful: they are not neurotic. Self-doubt is inherently neurotic. It is one thing to 
know one’s own limitations: that is not self- doubt; that is just plain self-knowledge. But 
self-doubt is not about self-knowledge or even
quasi-self-knowledge; it is moralistic self-undermining; it is neurotic self-punishment. 
And because psychopaths don’t have consciences, they don’t doubt themselves and, 
more generally, they are not neurotic. Neurosis is about a conflict between one’s primal 
desires and one’s conscience. So psychopaths, having as they do no conscience, are 
not neurotic; and they are therefore not held by neurosis or, consequently, by neurotic 
self-doubt or, consequently, by any self-doubt. Non-psychopaths, on the other hand, 
even exceptionally gifted ones—nay, especially exceptionally gifted ones—are riddled 
with neurosis and therefore with self-doubt and are therefore prone to self-sabotage. 
The ideal condition is to be a non-neurotic, non-psychopath; and that condition is 
achievable, and it is my objective as an analyst to get people attain that condition.
 
The Therapeutic Significance of a Healthy Sex Life



The purpose of psychoanalysis and indeed of all forms of psychotherapy is to get the 
patient to live in the real world. Freud himself knew this: he explicitly said that the sole 
functions of psychoanalysis were to get the patient to be able to work and to love.
At the same time, Freud said, or certainly seemed at certain junctures to say, that 
mental health is all about sex—that mental illness is all about not having a good sex life 
and that mental well-being is all about having a good one.
Which, if indeed he did say this, is seriously overstated, not positively erroneous.
But there is a way of interpreting this supposed conceit of Freud’s that gives it 
legitimacy.
Let’s start with the premise that sanity is about living in the real world and insanity is 
about living in one’s imagination.
To the extent that people are not actually having sex with other human beings, their sex 
lives are masturbatory and fantasy-based, and are therefore dissociated from reality 
and, quite literally, insane. And to the extent that people are having sex---provided that it is emotionally engaged sex, as opposed to just masturbation that happens to another 
person’s body---one’s sex life is reality-based, non- dissociated and, therefore, quite 
literally sane.
Is there more to having a non-dissociated and sane existence than having a non- 
dissociated sex life? Yes. Is there more to having a dissociated and insane existence 
than having a dissociated sex life? Yes. But one’s life is non-dissociated to the extent 
that one’s sex life is non-dissociated and dissociated others. Moreover, as Freud said, 
however overstatedly, one’s sex life is to some extent a prototype for the rest of one’s 
life, as in, there is at least some tendency for the non-sexual component of one’s 
existence to be non-dissociated if the sexual component of one’s existence is non-
dissociated and dissociated otherwise.
To sum up: Sanity is about living in the real world. Masturbation is about not living in 
the real world. So if one’s sex life is masturbation-based, one is to that extent not sane, 
even if one is otherwise sane. And if one’s sex-life is masturbation-based, it is unlikely 
that one is otherwise completely sane.
 
Autism as Pseudo-self-containment

Autistic people seem to live in their own world, and the obvious thing to say about them,
 therefore, is that they are self-contained. But they are not self-contained. They are 
pseudo-self- contained. Autism is about needing people early in life and being given 
trauma instead. And the traumatized child reacts by withdrawing into himself. But his 
withdrawal is not about being emotionally self-sufficient. It is about quite the opposite. It 
is about being so dependent on others that unless one is given just the right cocktail of 
emotional support from others, one hysterically flees into psychotic seclusion. Consider 
the jilted bride who, instead of going back on the dating scene, continues to wear her 
wedding dress while pretending that the marriage actually happened. This person is 
psychotically secluded and therefore isolated from reality—but not
self-contained. Quite the opposite. And the same is true of the autistic.
Being genuinely self-contained is about balance. It is about being given—or having 
been given—the right level, and the right kind of emotional sustenance from others and,
 on that basis, being able, like a dolphin that has taken in enough air or a camel that 
has taken in enough water, to go out on one’s own. This is not what the autistic person 
is doing. Autism is a way of being damaged. True independence is about the opposite 
of being damaged. And a sign of true independence is flexibility, and rigidity is a sign of 
damage-based pseudo-independence.
One last point: Bureaucrats are people who are damaged and have no free will.
Bureaucrats need others to tell them what to do. And when they come across someone 
who is genuinely independently, they invidiously describe him as having ‘autism’ or 
‘Asperger’s’, sometimes going so far as to get psychiatrists to formally diagnose them 
in this way. But such characterizations are, as we have just seen, the opposite of the 
truth---and, like many invidious characterizations, not just false but also projective.
 
The Two Ways in which Intellectual Activity Promotes Intelligence


There are two distinct ways in which reading increases intelligence. On the one hand, 
reading makes one aware of truths of which one was previously unaware, and each 
such truth is a new weapon in the arsenal of one’s intellect. On the other hand, reading stimulates thought, and its effect on one’s intellect is similar in this respect to the effect 
that physical exercise has on one’s body. When one exercises, a message is sent to 
one’s metabolism that it must build
muscle-tissue and make all of the other changes to itself that are required for an 
exercise- intensive existence. And when one reads or otherwise engages one’s intellect,
 a message is sent to one’s central nervous system that it must forge new neural 
pathways and make all of the other changes to itself that are require for a thought-
intensive existence. So, reading is not simply about uploading new information. It is 
also about keeping one’s central nervous system constantly stimulated, so that it 
continues to keep itself combat-ready, so to speak, and able to take on new tasks and 
develop new capabilities. And just as one loses stamina, muscle-power and 
coordination if one does not engage in athletic training, so one loses neural plasticity 
and intellect if one does not engage in cognitive training. By reading and otherwise 
engaging one’s intellect, one keeps one’s mind nimble and elastic. Indeed, it is more by 
keeping one’s mind supple and active than it is by making one aware of new facts that 
reading promotes intelligence, and the same is true of other forms of intellectual activity.
 The primary intellectual benefit of vigorous intellectual activity is not that it stocks one’s 
mind with new ideas, so much as it is that it keeps one’s mind in a state of constant 
readiness and consequent responsiveness. And this heightened responsiveness, in its 
turn, makes one’s mind more able to acquire new information.
 
How Mental Illness Differs from Physical Illness

Somebody on Quora dot com asked the following question. I quote:
Do you believe mental illness is “an illness like any other” or is it a distinct and unique 
phenomenon? Why?
Here is my answer:
Mental illness is not an ‘illness like any other.’ Mental illnesses are contextual in nature.
In some contexts, being obsessive-compulsive is a liability and thus an illness, and in 
other contexts, being obsessive-compulsive is an asset and thus not a mental illness. 
And the same is true, to varying degrees, of all other mental illnesses. By contrast, 
physical illnesses are not contextual in nature: if somebody has asthma or lupus or 
cancer, that is not asset in some contexts and a liability in others: it is simply a liability. 
Also, mental illnesses serve a defensive function. Even though, in at least some 
contexts, OCD is a liability, it serves a protective function: it insulates the subject in a 
psychological ‘safe-space’: his obsessions and compulsions encircle that safe-space 
and, although dysfunctional in some respects, provide him some measure of safety 
from a toxic and threatening environment. And the same is true of psychosis: the 
psychotic did not become psychotic for no reason. He was in a situation in which he 
was being threatened and pumped full of negative validations and detriments to his 
sense of self-worth.
And he therefore decided to distance himself from that destructive negativity by 
withdrawing into a world of psychotic delusion. So even though psychosis is an illness 
and is some respects gravely dysfunctional, it does serve at least some protective 
function. Also, to develop a point hinted at earlier, some mental illnesses involve 
misdeployed strengths of intellect and character. OCD involves misdeployed 
intelligence and focus; schizophrenia involves misdeployed creativity and emotional 
sensitivity. So mental illnesses, though in many respects gravely debilitating, 
sometimes seem to involve heightened, albeit misdeployed, abilities. 
Psychiatric Munchausen’s by Proxy

Parents are getting psychiatrists to falsely diagnose their children as having autism, 
Asperger’s, ADHD, and the like. Such diagnoses—especially false diagnoses of 
Aspersers and Autism—are nothing more than cloaked child murder. The parent---
usually the mother---wants her husband’s attention; can’t get it; and then, in an effort to 
get it—or to get some surrogate thereof—figuratively murders the child with a false 
psychiatric diagnosis, which dooms the child in question to the over-attentions of idiot 
educationalists, psychologists, psychiatrists, and bureaucrats---the effect of which is to 
damage the child in some way or other, sometimes going so far as damage him in a 
way that retroactively validates the initial diagnosis. Of course, with rare exceptions, 
psychiatrists and educational psychologists are none too bright and are all too willing to 
foist bunk diagnoses on hapless youths, both because they need money and also 
because they need to feel that their line of goods has some legitimate application, 
which it doesn’t. But rule of thumb: when a child is sent to a psychiatrist, the first place 
to look for psychological maladies is in the Munchausen-by-proxy mother’s head, not 
the child’s. The child’s mind is the place to look for fall out from the blast, not for the 
blast’s mom-centric epicenter.
 
What is the Prognosis for People with OCD?

Somebody on Quora.com asked the following question:
What is the prognosis for patients with Obsessive–compulsive disorder (OCD)? Here is 
my answer:
People who have OCD have it because it provides them with relief from anxiety—from 
anxiety so intense as to be almost inconceivable to people who have not had OCD. But 
OCD is just a defense-mechanism. And as is the case with most defense-mechanisms, 
there comes a time in the subject’s life when it starts to do more harm than good. At 
that point, the obsessive- compulsive has to make a decision: What do I want? Do I 
want mere freedom from anxiety? Or do I want to live and grow? It is 100% up to him 
what he chooses. If he chooses to grow, he will, and his OCD, without exactly 
vanishing, will assume productive forms. If he chooses not to grow, he won’t.
OCD, though an affliction, is ultimately a voluntary one. If the obsessive-compulsive 
chooses to remain in his defensive bubble of isolation from the world, then his OCD will 
defeat him. If he chooses to re-engage the world, then his OCD will assume non-
pathological and in fact productive forms, as it did with Beethoven and Einstein.
OCD, though an affliction, is an affliction of the will—and is thus unlike cancer or 
asthma, which have nothing to do with the integrity of one’s will. And OCD is eliminated,
 or at least made to assume adaptive forms by duly exercising one’s will in growth-
oriented ways.
The symptoms of OCD are involuntary only in a relative sense. Relative to a 
determination to remain within his defensive bubble of non-engagement, there is 
nothing much that the obsessive-compulsive can do about those symptoms. He can 
engage in a bit of ‘cognitive-behavioral therapy’, but the effects of such therapy will be 
shallow and short-lived, absent a determination on the obsessive-compulsive’s part to 
grow and change himself in a way that makes his OCD irrelevant. On the other, if he 
does choose to leave his defensive bubble and
re-engage the world, then, indeed, his OCD will become irrelevant. So relative to a determination on his part to re-engage the world, the symptoms of OCD are voluntary.
So ultimately OCD is indeed voluntary, and if one sees it for the voluntary condition that 
it ultimate is, then the prognosis is 100% positive. But if one sees it as a garden-variety 
pathology, then the prognosis is negative. A garden-variety condition is one that does 
not afflict one’s will and that falls outside the scope of the one’s will. OCD is an affliction 
of one’s will; it is a weakening of one’s will, in consequence of a withdrawal from the 
world. And by choosing to go back out into the world, the obsessive-compulsive 
strengthens his will and causes one’s OCD to assume benign if not productive forms.
 
How is Rationalization Different from Auto-hypnosis?
Auto-hypnosis can be an enormously powerful tool. First of all, to auto-hypnotize is not 
to put oneself in a trance or daze. It is simply to give oneself a command to think or feel 
or act in a certain way, the efficacy of which command does not depend on the 
outcome of an act of conscious judgment on one’s part. Usually, such commands are 
issued as well as received unconsciously.
An element of auto-hypnosis is involved in any case where one has a feeling about 
such and such that extends beyond what the use of one’s critical faculties would 
warrant. Thus, there is an element of auto-hypnosis in any case where one has the 
requisite degree of excitement about the plan in question to succeed in carrying it out. 
Somebody who only followed through on plans that he been validated by his critical 
faculty would be somebody who never did anything: he would be the ultimate 
bureaucrat. So an element of auto-hypnosis is involved in doing anything that requires 
sustained enthusiasm. And auto-hypnosis is therefore not only not a bad thing but a 
positively essential thing, as in, it is essential for one’s not being the psychological 
equivalent of a quadriplegic.
Be it noted that auto-hypnosis can also be a bad thing: whenever one stews or 
obsesses or ruminates or wallows, one is negatively hypnotizing oneself. When 
autohypnosis has an affirmative character, it encourages action and when it has a 
negative character it prevents action. So given how much of one’s time one wastes and 
how much action one one’s part one prevents by negatively auto-hypnotizing, it is clear 
that auto-hypnosis can indeed be a very bad thing.
But auto-hypnosis is not inherently bad: it is rather that it can be put to bad use. And 
even when it is bad, its negative effects are surprisingly easily reversed. Once one 
knows that in obsessing or wallowing, one is really just negatively hypnotizing oneself, 
it is surprisingly easy to flip a mental switch and replace that negative auto-hypnosis 
with affirmative auto-hypnosis.
But rationalization is inherently bad.
But how exactly does auto-hypnosis different from rationalization? Isn’t auto-hypnosis 
the act of making oneself believe something that one knows not to be true or that one 
at least regards as doubtful, and isn’t auto-hypnosis the same thing?
No. Auto-hypnosis is about generating an attitude of some kind without making one’s 
acceptance of the legitimacy of that attitude depend on some edict issued by one’s 
faculty of judgment. The whole point of hypnosis, and of auto-hypnosis, is to create 
conviction without deploying or therefore misdeploying one’s critical faculties. Auto-
hypnosis is about drawing enthusiasm from within oneself, as opposed to from a 
warped understanding of reality.
Rationalization on the other hand is about generating emotional conviction from a 
warped understanding of reality, rather than from within the depths of one’s soul.
So auto-hypnosis doesn’t involve a misdeployment of one’s faculty of judgment and it therefore doesn’t result in a perversion of those faculties. By contrast, rationalization 
does involve such a misdeployment and therefore does result in such a perversion.FREUD POSITION STATEMENT ADDITIONAL IMPORTANT PAPERSFREUD POSITION STATEMENT COLLECTED INTRODUCTORY WORKS 

POSITION STATEMENT FREUD COLLECTED INTRODUCTORY WORKS

Sources: Five Lectures on Psycho-Analysis (1910), Introductory Lectures on Psycho-
Analysis (1916-17), New Introductory Lectures on Psycho-Analysis (1933)

---

**FIVE LECTURES ON PSYCHO-ANALYSIS (1910)**

1. Hysterical symptoms have psychological meaning and can be traced to specific 
traumatic experiences.
2. The method of free association is superior to hypnosis for accessing unconscious 
material.
3. Dream interpretation is the royal road to knowledge of the unconscious.
4. Dreams possess latent content hidden behind manifest content through processes of 
condensation and displacement.
5. Repression is a defense mechanism whereby incompatible ideas are forced out of 
consciousness.
6. Resistance encountered during analysis is the same force that originally produced 
repression.
7. Symptoms are substitute formations representing repressed wishes in distorted form.
8. Infantile sexuality exists and develops through distinct stages (oral, anal, phallic).
9. The Oedipus complex is a universal phenomenon in which children take parents as 
their first love objects.
10. Neurosis originates from conflicts between sexual impulses and the forces that 
repress them.
11. Transference is the phenomenon whereby patients unconsciously transfer feelings 
from past relationships onto the analyst.
12. Childhood experiences, particularly sexual ones, determine susceptibility to later 
neurosis.
13. The unconscious operates according to its own laws distinct from conscious 
thought.
14. Psychoanalytic treatment works by making the unconscious conscious.
15. The therapeutic relationship recreates early conflictual relationships, enabling their 
resolution.

**INTRODUCTORY LECTURES ON PSYCHO-ANALYSIS (1916-17)**

16. Parapraxes (slips of tongue, forgetting, bungled actions) have meaning and reveal 
unconscious intentions.
17. Apparently accidental mental phenomena are psychically determined.
18. Parapraxes arise from the interference of two different intentions, one conscious 
and one unconscious.
19. Dreams are the fulfillment of repressed wishes, typically infantile in origin.
20. Dream-work transforms latent thoughts into manifest content through condensation, 
displacement, and secondary revision.
21. Symbols in dreams have relatively constant meanings, particularly regarding sexual content.
22. The censorship function operates during sleep to disguise forbidden wish-
fulfillments.
23. Anxiety dreams and punishment dreams do not contradict wish-fulfillment theory 
but represent wishes of the superego or self-punishment.
24. Neurotic symptoms, like dreams, are compromise formations between conflicting 
psychical forces.
25. Fixation at early libidinal stages predisposes individuals to specific forms of 
neurosis.
26. Regression to earlier developmental stages occurs when later development is 
blocked.
27. The libido can become attached to objects or can be withdrawn onto the ego 
(narcissism).
28. Actual neuroses differ from psychoneuroses in having a contemporary somatic 
etiology rather than psychological conflict.
29. Obsessional neurosis involves regression to the anal-sadistic stage and 
ambivalence toward objects.
30. Hysteria involves conversion of psychical conflict into somatic symptoms.
31. The transference neurosis that develops in analysis provides access to the patient's 
pathogenic conflicts.
32. Resistance takes multiple forms: repression resistance, transference resistance, 
secondary gain resistance, id resistance, and superego resistance.
33. Working through resistances is essential to therapeutic success.
34. The analyst must maintain neutrality and use the patient's transference as a 
therapeutic instrument.

**NEW INTRODUCTORY LECTURES ON PSYCHO-ANALYSIS (1933)**

35. The psyche is divided into three agencies: id, ego, and superego.
36. The id is the reservoir of instinctual drives, operates according to the pleasure 
principle, and is entirely unconscious.
37. The ego is the portion of the id modified by contact with external reality and 
mediates between id, superego, and external world.
38. The superego is formed through internalization of parental authority and the 
dissolution of the Oedipus complex.
39. The superego functions as conscience and ego ideal, enforcing moral restrictions 
and striving toward perfection.
40. The ego serves three tyrannical masters: the id, superego, and external reality.
41. Anxiety is a signal function of the ego warning of danger situations.
42. Different developmental stages correspond to different danger situations: loss of 
object, loss of love, castration anxiety, superego anxiety.
43. The death instinct (Thanatos) operates alongside the life instinct (Eros) and 
manifests as aggression and self-destruction.
44. Instincts can fuse or defuse, with aggression becoming more dangerous when 
separated from erotic binding.
45. Female psychosexual development differs from male development, with the girl 
having to change both erogenous zone and love object.
46. Penis envy plays a central role in female development, leading to the castration 
complex and its consequences.47. The superego in women develops differently and may be less impersonal than in 
men.
48. Telepathy and thought-transference, while requiring scientific caution, cannot be 
dismissed a priori.
49. Psychoanalysis is a natural science and maintains a scientific worldview 
(Weltanschauung).
50. Religion is an illusion based on wish-fulfillment and the projection of the father 
complex onto the cosmos.
